% TAZ SECTION: O
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\chapter{O: ORDER}
\label{SECO}


\begin{table}[H]
  \centering
  \LARGE
	\begin{tabular}[t]{|p{0.2\textwidth}|p{0.5\textwidth}}
 
	\textbf{NAME} & \texttt{ORDER}\\
	\hline
	\textbf{Purpose} & \begin{enumerate}
	\item Sort SPACE-DELIMITED ITEMS
	\item Order, or rather, Smartly Sort things.
	\item Sort Numbers by Quantity.
	\item Sort Words Lexically (by Alphabetical Order)
	\item Sort Sequences by Lexical Order of Characters
	\end{enumerate}\\
	\hline
	              
\end{tabular}
\caption{General Objectives of TEA Primitive \textbf{O:}}
  \label{TABTAZO}
\end{table}


\section{SEMANTICS of O:}
\label{SECSEMO}


\begin{table}[H]
\centering
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3} % Optional: increases row height
\rowcolors{1}{lightgray}{white}   % Alternating row colors
\begin{tabular}{>{\bfseries}m{0.4\linewidth} | m{0.6\linewidth}} % 2 columns, 
\rowcolor{white}
\textbf{\makecell[l]{INSTRUCTION\\ SIGNATURE}} & \textbf{INSTRUCTION FUNCTION} \\
\hline

o: & Return the AI sorted in ascending numerical order by words it contains if AI consists of words made of only valid number expressions, otherwise sort lexically in ascending order.\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{o:VALUE} & \makecell[l]{Same as o:, but using VALUE instead of AI}\\

o!: & Return the AI sorted alphabetically by characters it contains.\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{o!:VALUE} & Same as o!:, but using VALUE instead of AI\\


\makecell[l]{o*:vNAME\\o*!:vNAME} & Same as o:VALUE and o!:VALUE respectively, but using  the string stored in the vault with the name vNAME  as the VALUE\\

 
 \hline
\end{tabular}
\caption{The Semantics of O:}
\label{TABSEMO}
\end{table}


\section{NOTES about O:}
\label{SECNOTEO}


Being able to order things at will is such a formidable power, it can’t be underestimated that it is built into the TEA language as a primitive. Ordering makes design possible, prevents or limits chaos and randomness, and allows structure to be created or imposed on things. For TEA, the O: command space offers several utilities for performing ordering operations on strings directly in the program, user-provided or those stored in memory.

\vspace{2em}

A basic illustration of this power can be demonstrated by the following example TEA program that returns the alphabetically sorted initials of a person's name: 


\vspace{2em}



  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=O: EXAMPLE 1: Compute Sorted Initials of A Name, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
# following URL is hypothetical, but expected to 
# return the full, undecorated name of someone
W!: https://mit.edu/vc/name.txt 
f:ERROR:lEXPLICIT:lPROCESS
l:lEXPLICIT
#alternatively..
i!:{Terence L. Epstein Von Zalta}
l:lPROCESS
D!:^.: [a-zA-Z]
# (="T L E V Z")
G:
# (="TLEVZ")
O!:
# (="ELTVZ")
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}



\vspace{2em}

Another example:

\vspace{2em}


  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=O: EXAMPLE 2: Sort a list of words, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:{mice ice best acts zap} #specify the list of words, delimited by space
o: #invoke smart-lexical sorting on them
#should sort input by lexical order of words and then return...
#=acts best ice mice zap
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}


\vspace{2em}

However, if we instead use \texttt{O!:} in the above example, we instead produce something unlike a list of sorted words as can be seen in the example below...


\vspace{2em}


  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=O: EXAMPLE 3: Sort a list of words by character, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:{mice ice best acts zap}|o!:
#should sort input by lexical order of letters, and thus return:
#=    aabccceeeiimpssttz
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}


\vspace{2em}


The output thus produced ``    aabccceeeiimpssttz" shows that in this mode, characters have their natural order based on alphabetical position, however, also non-letter symbols such as space, punctuation and numbers are given special treatment as we might tell in the next example:


\vspace{2em}


  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=O: EXAMPLE 4: Sort a list of words by character, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:{1 Mice, 2 Ice, 3 Best, 4 Acts & 2 Zap!}|o!:
#sorts by lexical order of characters and returns:
#=          !&,,,12234ABIMZaccceeeipsstt
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}


\vspace{2em}


Thus we see that white space has higher precedence, followed by punctuation marks, then digits, then upper-case letters and finally lower-case letters.


\vspace{2em}


However, \textbf{ORDER} is not limited only lexical sorting, as we can tell in the next example involving \textbf{space delimited number expressions}:

\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=O: EXAMPLE 5: Quantitatively Sort a list of number expressions, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:{208919 919 1 211993   0 1 2 1e3 3 -10  5 19 .20 -1 -5e-3}|o:
#sorts by quantitative order of words and returns:
#=-10 -1 -5e-3 0 .20 1 1 2 3 5 19 919 1e3 208919 211993
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}


\vspace{1em}

In this case, among the most important things to note is that the expressions need to be legitimate number expressions --- this can be any expression that conforms to one of the following categories:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{Whole Numbers}: such as 1, 2, 301 or 100
\item \textbf{Fractional Numbers}: such as 0.1, 0.0, 999.99901 or even just .005
\item \textbf{Exponential Form Numbers}: such as 1e2 ($1 \times 10^2$), 2.501e6 ($2.501 \times 10^6$), 5e-3 ($5 \times \frac{1}{10^3}$), etc.
\item \textbf{Signed Numbers}: such as -1, +4, -0.0002, or even -5e-3
\end{enumerate}

\vspace{1em}

Further, for this numeric or quantitative sorting to take effect, \textbf{there must not be any other delimiters between the number expressions except just white space}. Thus, the following slightly modified version would produce an entirely different result, simply because the system detects there to be some non-numerical expressions in the input, and thus, decides to use lexical sorting instead:


\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=O: EXAMPLE 6: Invalid Number Expressions result in Lexical Sorting, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:{208919 919 1 211993  ,   0 1 2 1e3 3 -10 5 19 .20 -1 -5e-3}|o:
#sorts by quantitative order of words and returns:
#=, -1 -10 -5e-3 .20 0 1 1 19 1e3 2 208919 211993 3 5 919
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}


\vspace{2em}

Whereas, in case we take that last example and instead apply the \textbf{character-sorting} command \texttt{o!:}, we instead end up with:


\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=O: EXAMPLE 7: Character-level Lexical Sorting of Number Expressions?, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:{208919 919 1 211993  ,   0 1 2 1e3 3 -10 5 19 .20 -1 -5e-3}|o:
#sorts by quantitative order of words and returns:
#=                  ,----.00001111111111222233335589999999ee
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}


\vspace{1em}


That said, note that as with other TEA commands that allow \textbf{vault-mode}, the \texttt{o*:} and \texttt{o*!:} work as expected as shown in the following combined basic examples:


\vspace{1em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=O: EXAMPLE 8: All Kinds of Sorting for Data in Vaults, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
v:vNUMBERS:{1 414 2.0 135 6 23 7 -3.1}
v:vLETTERS:{a Dad Be ME for BC Got Cool Aid!}
o*:vNUMBERS | v:vNUM_SORTED
o*:vLETTERS | v:vLET_SORTED
o*!:vNUMBERS | v:vNUM_SORTED_X
o*!:vLETTERS | v:vLET_SORTED_X

#present results...
i!:{For the Numbers:
} | x*!:vNUMBERS | x!:{
SORTED Quantitatively:
}| x*!:vNUM_SORTED | x!:{
And Sorted Lexically By Character:
} | x*!:vNUM_SORTED_X

#also for the letters...

x!:{
-------------------
And for the Words:
}| x*!:vLETTERS | x!:{
SORTED Lexically By Word:
}| x*!:vLET_SORTED | x!:{
And Sorted Lexically By Character:
} | x*!:vLET_SORTED_X
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}


\vspace{2em}


Which shall produce the enlightening output...


  \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Output from Example 8]
For the Numbers:
1 414 2.0 135 6 23 7 -3.1
SORTED Quantitatively:
-3.1 1 2.0 6 7 23 135 414
And Sorted Lexically By Character:
       -..011112233344567
-------------------
And for the Words:
a Dad Be ME for BC Got Cool Aid!
SORTED Lexically By Word:
Aid! BC Be Cool Dad Got ME a for
And Sorted Lexically By Character:
        !ABBCCDEGMaaddefiloooort
  \end{tcbverbatim}


\vspace{2em}

As a final note concerning how TEA's \textbf{ORDER} works, it is important to keep in mind the first item listed in the table of purposes --- \textbf{\hyperref[TABTAZO]{Table \ref{TABTAZO}}} --- in particular, that \textbf{sorting is applied to SPACE-DELIMITED items}. Thus, when one would attempt to sort a list of numbers in ascending order (the natural case with this command), and they run the following code:


\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=O: EXAMPLE 9: Sorting Numbers BUT with INVALID FORMATTING, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:{2, 24, 4, 1, 00, -1, 1e3}
o:
#result: -1, 00, 1, 1e3 2, 24, 4,
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}


\vspace{1em}



They shall get the somewhat misleading result ``-1, 00, 1, 1e3 2, 24, 4," --- which, for \textbf{lexical ordering} is correct, but is wrong for \textit{quantitative ordering}. Thus, to fix this problem, the code needs to ensure that the list items are space-delimited before applying the \textbf{ORDER} instruction. Thus, the following would do the right thing:

\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=O: EXAMPLE 10: Sorting Numbers BUT with VALID FORMATTING, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:{2, 24, 4, 1, 00, -1, 1e3}
r!:,:{ } #enforce space delimiters
o:
#result: -1 00 1 2 4 24 1e3
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}


\vspace{1em}



\section{EXAMPLE APPLICATIONS of O:}
\label{SECEXAMPO}

  
There is not much to say concerning the applications of \textbf{ORDER} outside of the various scenarios we have encountered in the notes above. Especially when we realize that this operation mostly comes up in situations where either analysis or meaningful presentation of information necessitates the re-formatting of data based on some ordering criteria inherent in the data or some standard criteria such as lexical or quantitative ordering.

\vspace{2em}

Thus, the example application we are going to look at, only offers this idea a somewhat more general treatment, by allowing us to have an application that can do some basic ranking and sorting given arbitrary numerical data in a few standard forms.



\subsection{EXAMPLE 1: \textbf{STATISTICAL ANALYSIS:} The RANKING UTILITY (RU)}
\label{SECEXAMP1O}


This application, which we shall call \textbf{RU} --- the \textbf{Ranking Utility}, is meant to help a user take some sequence of numeric data, perhaps labeled or annotated, and then sort it in descending order of the numeric quantity of the items it contains. The application offers 3 use-cases:

\vspace{2em}

\begin{enumerate}
\item Sorting a list of numbers expressions in descending order.
\item Sorting a list of labeled number expressions (in the format \texttt{LABEL1:N1, LABEL\_2:N2, ..., LABEL:N,... etc.} ) in descending order of their associated numbers, but ensuring that the labels are left intact in the final sorted list.
\item Sorting a list of labeled numeric expressions as above, but also further annotating these sorted items with their corresponding positions in the final sorted list so that we have a final list formatted as \texttt{1st=LABELMAX:NMAX, 2nd=LABEL:N2, ... etc. nth=LABELN:NN,...}
\end{enumerate}

\vspace{2em}

Such a generic program might come in handy in situations where one has some data collected indicating some classes or categories and their corresponding \textbf{overall} scores --- we say overall, because in the current version, the program doesn't try to aggregate disparate tallies or scores for the same label, and thus, one would want to ensure that where summing is required, they further improve this program or perhaps first tally the counts for each duplicated label.


\vspace{2em}

Otherwise, the program as of \textbf{version 3} is as such...


\vspace{2em}


%\begin{figure}[H]
{
  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=TEA Program: Ranking Utility v.3, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
######################################
# RU: Ranking Utility v3
######################################
# This tool is meant to help
# organize well formatted lists of data
# by user-specified criteria.
#######################################

#if no externally provided data, use defaults...
#i:{5,3.2121,1,-5,5,2,5,00,-1.0,2e-5} #for un-labeled tasks
i:{E:45, A:3.2121 LABEL_1:1,C:-5, 88:5,  TEST_ty:2, A:5}

v:vDATA | v:vRAW_DATA #store externally provided data

#----| CONSTANTS |----
v:vDELIM:{
----------***----------
}
v:vWELCOME:{----------***----------
RU: RANKING UTILITY v3
----------***----------
Welcome to RU! 
This program helps you sort and rank numeric data by some criteria}
v:vMENU:{----------***----------
RU: TASK MENU
----------***----------
Pick a task by specifying its index:
[1] SORT RAW NUMERIC DATA: quantitatively in descending order
[2] SORT LABELLED NUMERIC DATA
[3] POSITIONIFY LABELLED NUMERIC DATA
[e] ENTER NEW NUMERIC DATA
[q] QUIT}
v:vPROMPT_DATA_1:{----------***----------
RU: Get RAW DATA
----------***----------
INSTRUCTIONS: Enter or paste one or more lines of raw numeric data delimited by space or commas, such as:
D1 D2 D3 D4... DN
R1,R2,R3,...,RN,etc.}
v:vPROMPT_DATA_2:{----------***----------
RU: Get LABELLED DATA
----------***----------
INSTRUCTIONS: Enter or paste one or more lines of labelled numeric data delimited by space or commas, such as:
LABEL1:D1 LABEL2:D2 LABEL3:D3 LABEL4:D4... LABELN:DN
LABEL2:R1,LABEL1:R2,LABELK:R3,...,LABELN:RN,etc.
----------***----------
NOTE: The label_s should not have spaces in them, but otherwise can be duplicated across the dataset.}
v:vPROMPT_INVALID_DATA:{----------***----------
RU: Got INVALID DATA
----------***----------
ERROR: Note that the data you entered does not conform to the accepted format for the chosen analysis and ranking criteria. 
Cross-check and re-try!}

#----| VARIABLES |----
v:vRESULTS:{RESULT: No Processing Done}

#----| WELCOME |----
y:vWELCOME | i*:
l:lPROMPT_MENU
#What to do?
y:vMENU | i*:
v:vMENU_OPT #store user selected task index
f:^[qQ]$:lQUIT #finish, do nothing else...
f:^[eE]$:lPROMPT_DATA #get new data...
f!:[123]:lPROMPT_MENU #if not correct, re-prompt

#store last data-processing option
v:vMENU_OPT_DATA 

#----| WHICH DATA? |----
#check if we have data, otherwise prompt for data
y:vDATA | t.: | f:^$:lPROMPT_DATA:lVERIFY_DATA

l:lPROMPT_DATA
y:vMENU_OPT # retrieve user option

f:[eE]:lFIX_OPT:lOPT_FINE #if not meaningful, use-last choice
l:lFIX_OPT
y:vMENU_OPT_DATA | v:vMENU_OPT

l:lOPT_FINE 
f:1:lPROMPT_DATA_CASE1 | f:2:lPROMPT_DATA_CASE2 | f:3:lPROMPT_DATA_CASE2
#------
l:lPROMPT_DATA_CASE1 # get raw data..
y:vPROMPT_DATA_1 | i*: | v:vDATA | v:vRAW_DATA
j:lVERIFY_DATA #then go to verification
#------
l:lPROMPT_DATA_CASE2 # get labelled data..
y:vPROMPT_DATA_2 | i*: | v:vDATA | v:vRAW_DATA
j:lVERIFY_DATA #then go to verification

#----| VERIFY DATA |----
l:lVERIFY_DATA
y:vDATA #retrieve data
#pre-process data..
t.: #trim trailing white-space

#special pre-process esp for TDT generated datasets
g.:, #enforce one liner with comma delimiters
d.:^\s+\[ | d.:\]$ #eliminate [] enclosures 
d:^[\s,]+:[\s,]+$ #trim trailing commas N space

v:vDATA
#check if we have any data, otherwise [re]prompt for data
y:vDATA | f:^$:lPROMPT_DATA

#pre-process based on case...
y:vMENU_OPT # retrieve user option
f:1:lVERIFY_DATA_CASE1 | f:2:lVERIFY_DATA_CASE2 | f:3:lVERIFY_DATA_CASE2
#------
l:lVERIFY_DATA_CASE1 # validate raw data..
y:vDATA | g.:, #replace new-lines with comma
r!:{ }:{,} | r!:[,]+:, #fix commas
#verify formatting...
f!:{^(([+-]?((\d+(\.\d*)?)|(\.\d+))([eE][+-]?\d+)?),?)+$}:lFLAG_INVALID_DATA
v:vDATA #override data with updated version
j:lPROCESS_DATA #then go to process
#------
l:lVERIFY_DATA_CASE2 # validate labelled data..
y:vDATA | g.:, #replace new-lines with comma
r!:[ ]+:{,} | r!:[,]+:, #fix commas
#verify formatting...
f!:{^((\w+:)([+-]?((\d+(\.\d*)?)|(\.\d+))([eE][+-]?\d+)?),?)+$}:lFLAG_INVALID_DATA
v:vDATA #override data with updated version
j:lPROCESS_DATA #then go to process

#----| IN-CASE INVALID DATA |----
l:lFLAG_INVALID_DATA
y:vPROMPT_INVALID_DATA | x*!:vDELIM | x*!:vRAW_DATA | i*:
j:lPROMPT_DATA #re-prompt for data

#----| PROCESS DATA |----
l:lPROCESS_DATA 
#process based on case...
y:vMENU_OPT # retrieve user option
f:1:lPROCESS_DATA_CASE1 | f:2:lPROCESS_DATA_CASE2 | f:3:lPROCESS_DATA_CASE3
#------
l:lPROCESS_DATA_CASE1 # sort raw data..
#--|start algorithm|------------
v:vSORTED_LIST:{} #initially empty..
y:vDATA #fetch raw data
r!:,:{ } #enforce space delimiters
o: #sort in ascending order
m: #transform to descending order
r!:[ ]+:, #swap space with commas
r!:[,]+:, #fix extraneous commas
r!:[,]:{, } #space items
v:vSORTED_LIST #store sorted list
#--|end algorithm|------------
v:vRESULTS | x:{DESC-SORTED DATA: 
} | v:vRESULTS
#go present results...
j:lRESULTS

#------
l:lPROCESS_DATA_CASE2  # sort labelled data..
y:vDATA 
#--|start algorithm|------------
v:vLIST_ORIG #stash original dataset
v:vSORTED_LIST:{} #to hold sorted dataset

l:lPROCESS_NEXT_ITEM
y:vLIST_ORIG # let's process the original dataset then...
#eliminate labels
r!:{(\w+:)}:{ } #better approach
#swap commas for space
r!:,:{ } | r!:[ ]+:{ }
#sort in descending order
o: | m: 

#retain only largest number
t.: | d:[ ].*$ |v:vNMAX 
#obtain [the first] labelled item corresponding to this number in the original dataset
x:{\w+:} | x!:,? | v:vREGEX_MAX | y:vLIST_ORIG | d*!.:vREGEX_MAX | d:,.*$ | x!:{,} | v:vLABELLED_MAX 

#store that as the next item in the sorted labelled list
x*:vSORTED_LIST:vLABELLED_MAX | v:vSORTED_LIST
#take the original dataset and eliminate [only] this labelled item then
y:vLABELLED_MAX | x!:? | v:vREGEX_MAX | y:vLIST_ORIG | d*.:vREGEX_MAX
#clean out trailing commas
d:^,
v:vLIST_ORIG | update/override original dataset 
#if empty, return sorted list, otherwise iterate...
t.: | f:^$:lRETURN_SORTED:lPROCESS_NEXT_ITEM

l:lRETURN_SORTED
y:vSORTED_LIST 
#clean out trailing commas and space
d:[,\s]*$
r!:[,]:{, } #space items
v:vSORTED_LIST #override, we have final sorted labelled dataset
#--|end algorithm|------------
v:vRESULTS | x:{DESC-SORTED LABELLED-DATA: 
} | v:vRESULTS
#go present results...
j:lRESULTS


#------
l:lPROCESS_DATA_CASE3 # sort and positionify labelled data..
y:vDATA 
#--|start algorithm|------------
v:vLIST_ORIG #stash original dataset
v:vSORTED_LIST:{} #to hold sorted dataset
v:vPOSITION:{1} #to hold positions
v:vPOSDELIM:{=} #to separate position from item

l:l3PROCESS_NEXT_ITEM
y:vLIST_ORIG # let's process the original dataset then...
#eliminate labels
r!:{(\w+:)}:{ }
#swap commas for space
r!:,:{ } | r!:[ ]+:{ }
#sort in descending order
o: | m: 

#retain only largest number
t.: | d:[ ].*$ |v:vNMAX 
#obtain [the first] labelled item corresponding to this number in the original dataset
x:{\w+:} | x!:,? | v:vREGEX_MAX | y:vLIST_ORIG | d*!.:vREGEX_MAX | d:,.*$ | x!:{,} | v:vLABELLED_MAX 

#should we process this?
d:, | t.: | f:^$:lFINISHED_POSITIONING

#process its position
y:vPOSITION
f:1$:lST:lNST
l:lST
x!:{st} | v:vPOSLABEL
j:lADDPOSITION

l:lNST
y:vPOSITION
f:2$:lSND:lNSND
l:lSND
x!:{nd} | v:vPOSLABEL
j:lADDPOSITION

l:lNSND
y:vPOSITION
f:3$:lRD:lTH
l:lRD
x!:{rd} | v:vPOSLABEL
j:lADDPOSITION

l:lTH
y:vPOSITION
x!:{th} | v:vPOSLABEL
j:lADDPOSITION

l:lADDPOSITION
g*:{}:vPOSLABEL:vPOSDELIM:vLABELLED_MAX | v:vLABELLED_MAX_POS 
y:vPOSITION | x!:{+1} | r.: | v:vPOSITION

l:lFINISHED_POSITIONING

#store that as the next item in the sorted labelled list
x*:vSORTED_LIST:vLABELLED_MAX_POS | v:vSORTED_LIST

#take the original dataset and eliminate [only] this labelled item then
y:vLABELLED_MAX | x!:? | v:vREGEX_MAX | y:vLIST_ORIG | d*.:vREGEX_MAX
#clean out trailing commas
d:^,

v:vLIST_ORIG | update/override original dataset 
#if empty, return sorted list, otherwise iterate...
t.: | f:^$:l3RETURN_SORTED:l3PROCESS_NEXT_ITEM

l:l3RETURN_SORTED
y:vSORTED_LIST 
#clean out trailing commas and space
d:[,\s]*$
r!:[,]:{, } #space items
v:vSORTED_LIST #override, we have final sorted labelled dataset
#--|end algorithm|------------
v:vRESULTS | x:{DESC-SORTED and POSITIONIFIED LABELLED-DATA: 
} | v:vRESULTS
#go present results...
j:lRESULTS


#----| PRESENT RESULTS |----
l:lRESULTS
y:vRESULTS | i*:
j:lPROMPT_MENU #iterate!


l:lQUIT #quit program gracefully...

i!:{ORIGINAL DATA:
}  | x*!:vDATA | x*:vDELIM | x*!:vDELIM | x!: {RU Processing Results:} | x*!:vDELIM | x*!:vRESULTS
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
    \captionof{figure}{TEA APPLICATION: Ranking Utility (RU) v3}
  \label{FIGOEX2}
  \vspace{1cm}
  

\vspace{2em}

So, to appreciate this tool, we can for example assume we have the following small, but somewhat representative and quirky datatset conforming to the constraints we laid out in the specification:

  \vspace{2em}
  
       \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample Well-formatted INPUT Dataset of 7 Labeled Numerical Expressions]
E:5, A:3.2121 LABEL_1:1,C:-5, 88:5,  TEST_ty:2, A:5
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 

\vspace{2em}

And so, if we select the RU Function 2: sorting numerical labeled data in descending order, we would obtain results such as:

    
     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample Well-formatted OUTPUT from Task 2 of RU]
----------***----------
ORIGINAL DATA:
E:5,A:3.2121,LABEL_1:1,C:-5,88:5,TEST_ty:2,A:5
----------***----------
RU Processing Results:
----------***----------
DESC-SORTED LABELLED-DATA: 
E:5, 88:5, A:5, A:3.2121, TEST_ty:2, LABEL_1:1, C:-5
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 
 
 \vspace{2em}
 
 
 While, if we use Task 3: \textbf{sorting and positionifying} labeled numeric data, against the same dataset, we would obtain...
 
 \vspace{2em}
 
  
     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample Well-formatted OUTPUT from Task 3 of RU]
----------***----------
ORIGINAL DATA:
E:5,A:3.2121,LABEL_1:1,C:-5,88:5,TEST_ty:2,A:5
----------***----------
RU Processing Results:
----------***----------
DESC-SORTED and POSITIONIFIED LABELLED-DATA: 
1st=E:5, 2nd=88:5, 3rd=A:5, 4th=A:3.2121, 5th=TEST_ty:2, 6th=LABEL_1:1, 7th=C:-5
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 
 \vspace{2em}
 

And as for Task 1: merely sorting a list of numeric expressions in descending order, we would have something like:

\vspace{2em}
  
   \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample Well-formatted OUTPUT from Task 1 of RU]
----------***----------
ORIGINAL DATA:
5,3.2121,1,-5,5,2,5,00,-1.0,2e-5
----------***----------
RU Processing Results:
----------***----------
DESC-SORTED DATA: 
5, 5, 5, 3.2121, 2, 1, 2e-5, 00, -1.0, -5 
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 

\vspace{2em}


It should be worth noting that, as with all the other TEA application examples we have looked at thus far, this one too, works the same way anywhere TEA works, and also that, it has no non-TEA functions such as those relying on ZAP, and thus, should be expected to stay very portable and stable for as long as the TEA standard it is based on remains supported. 




%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
% TAZ SECTION: P
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\chapter{P: PERMUTATE}
\label{SECP}


\begin{table}[H]
  \centering
  \LARGE
	\begin{tabular}[t]{|p{0.2\textwidth}|p{0.5\textwidth}}
 
	\textbf{NAME} & \texttt{PERMUTATE}\\
	\hline
	\textbf{Purpose} & \begin{enumerate}
	\item Generate unique permutations of Strings
	\item Generate Random Strings
	\item Generate Strings of a Particular Structure and Alphabet
	\end{enumerate}\\
	\hline
	              
\end{tabular}
\caption{General Objectives of TEA Primitive \textbf{P:}}
  \label{TABTAZP}
\end{table}


\section{SEMANTICS of P:}
\label{SECSEMP}


\begin{table}[H]
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3} % Optional: increases row height
\rowcolors{1}{lightgray}{white}   % Alternating row colors
\begin{tabular}{>{\bfseries}m{0.4\linewidth} | m{0.6\linewidth}} % 2 columns, 
\rowcolor{white}
\textbf{\makecell[l]{INSTRUCTION\\ SIGNATURE}} & \textbf{INSTRUCTION FUNCTION} \\
\hline

p: & Return the AI expanded to utmost 100 unique
permutations of its elements (which essentially means returning anagrams of the AI), glued together.\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{p:VALUE\\p:VALUE:GLUE\\p:VALUE:GLUE:LIMIT\\p:VALUE:GLUE:LIMIT:LLIMIT} & Same as p: but using VALUE instead of AI. Also, if the second argument is also provided, it becomes the GLUE string used for joining the permutations generated when constructing the IO. The default or if GLUE isn't specified, is the SINGLE SPACE CHARACTER. The last argument is expected to be a number LIMIT, to limit how many permutations to return at most. If LLIMIT is provided, then exactly restrict the output to between LLIMIT as lower limit and LIMIT as upper. If LLIMIT = LIMIT, return exactly LIMIT permutations if they can be generated.\\

p!: & Return a random string of utmost 100 characters.\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{p!:SIZE\\p!:SIZE:GLUE\\p!:SIZE:GLUE:ALPHABET} & Return a random string of exactly SIZE characters.  GLUE serves to join the generated word-strings if provided, otherwise is the default (SINGLE SPACE CHAR). If the third parameter, the string ALPHABET, is provided, then use only the characters in the provided string for generating the random words, otherwise, will use the full Latin alphabet extended with the SINGLE SPACE CHAR.\\


\makecell[l]{p*:vVALUE\\p*:vVALUE:vGLUE\\p*:vVALUE:vGLUE:vLIMIT:vLLIMIT} & Permutates provided string. Same as  p:VALUE:GLUE:LIMIT , but using  the string parameters stored in the vaults with the specified names, vVALUE  as the VALUE, vGLUE as glue, vLIMIT as limit. Only the first, parameter is mandatory.\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{p*!:vSIZE\\p*!:vSIZE:vGLUE\\p*!:vSIZE:vGLUE:vALPHABET} & Same as  p!:SIZE:GLUE:ALPHABET  but operating on values stored in vaults.\\

 \hline
\end{tabular}
\caption{The Semantics of P:}
\label{TABSEMP}
\end{table}


\section{NOTES about P:}
\label{SECNOTEP}


A basic illustration of this P: power can be demonstrated by the following example TEA program that returns all possible unique combinations of the first 3 English alphabet letters: 

\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=P: EXAMPLE 1: Compute Permutations of "ABC", breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
I!:{abc} | v:vA | v:vGLUE:- | p*:vA:vGLUE 
# (="abc-acb-bac-bca-cab-cba")
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{2em}

Perhaps, it is important to stress that since mathematically it is known that a string of N characters has at most N! (N-factorial) possible permutations or rather anagrams, then, without enforcing a hard limit such as the default 100, it can become very expensive to run the p: command especially on large values. Thus, where necessary, ensure to specify the LIMIT parameter when invoking p: commands.

\vspace{2em}


\texttt{P:} maps a string to a set of its anagrams. This also means, one could do what the \texttt{P:} command does, with creative use of the other TEA fundamental anagram command ``a!:". This interesting fact is illustrated by the following application of TEA:

\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[myterminalstyle, title=Simulating P:ABC using AWK and A:]
  \begin{lstlisting}[breaklines=true]
EXPERIMENTS|< 13:35:35 $>* awk -e "BEGIN{do{n++; system(\"tttt -i abc -c a!:\");}while(n<=100)}" | sort | uniq
  \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{2em}

And that commandline AWK+TEA combo returns:

\vspace{2em}


\begin{verbatim}
abc
acb
bac
bca
cab
cba
\end{verbatim}

\vspace{2em}


It produces similar output an invocation of \texttt{p:} would have given for the same input ``abc", however, it merely leverages the minimalist TEA program \texttt{a!:} and some clever use of the Awk programming language.


\vspace{2em}


\textbf{NOTE:} The \texttt{P!:} command space is the only utility in TEA, with which one might elegantly generate random text. This is the equivalent of generating random numbers, for words. A utility many, if not most programming languages never provide a primitive solution for.

\vspace{2em}

The most basic \textbf{Random String Generator} (RSG) possible, is simply implemented using the following minimalist TEA program:


\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=P: EXAMPLE 2: Most Basic Random String Generator in TEA, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
P!:
# could return "fnudgzwhh ztnttwhehb iptkerl chwuljtiw"
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{2em}

Note that, as indicate in \textbf{\hyperref[TABSEMP]{Table \ref{TABSEMP}}}, invoking \texttt{p!:} without any arguments shall always generate a string of \textbf{utmost 100 characters long}. This means, it might be as short as just 1 or a few characters, but also that, it would never exceed 100 characters. However, and when required, one can tightly control exactly how many and even what kind of strings this command can generate as we see next...

\vspace{2em}

The following basic TEA program for example, is guaranteed to generate useful random strings of exactly 10 characters spanning the Latin Alphabet:


\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=P: EXAMPLE 3: A Constrained RSG in TEA, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
P!:10
# could return "ssrmykqzyz", "dfooctwrid", "gdo yoqqlt", etc
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{2em}

Finally, note that, despite using different approaches, both \texttt{P:} --- a permutation generator, and \texttt{P!:} --- a random string generator, generate random strings. The key difference between them is that the first takes a starter string (such as AI or the argument it is given) and randomizes it without omitting any character from it --- thus ``anagrams". While the other either uses the standard Latin Alphabet ($\psi_{az}$) or some starter ``seed" string it is given or a specified ``alphabet", and generates random strings of arbitrary or specified length merely by sampling or choosing elements from the given seed. Thus, though they might be in similar contexts, the two generators are fundamentally different and each might be suited for specific purposes the other isn't.


\vspace{2em}

Concerning parametric use of the permutation command, the following example demonstrates how to generate so-called ``Vowel Hymns" or ``Vowel Mantras"\cite{lutalo_2025_grimlumtauto} based on chaining anagrams of the vowel set: 


\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=P: EXAMPLE 4: A Constrained Permutation Generator in TEA that generates ``Vowel Mantras", breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
v:vLIMIT:10|v:vLLIMIT:4|v:vALPH:{aeiou}|v:vGLUE:-|p*:vALPH:vGLUE:vLIMIT:vLLIMIT
#equivalent to just
#p:aeiou:-:10:4
#returns strings such as:
#oueai-iaeou-ueoia-eiuoa-oiuea-eoaui-uieoa-aeuoi
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{2em}


That example attempts to demonstrate how to leverage all the parameters that the \texttt{p*:} command accepts. However, for the case of generating beautiful vowel hymns, perhaps the version using the RSG command and which is depicted in the first example application of \textbf{P:} shall be more convincing...





\section{EXAMPLE APPLICATIONS of P:}
\label{SECEXAMPP}

  
As we have seen in the introductions, the \textbf{PERMUTATE} command is great for generating random strings or creating random variants of specified strings. And so, with regards to the kinds of applications we can envision leveraging this utility and the most of the \texttt{P:} command space, we shall explore applications that involve or require working with random strings mostly.


\subsection{EXAMPLE 1: \textbf{MUSIC \& POETRY:} A Random VOWEL HYMN Generator}
\label{SECEXAMP1P}


For those new to the idea of ``vowel hymns"\cite{lutalo_2025_grimlumtauto}, note that these are exemplified in the performance by the Ugandan Melodic Metal band \textbf{MAZERA}, on their 2025 \textbf{XMas Metal} album as the song ``Vowel Hymns" --- see: [audio] \url{https://t.me/mazeraworks/1152} and [audio-video] \url{https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=p_THWo4MjIU}



\vspace{2em}


 \begin{figure}[H]
 \Large
  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=TEA Program:  Random VOWEL HYMN Generator]
    \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
#############################################
# RANDOM VOWEL HYMN GENERATOR v1
#############################################
# Though legit vowel hymns ought be generated
# based on transforming the text of legit hymns
# or words, this generator instead generates 
#such hymns based on nothing really, but the
# outputs shall read and feel like legitimate
#vowel hymns nonetheless.
#-------------------------------------------
# For Theory: https://bit.ly/grimoirelumtauto
# For Example: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=p_THWo4MjIU
##############################################
v:vLINES:5 | v:vI:0 | v:vHYMN:{}
v:vLIMIT:10 | v:vGLUE:{ } | v:vALPHABET:{aeiou }
l:LOOP
p*!:vLIMIT:vGLUE:vALPHABET | v:vLINE #generate and store random string
g*:{
}:vHYMN:vLINE | v:vHYMN #add it to the hymn
y:vI | x!:{+1} | r.: | v:vI 
y:vI | x!:{>} | x*!:vLINES | r.: | f:^true$:FIN:LOOP #test if we must finish
l:FIN | y:vHYMN | k!:^$ #return clean hymn
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
  \caption{TEA APPLICATION: Random VOWEL HYMN Generator}
  \label{FIGP1}
\end{figure}


\vspace{2em}

So, that short but generic program shall generate interesting vowel hymns such as:


\vspace{2em}
  
   \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample Vowel Hymn OUTPUT]
aai iu oo
uouieae ai
uuiue e 
 eu uo iu
eiaioauooe
e iouieeai
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 

\vspace{2em}

  
   \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample Vowel Hymn OUTPUT]
iai ua uee
ee ouaoiei
ii aaeii e
oia uaoueo
a a ua u
 oeoouoiiu
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 

\vspace{2em}


\vspace{2em}

  
   \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample Vowel Hymn OUTPUT]
aeieo uoou
aua ei i 
iioe ii i 
ea oi iu 
eoeeeaeea 
eua oiiu 
  \end{tcbverbatim}
  
  
  
  \vspace{2em}

To access this service --- so-called \textbf{FREE SPIRITUAL MEDICINE}, use any of the options:

\vspace{2em}

\begin{enumerate}
\item Find VOWEL HYMN GENERATOR on the list of standard TEA programs via:\\ \url{https://tea.nuchwezi.com}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\chapter{Q: QUIT}
\label{SECQ}


\begin{table}[H]
  \centering
  \LARGE
	\begin{tabular}[t]{|p{0.2\textwidth}|p{0.5\textwidth}}
 
	\textbf{NAME} & \texttt{QUIT}\\
	\hline
	\textbf{Purpose} & \begin{enumerate}
	\item Quit a TEA program
	\item Unconditionally quit a TEA program
	\item Quit a TEA program upon some condition
	\item Facilitate TEA program debugging and profiling --- via \textit{break-point}-like \textbf{cut-points}
	\item Another logic and program control-flow mechanism in TEA programming.
	\end{enumerate}\\
	\hline
	              
\end{tabular}
\caption{General Objectives of TEA Primitive \textbf{Q:}}
  \label{TABTAZQ}
\end{table}


\section{SEMANTICS of Q:}
\label{SECSEMQ}


\begin{table}[H]
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3} % Optional: increases row height
\rowcolors{1}{lightgray}{white}   % Alternating row colors
\begin{tabular}{>{\bfseries}m{0.4\linewidth} | m{0.6\linewidth}} % 2 columns, 
\rowcolor{white}
\textbf{\makecell[l]{INSTRUCTION\\ SIGNATURE}} & \textbf{INSTRUCTION FUNCTION} \\
\hline

q: & Quit the program if AI is currently empty. Returns Empty String\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{q:REGEX} & Return the current AI and Quit the program iff AI matches REGEX\\

q!: & \textbf{Unconditionally quit} the TEA program returning the current AI\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{q!:REGEX} & Return the current AI and Quit the program iff AI DOES NOT match REGEX\\


\makecell[l]{q*:} & Quit the program if THERE ARE NO VAULTs set (doesn't quit if the DEFAULT VAULT was at least set)\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{q*:vREGEX} & Like q:REGEX, but using pattern stored in vault vREGEX\\

\makecell[l]{q*!:vREGEX} & Like q!:REGEX, but using pattern stored in vREGEX\\

 \hline
\end{tabular}
\caption{The Semantics of Q:}
\label{TABSEMQ}
\end{table}


\section{NOTES about Q:}
\label{SECNOTEQ}
  
  
Like \texttt{f:}, \texttt{q:} helps to introduce conditional processing into
TEA programs, and with the \texttt{q:} and \texttt{q!:} command, it becomes easy to ensure our TEA Programs shall always halt and that it's possible to control when they should halt.

\vspace{2em}


The following example program will avoid trying to run the expensive \texttt{p:} command on gibberish or unwanted externally obtained text: 


\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=Q: EXAMPLE 1: Quit Data Processing Upon Error, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
# assuming we want alpha-numeric string (National Identification Number)
# fetched from some [hypothetical] file hosted online...
w!: https://pastebin.org/NIN.txt
v:vA
# However, not wishing to process anything incase the resource cannot be reached..
Q:ERROR # quit in case output contains "ERROR" 
# Otherwise, present processed result...
g!:{-} | x:{RESULT: }
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{2em}


So that example helps us see that \textbf{Q:PATTERN} can be used to force the program to quit, the moment the specified literal string or regular expression pattern, \texttt{PATTERN}, is found within the output or rather \textbf{Active Input} (AI) at the moment that instruction is executed. So that, in that example, since we are trying to fetch data from a non-existent or innaccessible online resource, we shall obtain the typical error message such as 

\vspace{2em}

\begin{verse}
[ERROR]: NetworkError: Failed to execute 'send' on 'XMLHttpRequest': Failed to load 'https://pastebin.org/NIN.txt'.
\end{verse}

\vspace{2em}


And so that, since the message matches the pattern specified in the instruction \texttt{Q:ERROR}, we shall get just that error message as the output and the program quits. Otherwise, in case we comment out that instruction or perhaps change the condition pattern to something that is different --- but with still the same above error happening, or perhaps getting the correct expected output, then we shall instead obtain a result such as...

\vspace{1em}


  \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Output from (Modified) Example 1]
RESULT: -ERROR-NetworkError-Failed-to-execute-send-on-XMLHttpRequest-Failed-to-load-https-pastebin-org-NIN-txt-
  \end{tcbverbatim}
  
\vspace{1em}
  
The following example further demonstrates how \texttt{Q:} can be useful. For example, this program will return a random number if the input contains a hyphen``-" in it, otherwise will return the input with its content shuffled:


\vspace{1em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=Q: EXAMPLE 2: Using Q for Control-Flow and Dynamic Outputs, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:"hi there"
i:"hi-there"
a!:|Q!:-|n: 
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

%draw horizontal line
\noindent\rule{\textwidth}{0.4pt}

\textbf{IMPORTANT!} Note that any TEA program whose first instruction is just ``q!:" shall never do anything useful other than immediately quit/return.

%draw horizontal line
\noindent\rule{\textwidth}{0.4pt}

\vspace{2em}

For the cases where we use the other variants of q:, especially those that can cause the program to quit based on values in vaults, the following is a good test example:


\vspace{1em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=Q: EXAMPLE 3: Using Q With Patterns in Vaults, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:{Hello World} #we start by setting the message "hello world"
v: # we then store than into default vault
q*!: # should quit if default vault (with name "") is blank
a!: #otherwise anagrammatize AI
v:vANA #store that into a vault
q*: #quit in case default vault is blank
i!: # clear AI
v: # store that into default vault
y:vANA # set AI to contents of vault "vANA"
q*!: # quit in case default vault is blank
#otherwise return some random number prefixing anagrammatized original message
e:"v:A|n:|x!:{:}|x*!:A"
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

As is, the program shall return ``Hello World" shuffled. Note that only the final quit command \texttt{q*!:} takes effect, because the default vault was set to an empty value with the two earlier instructions \texttt{i!: | v:}. Otherwise (like if we change that bit to \texttt{i!:TEST | v:} or eliminate that final \textbf{Quit} command, then the program would return the shuffled original message prefixed by a random number --- something like ``8:HleolW odlr"



\section{EXAMPLE APPLICATIONS of Q:}
\label{SECEXAMPQ}

  
Especially because we can use \textbf{Q:} commands for control flow in a TEA program with either complex or non-linear program logic, the example application we shall explore shall especially hinge on this --- using quit patterns to decide when to stop processing and quit or return processing results especially.


\subsection{EXAMPLE 1: \textbf{PSYCHOLOGY \& ASTROLOGY:} PERSONALITY CALCULATOR  }
\label{SECEXAMP1Q}


In many cultures and traditions around the world, it is well known among students and practitioners of especially occult science and wisdom, that, there are certain particular but also general attributes of a person --- such as their family or given names, their date of birth, their gender and such, that when analyzed from the context of both ancient and modern divination and astrological frameworks, allows one to isolate and tell or predict several general traits and or profiles that might fit or explain the person's psychological, spiritual and lifestyle character. For many people that only look at the world through a materialistic and especially objective lens only, much of this might come off as sheer speculation or as kinds of \textit{woo-woo} otherwise hard to objectify, prove or disprove via normal, scientific means. However, and as we learn from one of the modern authorities on this subject --- particularly the huge book, \textbf{The Secret Language of Birthdays: Personology Profiles for Each Day of the Year}\cite{goldschneider1994secret} by Gary Goldschneider and Joost Elffers, there is indeed both an objective and subjective dimension to this ``esoteric science", and that for those willing to invest some time into understanding the underlying theories and application frameworks, the idea of being able to discern a person's character and or personality merely based on minimal baseline information such as just their date of birth, isn't all just speculation.

\vspace{2em}

In this next application then, we shall especially focus on just one attribute of a person's identity --- their \textbf{date of birth}, and from this, shall then compute and present a brief profile of their personality based on the information and guidelines already laid out in Gary's work\cite{goldschneider1994secret}.

\vspace{2em}

The basic approach to using this application is as such:

\begin{enumerate}
\item First, one picks the Month of the Year.
\item Second, one specifies the Day of the Month.
\item Finally, computes the relevant personality profile and returns it as the final program output.
\item Iterate or quit, based on user input.
\end{enumerate} 
  
 
 \vspace{2em}
 
 The relevant source for this very useful and interesting application is as such...
 
 
  
  \vspace{2em}


%\begin{figure}[H]
{
  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=TEA Program: Personality Calculator v3, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
#+++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++
#+ PERSONALITY CALCULATOR (PC) v3
#-------------------------------------------------
#+ This program is based on western astrology
#+ And is meant to help predict or compute a
#+ person's personality trait and character 
#+ based on just their Bithdate information.
#+ THEORY: Gary et. al (1994):
#+ https://openlibrary.org/books/OL1102071M/The_Secret_Language_of_Birthdays
#+++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++
# Program Architect: Fut. Prof. JWL (NES)
#-------------------------------------------------

#---[ CONSTANTS ]
v:vDELIM:{
~~~~~~~~~~~~|~~~~~~~~~~~~
}
v:vDELIM_CREDIT:{
~~~[CREDIT: PC v3@ tea.nuchwezi.com ]~~~
}
v:vAPPNAME:{PERSONALITY CALCULATOR v3}
v:vPURPOSE:{Using ancient science and principles, PC can tell you a person's traits and character based on just their date of birth.}
v:vGUIDE:{To use, simply specify the person's birth Month and Day. | [qQ] to QUIT.}
v:vMENU_MONTHS:{Select Month: 
1:JAN | 2:FEB | 3:MAR | 4:APR | 5:MAY | 6:JUN
7:JUL | 8:AUG | 9:SEP | 10:OCT | 11:NOV | 12:DEC
}
v:vMENU_DAY:{Specify DAY of Birth: }

#for checking Month-Day bounds using a single test...
z.:PLATFORM | v:vPLATFORM
y:vPLATFORM | f:WEB:lTESTER_WEB:lTESTER_CLI
l:lTESTER_WEB
v:vTESTER:{(LM*100+LD) <= (@*100+.) && (@*100+.) <= (UM*100+UD)} 
j:lSKIP_CLI_TESTER
l:lTESTER_CLI
v:vTESTER:{(LM*100+LD) <= (@*100+.) & (@*100+.) <= (UM*100+UD)} 
l:lSKIP_CLI_TESTER

v:vPAT_DAY:{.}
v:vPAT_MON:{@}
v:vTEMP_PROF:{Born on DD MM (SS)} | v:vT_DD:{DD} | v:vT_MM:{MM} | v:vT_SS:{SS}
v:vPROFILE:{No Processing Done Yet.}

#---[ INTRODUCTIONS ]
g*:{}:vDELIM:vAPPNAME:vDELIM:vPURPOSE | k!:^[ ]*$ | v:vWELCOME
g*:{}:vDELIM:vAPPNAME:vDELIM:vGUIDE | k!:^[ ]*$ | v:vINSTRUCTIONS

#present intro interface...
l:lSTART
y:vWELCOME | i*: | y:vINSTRUCTIONS | i*:

#should we quit?
t.:| f:^[qQ]$:lQUIT

#prompt for month
l:lPROMPT_MON
y:vMENU_MONTHS | i*: | v:OPT
#verify choice or loop...
t.: | f!:{^(([1-9])|(1[012]))$}:lPROMPT_MON:lPROCESS_MONTH
l:lPROCESS_MONTH
v:vN_MON #store month


#prompt for day
l:lPROMPT_DAY
y:vMENU_DAY | i*: | v:OPT
#verify choice or loop...
t.: | f!:{^(([1-9])|([12][0-9])|(3[01]))$}:lPROMPT_DAY:lPROCESS_DAY
l:lPROCESS_DAY
v:vN_DAY #store day

#-----------
#process day position
y:vN_DAY
f:1$:lST:lNST
l:lST
x!:{st} | v:vPOSLABEL
j:lADDPOSITION

l:lNST
y:vN_DAY
f:2$:lSND:lNSND
l:lSND
x!:{nd} | v:vPOSLABEL
j:lADDPOSITION

l:lNSND
y:vN_DAY
f:3$:lRD:lTH
l:lRD
x!:{rd} | v:vPOSLABEL
j:lADDPOSITION

l:lTH
y:vN_DAY
x!:{th} | v:vPOSLABEL
j:lADDPOSITION

l:lADDPOSITION
y:vPOSLABEL | v:vDAY
#-----------

#-----------
#process month name
v:vMNAMES:{JAN FEB MAR APR MAY JUN JUL AUG SEP OCT NOV DEC}
v:vN_MI:{0}
l:lSEEKMON
y:vN_MI | x!:{+1} | r.: | v:vN_MI
y:vMNAMES | d:[ ].*$ | v:vMON
y:vMNAMES | d!:[ ].*$ | t.: | v:vMNAMES 
y:vN_MON | x!:{==} | x*!:vN_MI | r.: | f:^true$:lFINMON:lSEEKMON
l:lFINMON
#-----------

#---[ COMPUTE PROFILE ]
# Our Method is to Limit Profiling to 4 Seasons of Birth

#==[ SPRING: MAR 21-JUN 21]
#build the test...
y:vTESTER | r!:LM:3 | r!:UM:6 | r!:LD:21 | r!:UD:21 | v:vTEST 
r*!:vTEST:vPAT_DAY:vN_DAY | v:vTEST
r*!:vTEST:vPAT_MON:vN_MON | v:vTEST
y:vTEST | r.: | f!:^true$:lNOTSPRING 
#build profile...
v:vSEASON:{SPRING}

#fill-in profile...
r*!:vTEMP_PROF:vT_DD:vDAY | v:vPROFILE
r*!:vPROFILE:vT_MM:vMON | v:vPROFILE
r*!:vPROFILE:vT_SS:vSEASON | v:vPROFILE

x*!:vDELIM 
x!:{SIGNS: Aries, Taurus, Gemini
RULERS: Mars, Venus, Mercury
SYMBOLS: Ram, Bull, Twins
ELEMENTS: Fire, Earth, Air
MOTTOS: I Am, I Have, I Communicate
DOMINANT FACULTY: Intuition}

x*!:vDELIM 
x!:{Enthusiast for life. Enjoys initiating [new] projects. More extroverted than introverted. Likes to share results. Child-like. Innocent, Spontaneous, and Impulsive. Generally Positive Minded.}

v:vPROFILE #store complete profile

j:lFINISH #we got profile complete...

l:lNOTSPRING #it wasn't spring..
#==[ SUMMER: JUN 22-SEP 23]
#build the test...
y:vTESTER | r!:LM:6 | r!:UM:9 | r!:LD:22 | r!:UD:23 | v:vTEST 
r*!:vTEST:vPAT_DAY:vN_DAY | v:vTEST
r*!:vTEST:vPAT_MON:vN_MON | v:vTEST
y:vTEST | r.: | f!:^true$:lNOTSUMMER 
#build profile...
v:vSEASON:{SUMMER}

#fill-in profile...
r*!:vTEMP_PROF:vT_DD:vDAY | v:vPROFILE
r*!:vPROFILE:vT_MM:vMON | v:vPROFILE
r*!:vPROFILE:vT_SS:vSEASON | v:vPROFILE

x*!:vDELIM 
x!:{SIGNS: Cancer, Leo, Virgo
RULERS: Moon, Sun, Mercury
SYMBOLS: Crab, Lion, Virgin
ELEMENTS: Water, Fire, Earth
MOTTOS: I Feel, I Create, I Serve
DOMINANT FACULTY: Feeling}

x*!:vDELIM 
x!:{Very critical about life. Enjoys completing [existing] projects. Mixed between extroverted than introverted based on feelings. Emotionally attached to people, work and environment. Not eager to shine in the world. Often willing to serve others. Need to be needed.}

v:vPROFILE #store complete profile

j:lFINISH #we got profile complete...

l:lNOTSUMMER #it wasn't summer..
#==[ FALL: SEP 24-DEC 21]
#build the test...
y:vTESTER | r!:LM:9 | r!:UM:12 | r!:LD:24 | r!:UD:21 | v:vTEST 
r*!:vTEST:vPAT_DAY:vN_DAY | v:vTEST
r*!:vTEST:vPAT_MON:vN_MON | v:vTEST
y:vTEST | r.: | f!:^true$:lNOTFALL 
#build profile...
v:vSEASON:{FALL}

#fill-in profile...
r*!:vTEMP_PROF:vT_DD:vDAY | v:vPROFILE
r*!:vPROFILE:vT_MM:vMON | v:vPROFILE
r*!:vPROFILE:vT_SS:vSEASON | v:vPROFILE

x*!:vDELIM 
x!:{SIGNS: Libra, Scorpio, Sagittarius
RULERS: Venus, Pluto, Jupiter
SYMBOLS: Scales, Scorpion, Archer
ELEMENTS: Air, Water, Fire
MOTTOS: I Weigh, I Control, I Philosophize
DOMINANT FACULTY: Sensation}

x*!:vDELIM 
x!:{Like to be in control of their life. Often very selective and critical. Like to maintain rather than initiate or change projects. Typically more introverted than not. Keep feelings and emotions under strict control. Have strong urge to participate in serious relationships. Often very conscious of what is going on in society.}

v:vPROFILE #store complete profile

j:lFINISH #we got profile complete...

l:lNOTFALL  #it wasn't fall..
#==[ WINTER: DEC 22-MAR 21]
#no need to test.. 
#build profile...
v:vSEASON:{WINTER}

#fill-in profile...
r*!:vTEMP_PROF:vT_DD:vDAY | v:vPROFILE
r*!:vPROFILE:vT_MM:vMON | v:vPROFILE
r*!:vPROFILE:vT_SS:vSEASON | v:vPROFILE

x*!:vDELIM 
x!:{SIGNS: Capricorn, Aquarius, Pisces
RULERS: Saturn, Uranus, Neptune
SYMBOLS: Goat, Water Bearer, Fish
ELEMENTS: Earth, Air, Water
MOTTOS: I Matter, I Universalize, I Believe
DOMINANT FACULTY: Thought}

x*!:vDELIM 
x!:{More concerned with larger picture of life. Often rulers and dominant. Often the most flexible, sensitive, accepting, and spiritual. Typically quiet, mostly expressing themselves through their work and ideas. Readily distinguished by their active imagination and fantasies. Can objectify fantasies and imaginations. Very idealistic and utopian.}

v:vPROFILE #store complete profile

j:lFINISH #we got profile complete...

#---[ FINISH ]
l:lFINISH

#present completed profile if any, then loop...
y:vPROFILE | i*: | j:lSTART

l:lQUIT
# otherwise, let's quit gracefully..
y:vPROFILE | x*!:vDELIM_CREDIT
Q!: #well, quit
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
    \captionof{figure}{TEA APPLICATION: Personality Calculator (PC) v3}
  \label{FIGQEX1}
  \vspace{1cm}
  

\vspace{2em}
  

Note that, even though Gary's book provides more detailed information than one might need for a cursory mini-profile associated with a particular birthday, our PC program attempts to distill the most critically important facts concerning any such profile, and will produce succinct, somewhat general (since we don't attempt to compute date-specific profiles even though cite{goldschneider1994secret} provides them) personality profiles such as:


\vspace{2em}
  
   \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample PC OUTPUT]
Born on 23rd AUG (SUMMER)
~~~~~~~~~~~~|~~~~~~~~~~~~
SIGNS: Cancer, Leo, Virgo
RULERS: Moon, Sun, Mercury
SYMBOLS: Crab, Lion, Virgin
ELEMENTS: Water, Fire, Earth
MOTTOS: I Feel, I Create, I Serve
DOMINANT FACULTY: Feeling
~~~~~~~~~~~~|~~~~~~~~~~~~
Very critical about life. Enjoys completing [existing] projects. Mixed between extroverted than introverted based on feelings. Emotionally attached to people, work and environment. Not eager to shine in the world. Often willing to serve others. Need to be needed.
~~~[CREDIT: PC v3@ tea.nuchwezi.com ]~~~
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 

\vspace{2em}

 
  
   \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample PC OUTPUT]
Born on 25th DEC (WINTER)
~~~~~~~~~~~~|~~~~~~~~~~~~
SIGNS: Capricorn, Aquarius, Pisces
RULERS: Saturn, Uranus, Neptune
SYMBOLS: Goat, Water Bearer, Fish
ELEMENTS: Earth, Air, Water
MOTTOS: I Matter, I Universalize, I Believe
DOMINANT FACULTY: Thought
~~~~~~~~~~~~|~~~~~~~~~~~~
More concerned with larger picture of life. Often rulers and dominant. Often the most flexible, sensitive, accepting, and spiritual. Typically quiet, mostly expressing themselves through their work and ideas. Readily distinguished by their active imagination and fantasies. Can objectify fantasies and imaginations. Very idealistic and utopian.
~~~[CREDIT: PC v3@ tea.nuchwezi.com ]~~~
  \end{tcbverbatim}
