\chapter{R: REPLACE-REWRITE}
\label{SECR}


\begin{table}[H]
  \centering
  \LARGE
	\begin{tabular}[t]{|p{0.2\textwidth}|p{0.5\textwidth}}
 
	\textbf{NAME} & \texttt{REPLACE/REWRITE}\\
	\hline
	\textbf{Purpose} & \begin{enumerate}
	\item Replace things with other things
	\item Perform \textbf{Text Substitution}
	\item Automate Text Editing using Pattern Matching
	\item \textbf{Replace Mathematical Expressions with Simple Terminal Values:} pure or fractional numbers or boolean values. Only TEA command designed for this.
	\item Facilitate \textbf{Processing of Invisible Text!}
	\item Facilitate a Powerful General-Purpose \textbf{Re-Write System} using TEA.
	\end{enumerate}\\
	\hline
	              
\end{tabular}
\caption{General Objectives of TEA Primitive \textbf{R:}}
  \label{TABTAZR}
\end{table}


\section{SEMANTICS of R:}
\label{SECSEMR}


\begin{table}[H]
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3} % Optional: increases row height
\rowcolors{1}{lightgray}{white}   % Alternating row colors
\begin{tabular}{>{\bfseries}m{0.4\linewidth} | m{0.6\linewidth}} % 2 columns, 
\rowcolor{white}
\textbf{\makecell[l]{INSTRUCTION\\ SIGNATURE}} & \textbf{INSTRUCTION FUNCTION} \\
\hline

r: & Replaces all visible characters in AI with the SINGLE WHITESPACE, and all whitespace other than the NEW LINE character in AI with the FULL-STOP character.\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{r:REGEX:SUBSTR} & Return AI with the first section matching REGEX replaced by SUBSTR\\

r!: & The Extreme Case of \texttt{r:} --- replaces all visible characters in AI with the EMPTY STRING and any whitespace except the NEW LINE character with the FULL-STOP character.\\


\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{r!:REGEX:SUBSTR} & Return AI with ALL sections matching REGEX replaced by SUBSTR\\


\makecell[l]{r*:} & INERT\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{r*:vNAME:vREGEX:vSUBSTR} & Same as r:REGEX:SUBSTR,but operating on the string stored in the vault with the name vNAME  instead of AI.\\

\makecell[l]{r*:vNAME} & With only vNAME, like \texttt{r:}, but operating on string in the named vault, vNAME instead of AI. Result becomes AI.\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{r*!:vNAME} & Same as \texttt{r!:}, but operating on the string stored in the vault with the name vNAME  instead of AI.\\

\makecell[l]{r*!:vNAME:vREGEX:vSUBSTR} & Same as \texttt{r!:REGEX:SUBSTR}, but operating on the strings stored in the specified vaults\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{r.:\\r.:EXPR} & Replace AI with the result of evaluating it as a pure arithmetic expression. With EXPR, instead operate on the explicit string EXPR. In all cases, result becomes AI, and in case the expression can’t be processed, AI is set to EMPTY SPACE. This is the only way to process basic explicit mathematics in TEA without using Z:\\

\makecell[l]{r*.:vEXPR} & Like r.:EXPR, but instead processing the string stored in vault vEXPR.\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{r*.:} & INERT\\


\makecell[l]{r@:\\r@:EXPR} & Treat AI as a \textbf{TEA Template Expression} and rewrite it by replacing all occurrences of variables/vaults expressed as with \texttt{\#vA\#} for some vault named \texttt{vA} with the value stored in that vault [\textbf{without recursion}]. With the parameters \textbf{EXPR} provided, then instead operate on that explicit string instead of AI. Returns the result of the re-write operation or unmodified expression if no transformations occurred. In case Template contains a \textbf{deep variable} such as \texttt{\#\#vA\#\#}, also apply the transform on the contents of the vault [recursively] before making the substitution.\\

\makecell[l]{r*@:vEXPR} & Same as \texttt{r@:EXPR} but instead loads the expression to be produced from the vaul with specified name \textbf{vEXPR}. Without vEXPR parameter, just works on AI as \texttt{R@:}\\

\makecell[l]{r@!:\\r@!:EXPR} & The deep resolution version of \texttt{R@:} and \texttt{R@:vEXPR}, in which mode, \textbf{any template variable deep or not, is resolved recursively.}\\

\makecell[l]{r*@!:vEXPR} & Same as \texttt{r@!:EXPR} but instead loads the expression to be produced from the vaul with specified name \textbf{vEXPR}. Without vEXPR parameter, just works on AI as \texttt{R@!:}\\




 \hline
\end{tabular}
\caption{The Semantics of R:}
\label{TABSEMR}
\end{table}


\section{NOTES about R:}
\label{SECNOTER}
  

String substitution as a core operation in most text processing, finds its main mechanics implemented using the R-command space in TEA. However, it should be noted that r: primitives aren’t the only kind that can perform string substitution in TEA. Some kinds of text replacement operations are possible using other TEA primitives such as g: that replaces whitespace with empty space, but also does some automatic replacements on punctuation with the g! variant.

\vspace{2em}

\textbf{IMPORTANT:} It shall be worth noting that with the REPLACE command in TEA, there are two modes of performing substitutions in all cases. NAÏVE mode: in which, the pattern or regex in the command signature is treated as a literal pattern search string. This mode is very useful in situations where the search pattern might contain special characters that would otherwise be hard to correctly express via a regular expression. However, in case such case, if the replace command finds that the substitution cannot be done or that it effects no change, then it resorts to the regular expression (REGEX) mode. Thus, when in doubt, you can rest-assured that as long as the search pattern exists literally in the target string, it shall be replaced correctly with either r: or r!: or r*: or r*!:. However, when needed, know that the REGEX mode shall likewise work as expected, using the same exact command signature! Thus, the REPLACE TEA instruction somewhat performs smart substitution, and one can tell which mode was used, by running their commands using DEBUG MODE. 

\vspace{2em}

For example, when one has a text such as

\vspace{2em}

\begin{verbatim}
{
"Name": "<p!:8|t.:|z*:>",
"Age": "<n:26:8>"
"Description": "<p!:|s:|s:|z:>"
}
\end{verbatim}

\vspace{2em}

And one wishes to replace the substring ``<p!:8|t.:|z*:>", you shall notice that, if we were to merely write a replace call such as:
  
\vspace{1em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=R: EXAMPLE 1: Using R*:, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
r*:vSCHEMA_TEMPLATE:vTEA_EXP:vTEA_EXP_VAL
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

Assuming \texttt{vSCHEMA\_TEMPLATE} contains the text to be processed,  \texttt{vTEA\_EXP} contains the pattern to use to search for what to replace, and  \texttt{vTEA\_EXP\_VAL} contains the substitution value e.g ``John Doe". Then, if we run this command when the vault \texttt{vTEA\_EXP} contains the literal string ``<p!:8|t.:|z*:>", then the substitution shall operate as expected via \textbf{naive mode}. However, if we instead have that vault containing the regular expression ``\verb|<[^"]*>|" such as via the operation:


\vspace{2em}

\begin{verbatim}
v:vTEA_EXP:{<[^"]*>}
\end{verbatim}

\vspace{2em}

We shall then correctly find (in either case), that the result we obtain is the text:


\begin{verbatim}
{
"Name": "John Doe",
"Age": "<n:26:8>"
"Description": "<p!:|s:|s:|z:>"
}
\end{verbatim}


\vspace{2em}


Concerning controlling what to replace in a string, note that \texttt{r:REGEX:STR} replaces only the FIRST occurrence of REGEX in what is being processed with STR, but \texttt{r!:REGEX:STR} replaces ALL occurrences of the pattern (REGEX) in (STR) what is being processed. We see this by the following two examples:


\vspace{1em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=R: EXAMPLE 2, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:I like this|r:[aeiou]:_: 
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

returns ``\verb|I l_:ke this|", while 

\vspace{1em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=R: EXAMPLE 3, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:I like this|r!:[aeiou]:_:  
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}


returns ``\verb|I l_:k_: th_:s|". Basically, the \texttt{R!:} form performs multiple substitutions, while \texttt{R:} only replaces the first occurrence of the target pattern.


\vspace{2em}


\subsection{The Peculiar Side of R: --- Using R: without explicit arguments}

\subsubsection{The Braillish Transforms: r: and r!:}

Note that the \texttt{r:} and \texttt{r!:} primitives --- in their \textbf{argumentless-signatures} --- offer distinct, but related \textbf{cryptographic utilities} for \textbf{using white-space as information} in machine-readable cryptograms. Essentially, they offer a strange but useful means to read ``words between lines" so to say; systematically \textbf{converting usual writing using visible text to a kind of invisible but readable writing system} --- writing using whitespace, an important primitive capability for some families of cryptography as we are about to see. 


\vspace{2em}


Also, note that \texttt{r:} and \texttt{r!:} work very differently, though somewhat similarly. The first recognizes visible characters (replacing them with white space in the result) and new lines, and otherwise simplifies reading original white-space, while \texttt{r!:} only recognizes original white-space, eliminating originally visible characters (replacing them with empty space in the result) and thus only recognizes spaces and tabs (replacing them with "." in the result) and new lines. Some examples shall help drive these points home...


\vspace{2em}

Assuming we have the following simple text as the input:


   \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample Text Input]
Myself should tell 
You O my Lord.
I trust You Know Me.
  \end{tcbverbatim}
  
  
  \vspace{2em}
  
  
We can then appreciate the ``Braillish" --- meaning, like \textbf{Braille} --- projections of it with \texttt{r:} and \texttt{r!:} as seen hereafter. First, assume we the \texttt{r:} instruction to that text as such:


\vspace{1em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=R: EXAMPLE 4, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:{Myself should tell 
You O my Lord.
I trust You Know Me.} | r:
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

We would then obtain the following kind of output..


   \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample Text OUTPUT from Example 4]
      .      .    .
   . .  .     
 .     .   .    .   
  \end{tcbverbatim}


\vspace{1em}

While, if we instead apply \texttt{r!:} to the same text, as such:

\vspace{1em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=R: EXAMPLE 5, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:{Myself should tell 
You O my Lord.
I trust You Know Me.} | r!:
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

We would then obtain the following kind of output..


   \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample Text OUTPUT from Example 5]
...
...
....   
  \end{tcbverbatim}


\vspace{1em}


More specifically, note that, if we had some basic text such as ``Hello World", and then applied \texttt{r:} to it, we would obtain the peculiar ``     .     ". This, because, \textbf{R:} would override every originally visible character with a SINGLE SPACE character, and then replace all originally invisible text (essentially white-space) \textbf{except the new-line character} with a FULLSTOP ``.". 

\vspace{2em}

While, applying just \texttt{r!:} to ``Hello World" would result in just ``." --- so that, we see that all originally visible text is eliminated (essentially, each originally visible character was replaced by an EMPTY SPACE), while all originally invisible text (apart from the new line character) is replaced by a single FULLSTOP at every occurrence; so, ``Hello  World", with two spaces between the words would result in ``     ..      " when \texttt{r:} is applied to it, however, it would result in the more compact ``.." when \texttt{r!:} is the one applied to it.


\vspace{2em}


These two instructions were definitely inspired by braille --- a system of reading and writing that is conducive to blind people --- however, they aren't exactly like braille; in the sense that, these commands do not try to encode visible text using braille-like code or characters, but instead would serve purposes more inclined towards developing codes and forms of cryptographic writing that can leverage ``hiding messages" in normal text using white-space or otherwise leveraging the length of words to encode messages instead of their literal contents, etc. This line of work is still under active research\footnote{\textbf{NOTE:} This line of inquiry was originally kick-started at Nuchwezi Research as early as 2020, via projects such as \textbf{Ghost Messages} --- \url{https://ghost.nuchwezi.com} also check \url{https://codepen.io/mcnemesis/pen/zqBmdL}}. Clearly, we see that these two primitives serve a role very hard to replace with anything else!


\subsubsection{TEA Mathematical Expressions and The Mathematics Re-Write Transform: r.:}
\label{SECTME}

It should first of all be worth noting that in fact, the TEA \textbf{R} command space should have originally been named \textbf{REWRITE} and not \textbf{REPLACE} --- especially because of how several classical and popular terminologies for re-write systems in both computer science and software engineering are named or how they operate; e.g the fact that the fundamental concept behind much if not all of how software language engineering (the stuff that makes all programming languages possible) is based on the theories and formalisms of re-write systems such as BNF and generally, production rules that essentially reduce any or most programming language implementations to just a series of re-writing mechanism that take formal expressions in one system or language, and then re-write them in another --- compilers and interpreters, all the way from first-generation low-level languages, to fourth+ generation high-level languages such as C++, Java and even this TEA language all are fundamentally based on \textbf{re-write systems}. And that, \textbf{replacing} or rather \textit{substitution} and \textbf{rewriting} are conceptually equivalent: terms or expressions in one formal system or language getting replaced by those in another --- such as when identifiers (variable names, function names, function arguments, etc) are replaced by their literal values (or perhaps addresses to the literal values) in the translation (for compilers) or evaluation (for interpreters) of expressions in a language like C or Python respectively.

\vspace{2em}

In TEA though, we especially encounter the notion of a re-write system via command spaces such as \textbf{ZAP} --- which knows to take command expressions targeting the external/non-TEA environment (such as Windows or Linux system/shell for CLI TEA or JavaScript for WEB TEA) and return their equivalent expression after their evaluation. And then the TEA \textbf{EVALUATE} command space (see \textbf{\hyperref[CHAPE]{Chapter \ref{CHAPE}}}), that accepts any valid TEA expression and returns/rewrites it with the result of its evaluation.

\vspace{2em}

However, and especially because at core, and originally, TEA was meant or designed to be a purely TEXT-PROCESSING oriented language --- essentially, such that even [usually] basic things such as evaluating mathematical expressions were meant to be implemented via mere text-processing that is devoid of any notions of type-systems --- especially since, for TEA, everything is just text or string irrespective of how it is expressed, we later found, after especially intensive practical explorations of how the TEA language might be utilized in real-life (such as most of the work in the present manual, the TAZ, has laid down in the many chapters and practical examples and applications encountered in each of the chapters), that it shall be \textbf{essential to not overly complicate} the work of TEA programmers by not providing a basic mathematics or at least arithmetic evaluation facility as a first-level facility within the language. This, especially after studying the designs and implementations of several other general-purpose programming languages --- such as C, C++, Haskell, JavaScript, Python, Erlang, Go, Ruby, Lisp, etc. and finding that, even where no external libraries are loaded or required, a good and practically useful [programming] language generally must be able to at least readily allow for basic arithmetic expressions such as ``2+1", ``4/5" or even the non-trivial BODMAS\footnote{Brackets, Of, Division, Multiplication and Subtraction} expressions such as ``((2+1)*(100-1/2)*(0--1))", etc. to be reducible to their corresponding correct, terminal [numeric] expressions --- such as ``298.5" in that last case.

\vspace{2em}

It was then decided to leverage the \textbf{R} command space to make this possible --- especially because, it was necessary to ensure that such a facility ought not depend on where one is using TEA from (such as is the case with some aspects of \textbf{ZAP}), and that, the way we evaluate a mathematical expression in a TEA program needs be consistent and work the same way irrespective of where TEA is being used from (unlike what some \textit{ZAP expressions} might be like). A decision then, was made (late 2025), to use the \textbf{R.:} signature to expose a mathematical expression re-write facility to all TEA programs. 


\vspace{2em}

Also, note that, because of how many other GPLs work, it was decided that this same facility would be how we handle or evaluate boolean arithmetic or boolean algebra in TEA --- essentially, evaluating basic logical expressions such as ``$2 > 1$", ``$(1+6) == 10 - 3$", ``\verb|0 != 1-1|", etc. And concerning the case of boolean arithmetic, the results extending to re-writing expressions as either ``true" or ``false" depending on the evaluation of a pure-numeric expression\footnote{Since TEA has no usual/C-like concept of evaluating arithmetic nor boolean algebra expressions that mix literals such as numbers with variables --- that is to say, we cannot readily evaluate an expression such ``$1 + a$" or ``$1 + a == vNA$" where both ``a" and ``vNA" are some variables or in the case of TEA, perhaps names of TEA vaults.}.


\vspace{2em}

And so, we can formally specify the mathematical language that the \textbf{R.:} command space --- which we shall call the \textbf{TEA Mathematics RE-WRITE command} --- as such follows:

%draw horizontal line
\noindent\rule{\textwidth}{0.4pt}

{
\normalsize

\textbf{TEA Mathematical Expressions: Introduction}

Arithmetic, comparison, and logical expressions form the core of many programming languages, including C, Python, JavaScript and now they are also to be found in TEA. These expressions are traditionally defined using formal grammars and lexical rules, enabling precise parsing and evaluation. In TEA, which is an interpreted language, it is useful for the programmer, the user of the language, to understand what is possible and how when using this language feature then.

\vspace{1em}

So, in this brief diversion, we present a formal specification of \textbf{TEA Mathematical Expressions} (TME) using regular expressions, context-free grammars (CFGs), and automata theory, with evaluation semantics grounded in Boolean logic \cite{aho2006compilers,scott2021pragmatics,felleisen2001semantics}.

\vspace{1em}

\textbf{TEA Mathematical Expressions: Lexical Specification (Regular Expressions)}

\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Number}: \texttt{[0-9]+(\textbackslash.[0-9]+)?}
  \item \textbf{Scientific Notation Number}: such as \texttt{1e3} (1000), \texttt{.314e1} (3.14), \texttt{1.0e-3} (0.001), etc.
  \item \textbf{Arithmetic operators}: \texttt{+}, \texttt{-}, \texttt{*}, \texttt{/},   \texttt{**}
  \item \textbf{Comparison operators}: \texttt{>}, \texttt{<}, \texttt{>=}, \texttt{<=}, \texttt{==}, \texttt{!=}
  \item \textbf{Logical operators}: \texttt{\&\&}, \texttt{||}
  \item \textbf{Other operators}: \texttt{\^} (Bitwise XOR), \texttt{\_} (String concatenation)\footnote{Only valid between digits such as ``2\_1" evaluating as ``21".}
  \item \textbf{Boolean literals}: \texttt{true}, \texttt{false}
  \item \textbf{Parentheses}: \texttt{(}, \texttt{)}
\end{itemize}

\vspace{1em}

\textbf{TEA Mathematical Expressions: Syntactic Specification (Context-Free Grammar)}

\begin{align*}
\text{Expr} &\rightarrow \text{LogicExpr} \\
\text{LogicExpr} &\rightarrow \text{LogicExpr} \; \texttt{||} \; \text{LogicTerm} \mid \text{LogicTerm} \\
\text{LogicTerm} &\rightarrow \text{LogicTerm} \; \texttt{\&\&} \; \text{LogicFactor} \mid \text{LogicFactor} \\
\text{LogicFactor} &\rightarrow \texttt{!} \; \text{LogicFactor} \mid \text{Comparison} \\
\text{Comparison} &\rightarrow \text{ArithExpr} \; \text{CompOp} \; \text{ArithExpr} \mid \text{BoolLiteral}\\
\text{ArithExpr} &\rightarrow \text{Term} \; \{(+ \mid -) \; \text{Term}\}^* \\
\text{Term} &\rightarrow \text{Factor} \; \{(* \mid /) \; \text{Factor}\}^* \\
\text{Factor} &\rightarrow \text{Power} \; [\text{**{}} \; \text{Factor}] \\
\text{Power} &\rightarrow \text{Number} \mid ( \text{Expr} ) \\
\text{CompOp} &\rightarrow > \mid < \mid >= \mid <= \mid == \\
\text{BoolLiteral} &\rightarrow \texttt{true} \mid \texttt{false}
\end{align*}

This grammar supports:
\begin{itemize}
\item Arithmetic expressions: \texttt{3 + 4 * 2}
\item Comparisons: \texttt{(1 + 2)**{}3 == 27}
\item Logical combinations: \texttt{(x > 5) \&\& !(y < 3 || false)}
\end{itemize}

\vspace{1em}

\textbf{TEA Mathematical Expressions: Semantics}

\begin{itemize}
  \item Arithmetic expressions evaluate to numeric values.
  \item Comparison expressions evaluate to Boolean values (\texttt{true} or \texttt{false}).
  \item Logical expressions operate on Boolean values using short-circuit semantics.
\end{itemize}

This layered grammar also reflects the operator precedence and associativity rules found in many programming languages \cite{grune2007parsing}.

\vspace{1em}

\textbf{TEA Mathematical Expressions: Automata Perspective}

Lexical tokens are recognized by finite state machines (FSMs), while the nested structure of expressions requires a pushdown automaton (PDA) \cite{sipser2012theory}. And then, logical connectives and Boolean literals are treated as terminals and integrated into the PDA’s parsing logic. In the current TEA reference implementations though (WEB TEA: based on JavaScript and CLI TEA: based on Python3), we worry less about these technicalities since the underlying (host language) takes care of the technicalities and we only extra TME and pass these to the relevant \texttt{eval(...)} facility of the underlying host language\footnote{Unless in the future we decide or reach a point where TEA must be implemented as a direct platform on-top of say Assembly or the processor/Machine Code --- which is very unlikely, given where we envision TEA to be most useful, and given many other robust GPLs such as C\# and Ruby are themselves designed atop other language platforms, etc. --- we shall keep relying on the time-proven and stable low-level mathematics processing facilities of our host languages.}. 

\vspace{1em}

\textbf{TEA Mathematical Expressions: Conclusion}

By combining regular expressions, context-free grammars, and automata theory, we can formally specify and reason about the structure and evaluation of arithmetic, comparison, and logical expressions in TMEs, and so that, someone already familiar with how mathematical expressions work in other GPLs --- especially C-family languages (since both Python and JavaScript are somewhat C-related) shall readily be able to transfer their knowledge and experience to how TEA arithmetic and logical expressions work.

}
%draw horizontal line
\noindent\rule{\textwidth}{0.4pt}

As for where we have applied the \textbf{TMEs} via \texttt{R.:}, refer to examples such as \textbf{Personality Calculator} (\textbf{\hyperref[SECEXAMP1Q]{Section \ref{SECEXAMP1Q}}}), \textbf{Statistical Analysis Tool} (\textbf{\hyperref[SECEXAMP2N]{Section \ref{SECEXAMP2N}}}) and several other examples and applications including the one we cover in this chapter --- the \textbf{Advanced Mathematics Calculator} (\textbf{\hyperref[SECEXAMP2R]{Section \ref{SECEXAMP2R}}}).

 
 \subsection{TEA TEMPLATE Expressions --- TEA ReWRITE System}
 

In the previous sections, especially in \textbf{\hyperref[SECTME]{Section \ref{SECTME}}}, we have encountered some peculiar powers of TEA's rewriting system, such as how those that can take any valid mathematical expression (such as ``r.:$(1/2)*(1+5**(1/2))$") --- the \textbf{golden ratio} ---- and reduce it to the corresponding terminal \textbf{numeric} value. For example, we saw that invoking the following TEA:


\vspace{1em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=R: EXAMPLE 6: Using R.:, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
r.:(1/2)*(1+5**(1/2))
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

Would result in the answer ``1.618033988749895".

\vspace{2em}

However, and as might be the case in some real-life situations, one finds that they have some expression that doesn't necessarily contain only terminal symbols that a TEA Mathematical Expression expects --- that is, that, the expression contains some variables for example. We might for example have the above expression instead presented as a parameterized expression as such: ``$(1/a)*(1+5**(1/a))$" --- in which case we have replaced some of the original numbers, numeric expressions or operators with some variable --- in this case we have all instances of ``2" replaced by a variable ``a". This could make sense when the expression for example represents a formula that needs to be computed based on some externally specified value of ``a". However, currently, attempting to evaluate that expression as is, such as with the following TEA program would fail:

\vspace{1em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=R: EXAMPLE 7: Using R.:, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
r.:1+100*0.5^(1-a**(1/a))
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

One work-around might be to first perform a replacement using the usual \textbf{R:} commands such as with \texttt{R!:a:2} as shown in the following modified program:

\vspace{1em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=R: EXAMPLE 8: Using R.:, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:(1/a)*(1+5**(1/a))
r!:a:2
r.:
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

or perhaps using a vaulted expression such as with...


\vspace{1em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=R: EXAMPLE 8: Using R.:, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
v:vEXPR:{(1/a)*(1+5**(1/a))}
v:vREGEX:a
v:vSUBST:2
r*!:vEXPR:vREGEX:vSUBST
r.:
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

And that correctly gets us the original terminal value ``1.618033988749895". However, such an approach, despite being fine and functional, might create some problems when  we are dealing with more tricky expressions. For example, consider the case when our mathematical expression is mixed with some other text, perhaps descriptive text, such as in the following version of the expression...


\vspace{1em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=R: EXAMPLE 8: Using R.:, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
v:vEXPR:{The Golden Ratio is computed as: (1/a)*(1+5**(1/a))}
v:vREGEX:a
v:vSUBST:2
r*!:vEXPR:vREGEX:vSUBST
r.:
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}


Attempting to evaluate that vaulted expression --- held in vault named \texttt{vEXPR}, and whose literal value is ``The Golden Ratio is computed as: (1/a)*(1+5**(1/a))" --- fails, because after we substitute for ``a" with ``2" via the instruction \texttt{r*!:vEXPR:vREGEX:vSUBST}, we then have a non-mathematical expression: ``The Golden R2tio is computed 2s: (1/2)*(1+5**(1/2))". Clearly, that one can't be readily reduced to a simple terminal value. So, we are instead going to have to utilize what are known as \textbf{TEA Template Expressions}.


\vspace{2em}

In very basic terms, a TEA Template Expression is any text of the form...

\vspace{2em}

\begin{figure}[H]
  \centering
  \begin{tcolorbox}[myterminalstyle, title=TEA TEMPLATE Expression Format]
  \begin{lstlisting}
Any text, could even contain (1+a)*b with some #variable# or ##deepvariable## or even multiple #variable2#, etc...
  \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
  \caption{The TEA TEMPLATE Expression format}
  \label{FIGTEATEMPL}
\end{figure}


\vspace{2em}

In any such expression, all instances of \textbf{text held inside of} a single ``\#" on either side --- such as ``\#variable\#" and ``\#variable2\#" in this case --- but also those  delimited by exactly only two hash symbols, such as ``\#\#deepvariable\#\#", are known as \textbf{TEA Template variables}, with the latter kind being known as \textbf{TEA Template deep variables} or just ``deep variables".

\vspace{2em}

So, when we encounter such an expression and wish to re-write it, we shall follow the following basic two rules:

\begin{enumerate}
\item We shall attempt to replace all instances of TEA variables with their corresponding values held in the vaults of the designated or matching names. That is, for ``\#variable\#", we look for a vault named ``variable" and replace all occurrences of the TEA template variable in the template expression with the vault value we found. In case no such vault exists, the template variable is left intact in the template expression.
\item{When we encounter a TEA template deep variable such as ``\#\#meta\#\#", we then must perform two or more re-writes\footnote{It might be that we need to perform several recursions in order to finally eliminate all further nested templates in the expression of interest, however, for now, we enforce a maximum recursion depth of 20, so as to avoid problematic infinite or cyclic recursion.}
\begin{itemize}
\item First, we look for the vault with the name ``meta", and perform the usual TEA template variable re-write transformation on its contents, ensuring to dive deeper and resolve any further template variable found --- both normal variables and deep variable, so as to arrive at a final expression.
\item Next, we take the results from the above step, and use them to replace all instances of ``\#\#meta\#\#" in the original TEA template expression.
\end{itemize}
}
\end{enumerate}

\vspace{2em}

With that facility then, we can then re-approach our problematic expression above as such...

\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=R: EXAMPLE 9: Using R*!@:, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
v:vEXPR:{The Golden Ratio is computed as: (1/#a#)*(1+5**(1/#a#))}
v:a:2
r*!@:vEXPR
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

Which shall return ``The Golden Ratio is computed as: (1/2)*(1+5**(1/2))".

\vspace{2em}

And in case we wish to use deep variables, we could write that same program as such:


\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=R: EXAMPLE 10: Using R*!@:, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
v:vEXPR:{The Golden Ratio is computed as: ##innerEXPR##}
v:a:2
v:b:{(1/#a#)}
v:innerEXPR:{(1/#a#)*(1+5**#b#)}
r*!@:vEXPR
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

Which shall indeed return ``The Golden Ratio is computed as: (1/2)*(1+5**(1/2))". However, in case we wish to instead have the embedded mathematical expression evaluated as well, then we would instead write that program as such:


\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=R: EXAMPLE 11: Using R*!@:, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
v:vEXPR:{The Golden Ratio is computed as: ##vInnerVALUE##}
v:a:2
v:b:{(1/#a#)}
v:innerEXPR:{(1/#a#)*(1+5**#b#)}
r*!@:innerEXPR | r.: | v:vInnerVALUE
r*!@:vEXPR
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

Which then returns ``The Golden Ratio is computed as: 1.618033988749895". And of course, we could work without vaults as with just \texttt{R@:} as in...


\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=R: EXAMPLE 12: Using R!@:, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
v:a:2
v:b:{(1/#a#)}
i!:{(1/#a#)*(1+5**#b#)} | r@:
# OR just..
r!@:{(1/#a#)*(1+5**#b#)} 

# then evaluate inner expression first..
r.: | v:vInnerVALUE

# then do original template...
r!@:{The Golden Ratio is computed as: ##vInnerVALUE##}
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

Which also returns ``The Golden Ratio is computed as: 1.618033988749895". Thus is the queer power of TEA Templates!

\vspace{2em}

Finally, the following example should help demonstrate the differences between deep-by-default template variable resolution (such as with \texttt{R!@:}) Vs non-deep resolution (such as with \texttt{R@:}).

\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=R: EXAMPLE 13: Using R@: Vs R!@:, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
# TESTING Recursive Template Resolution
V:a:1
V:b:{(1/#a#)}
V:c:{[#b# in c]} 
V:EXPR:{#a# + #b# + #c#}
v:MSG:{I read the expression about #a# as:
 #EXPR#
and then as 
##EXPR## and #c# as ##c##
}
y:MSG | R@:

# returns:
I read the expression about 1 as:
 #a# + #b# + #c#
and then as 
1 + (1/1) + [(1/1) in c] and [#b# in c] as [(1/1) in c]

# while...
y:MSG | R!@:

# returns:
I read the expression about 1 as:
 1 + (1/1) + [(1/1) in c]
and then as 
1 + (1/1) + [(1/1) in c] and [(1/1) in c] as [(1/1) in c]
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

As a rule of thumb, when not sure when to use which, just use normal template \#variables\#, and ensure to render using deep-by-default with \texttt{R!@:} or \texttt{R*!@:vEXPR} when using vaulted templates.
 
  
\section{EXAMPLE APPLICATIONS of R:}
\label{SECEXAMPR}

  
In exploring how to leverage the \textbf{REPLACE} TEA command space, note that there are indeed many many ways this facility can be utilized, many of which are already demonstrated in several of the applications we have already covered --- such as in \textbf{Word Counter} (\textbf{\hyperref[SECEXAMP1G]{Section \ref{SECEXAMP1G}}}), \textbf{Boobs 'N' Traps} game (\textbf{\hyperref[SECEXAMP1H]{Section \ref{SECEXAMP1H}}}), \textbf{Mental Health Profiler} utility (\textbf{\hyperref[SECEXAMP1J]{Section \ref{SECEXAMP1J}}}) and others! However, in this section, we shall endavour to explore one obvious application of the text-substitution transformer --- in the form of a text-compressor, and then two non-obvious applications; one as a mathematics calculator, the other as a cryptography utility.


\subsection{EXAMPLE 1: \textbf{HUMAN SUPPORT:} SMS and TEXT Minifier Program }
\label{SECEXAMP1R}


Realize that much can be accomplished with mere text substitution operations. The application we are going to look into next, is one basic solution to the problem of compressing messages meant for SMS (Short Messages), especially so as to keep the texts short, still meaningful, and thus save on SMS/data charges. Moreover, and as one shall notice, the minifier somewhat makes the texts even more interesting to read, and especially among young people and especially internet users such as those that like to hold long conversations via instant messaging and chatting apps, this solution shall offer a compelling means to take long texts and compress them in an agreeably creative, but still meaningful way.

\vspace{2em}


 \begin{figure}[H]
 \Large
  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=TEA Program:  TEXT MINIFIER v1]
    \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
#====================================
# TEXT MINIFIER v1
#----|what it is|-------------------
# A bsc TXT/SMS mnifyr
#====================================
v:vMSG

#----|THE CONSTANTS|-------------------
v:vDELIM:{
***********************
}

t.: | f!:^$:lPROCESS:lPROMPT
l:lPROMPT | 
i!:{Welcome to TEXT MINIFIER v1} | x*!:vDELIM 
x!:{Enter a text message that you want minified:}
i*: | t.: | f!:^$:lPROCESS:lPROMPT

l:lPROCESS
v:vMSG | v:vMSG_ORIG #store original message
z: #convert all text to lower-case first

#----|THE COMPRESSORS|-------------------
#first, eliminate redudancy
r!:a+:a|r!:b+:b|r!:c+:c|r!:d+:d|r!:e+:e|r!:f+:f|
r!:g+:g|r!:h+:h|r!:i+:i|r!:j+:j|r!:k+:k|r!:l+:l|
r!:m+:m|r!:n+:n|r!:o+:o|r!:p+:p|r!:q+:q|r!:r+:r|
r!:s+:s|r!:t+:t|r!:u+:u|r!:v+:v|r!:w+:w|r!:x+:x|
r!:y+:y|r!:z+:z|r!:\s+:{ }|r!:\.+:.|r!:,+:,
r!:\?+:?|r!:\!+:!|r!:{\:+}:{:}|r!:{;+}:;

#then perform smart/creative replacements...
r!:wh:w|r!:ing:in|r!:and:n
r!:{it is}:tis|r!:you:{u}|r!:the:d
r!:to:2|r!:for:4|r!:how:hw|r!:ed:d
r!:[ ]*are[ ]:{ r }|r!:why:y|r!:ou:u
r!:[ ]be[ ]:{ b }|r!:your:yo|r!:right:8t
r!:phone:4ne|r!:ike:yk|r!:day:dy|r!:now:nw
r!:can:cn|r!:be:b|r!:love:lv
# and more as time shall inspire...

#----|PRESENT|-------------------
v:vMSG #store final compressed version
#compute compression analytics...
y!:vMSG | v:vN_MSG
y!:vMSG_ORIG | v:vN_MSG_ORIG
v:vMEASURE_EXPR:{((I-O)/(1.0*I)) * 100}
v:vI:I | v:vO:O
r*!:vMEASURE_EXPR:vI:vN_MSG_ORIG | v:vMEASURE_RESULT
r*!:vMEASURE_RESULT:vO:vN_MSG | v:vMEASURE_RESULT
r*.:vMEASURE_RESULT | d!:^.*\... | v:vMEASURE_RESULT

#Present compressed message and analytics
i!:{ORIGINAL MESSAGE:} | x*!:vDELIM | x*!:vMSG_ORIG | x*!:vDELIM 
x!:{COMPRESSED [by } | x*!:vMEASURE_RESULT | x!:{%]:} | x*!:vDELIM 
x*!:vMSG
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
  \caption{TEA APPLICATION: SMS/TEXT MINIFIER v1}
  \label{FIGR1}
\end{figure}


\vspace{2em}


So that, with that program and some sample texts, we can obtain results such as:


   \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 1 from TEXT MINIFIER]
ORIGINAL MESSAGE:
***********************
ORIGINAL MESSAGE:
***********************
Hello  World. Do you realize that the world can be made simpler than it actually is?? And, concerning ways how we might go about making this happen, well, perhaps we can start by writing less! What do you think?
***********************
COMPRESSED [by 11.37%]:
***********************
helo world. do u realize that d world cn b made simpler than it actualy is? n, concernin ways hw we might go abut makin this hapen, wel, perhaps we cn start by writin les! wat do u think?
  \end{tcbverbatim}
  
  
  Another, better compressed example...
  
  
     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 2 from TEXT MINIFIER]
ORIGINAL MESSAGE:
***********************
Hi there darling. What is the date you said you wish that we meet? I'll be in town till like Friday. Do let me know if we can meet on any of the evenings before then. Cheers! Love. ~ZEN
***********************
COMPRESSED [by 16.75%]:
***********************
hi dre darlin. wat is d date u said u wish that we met? i'l b in 2wn til lyk fridy. do let me knw if we cn met on any of d evenins b4e dn. chers! lv. ~zen
  \end{tcbverbatim}


And for a very basic case such as just the phrase "Hello World" which has some redundancy, we still get some meaningful compression nonetheless...


     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 3 from TEXT MINIFIER]
ORIGINAL MESSAGE:
***********************
Hello World
***********************
COMPRESSED [by 9.09%]:
***********************
helo world
  \end{tcbverbatim}
  
  
Thus, this program can surely be quite useful in the hands of almost anyone engaging in regular but costly text-based informal chatting and messaging as we can see in the above examples. Of course, one should note that this program returns both the original and compressed message as it is. However, we can also have a version that only returns the compressed message when needed, and such would be as such:


\vspace{2em}


{
 \Large
  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=TEA Program:  SMS-TEXT MINIFIER v3, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
#====================================
# SMS-TEXT MINIFIER v3
#----|what it is|-------------------
# A bsc TXT/SMS mnifyr
#====================================
v:vMSG | v:vMSG_ORIG

#----|THE CONSTANTS|-------------------
v:vDELIM:{
***********************
}

f!:^$:lPROCESS:lPROMPT
l:lPROMPT | 
i!:{Welcome to TEXT MINIFIER v3} | x*!:vDELIM 
x!:{Enter a text message that you want minified:}
i*: | f!:^$:lPROCESS:lPROMPT

l:lPROCESS
v:vMSG | v:vMSG_ORIG #store original message
z: #convert all text to lower-case first
t.: #truncate trailing white-space

#----|THE COMPRESSORS|-------------------
#first, eliminate redudancy
r!:a+:a|r!:b+:b|r!:c+:c|r!:d+:d|r!:e+:e|r!:f+:f|
r!:g+:g|r!:h+:h|r!:i+:i|r!:j+:j|r!:k+:k|r!:l+:l|
r!:m+:m|r!:n+:n|r!:o+:o|r!:p+:p|r!:q+:q|r!:r+:r|
r!:s+:s|r!:t+:t|r!:u+:u|r!:v+:v|r!:w+:w|r!:x+:x|
r!:y+:y|r!:z+:z|r!:\s+:{ }|r!:\.+:.|r!:,+:,
r!:\?+:?|r!:\!+:!|r!:{\:+}:{:}|r!:{;+}:;

#numbers in words with digits
r!:one:1|r!:two:2|r!:three:3|r!:four:4
r!:five:5|r!:six:6|r!:seven:7|r!:eight:8
r!:nine:9|r!:ten:10|r!:zero:0

#then perform smart/creative replacements...
r!:wh:w|r!:ing:in|r!:and:n
r!:{it is}:tis|r!:you:{u}|r!:the:d
r!:to:2|r!:for:4|r!:how:hw|r!:ed:d
r!:[ ]*are[ ]:{ r }|r!:why:y|r!:ou:u
r!:[ ]be[ ]:{ b }|r!:your:yo|r!:right:8t
r!:phone:4ne|r!:ike:yk|r!:day:dy|r!:now:nw
r!:can:cn|r!:be:b|r!:love:lv|r!:ight:8
# and more as time shall inspire...

#----|PRESENT|-------------------
v:vMSG #store final compressed version
#compute compression analytics...
y!:vMSG | v:vN_MSG
y!:vMSG_ORIG | v:vN_MSG_ORIG
v:vMEASURE_EXPR:{((I-O)/(1.0*I)) * 100}
v:vI:I | v:vO:O
r*!:vMEASURE_EXPR:vI:vN_MSG_ORIG | v:vMEASURE_RESULT
r*!:vMEASURE_RESULT:vO:vN_MSG | v:vMEASURE_RESULT
r*.:vMEASURE_RESULT | 
f!:\.:lNOTRUNC
d!:^.*\...
l:lNOTRUNC
v:vMEASURE_RESULT

#Present compressed message and analytics
i!:{ORIGINAL MESSAGE:} | x*!:vDELIM | x*!:vMSG_ORIG | x*!:vDELIM 
x!:{COMPRESSED [by } | x*!:vMEASURE_RESULT | x!:{%]:} | x*!:vDELIM 
x*!:vMSG | v:vFIN_MSG_WITH_ANALYTICS

i!:{TEXT MINIFIER: processing message is complete | Choose what to do:
[1] Return Final Message Only | [else] RESULTS + Analysis} | i*: | t.:
f:^1$:lNOANALYSIS

y:vFIN_MSG_WITH_ANALYTICS | q!: #show original msg+analysis+final message

l:lNOANALYSIS #only show final compressed message
y:vMSG
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
  \caption{TEA APPLICATION: SMS/TEXT MINIFIER v3}
  \label{FIGR2}
}


\vspace{2em}

When used in the original mode, it would produce results such as:


     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 4 from TEXT MINIFIER v3]
ORIGINAL MESSAGE:
***********************
this is the rhythm of the night
***********************
COMPRESSED [by 22.58%]:
***********************
this is d rhythm of d n8
  \end{tcbverbatim}


\vspace{2em}

     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 4 from TEXT MINIFIER v3]
ORIGINAL MESSAGE:
***********************
    T
***********************
COMPRESSED [by 80%]:
***********************
t
  \end{tcbverbatim}


\vspace{2em}


While, when used in the ``result-only" mode, it would produce output such as just

\vspace{2em}

     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 4 from TEXT MINIFIER]
this is d oficial web integratd development environment 4 d trans4min executable alphabt computer programin language.
  \end{tcbverbatim}
  
  
 \vspace{2em}
 
 
 Thus, this simple application can really come in handy, and can be used both via the web, but also in pure offline mode via the commandline. Note that, for quick testing, access it via the TEA standard programs, or via the links:
 
 
 \vspace{1em}

 \url{https://tea.nuchwezi.com/?i=your+text+message+here&fc=https://gist.githubusercontent.com/mcnemesis/648d348ce61eb5765421e8fb434c1b19/raw/sms_text_minifier.tea}

\vspace{1em} 

OR

\vspace{1em} 

 \url{https://bit.ly/text2minisms}


\subsection{EXAMPLE 2: \textbf{MATHEMATICS:} ADVANCED MATHEMATICS COMPUTER}
\label{SECEXAMP2R}


Having looked at what kinds of mathematics we can accomplish using TEA's mathematical \textbf{REWRITE} instruction, \texttt{r.:} --- refer to \textbf{\hyperref[SECTME]{Section \ref{SECTME}}}, we can then proceed to look at a basic general-purpose application that can help anyone quickly evaluate basic to advanced mathematical expressions in a very simple and direct way, via an interactive interface. We shall refer to this program as the \textbf{Advanced Mathematics Computer} (AMC), and the version 3.1 of this utility is as presented hereafter...



\vspace{2em}


{
 \Large
  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=TEA Program:  Advanced Mathematics Computer v3.1, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
#!/usr/bin/tttt -fc
v:vDESCRIPTION:{
###############################
# ADVANCED MATHEMATICS COMPUTER: 
# AMC v3.1
###############################
This program is meant to help you quickly reduce mathematical expressions to their numerical or boolean values. You might also think of it as a simple advanced calculator.}

#------[ DEFINE CONSTANTS ]
v:vDELIM:{
#####################
}
v:vPROMPT:{INPUT: [Q] QUIT | [H] HELP | Mathematical Expression for AMC to compute:}
v:vEXP_PREFIX:{You entered:}
v:vEXP_SUFFIX:{AMC Evaluated it as: }
v:vTEA_MATH_EXPRESSIONS:{
AMC expects and correctly evaluates ONLY legitimate TEA Mathematical Expressions:
#####################
-> Numbers: 0,1,2,...,9,... including fractions and signed numbers
-> Scientific Notation Numbers: such as 1e3 (1000), .314e1 (3.14), 1e-3 (0.001), etc.
-> Arithmetic Operators: + (Addition), - (Subtraction), * (Multiplication), / (Division), ,  ** (Exponentiation), % (Modulus)
-> Comparison Operators: > (Greater than), < (Less than), >= (Greater or Equal), <= (Less or Equal), == (Equivalent), != (Not Equivalent)
-> Logical Operators: && (And), || (Or)
-> Other Operators: ^ (Bitwise XOR), _ (Number Concatenation)
-> Boolean Literals: true, false
-> Parentheses: ( and )
-> Last Answer Variables: answer, ans, a (such as answer+1)
#####################
If you know what you are doing, you can use those operators and numbers to evaluate basic and complex mathematical expressions of many kinds, via AMC.
}
v:vNL:{
}
v:vREGEX_ANS:{answer|ans|a} #to match answer variable in TMEs

#------[ DEFINE VARIABLES ]
v:vREPORT:{-|AMC Evaluated Nothing Yet|-}
v:vANSWER_ORIG:{0} # hold last evaluated answer value

#------[ Get Input ]
v:vINPUT #store any external input originally provided
# show the welcome message
y:vDESCRIPTION | i*:
# then prompt for input if blank...
y:vINPUT | t.: | v:vINPUT | f:^$:lPROMPT:lCOMPUTE

l:lHELP
y:vTEA_MATH_EXPRESSIONS | i*: #show AMC TME expression guidelines..

l:lPROMPT
y:vPROMPT | x*:vNL | x*:vDELIM | x*:vREPORT | i*: | v:vINPUT | t.: | f:^$:lPROMPT:lCOMPUTE

l:lCOMPUTE
f:^[qQ]$:lFINISH
f:^[hH]$:lHELP
y:vINPUT 
r*!:vINPUT:vREGEX_ANS:vANSWER_ORIG #overwrite answer variable
r.: | v:vANSWER | v:vANSWER_ORIG #evaluate TME and store answer

#------[ Present Results ]
y:vEXP_PREFIX | x*!:vDELIM | x*!:vINPUT | x*!:vDELIM | x*!:vEXP_SUFFIX | x*!:vANSWER | v:vREPORT | i*:

# done processing, prompt for next task...
j:lPROMPT

#------[ QUIT ]
l:lFINISH
y:vREPORT # show last report and quit
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
  \caption{TEA APPLICATION: Advanced Mathematics Computer v3.1}
  \label{FIGR3}
}


And so that, having that program and some tricky mathematical expressions that one needs to evalute, there shall be no need to guess or waste time, but instead just load AMC and compute their corresponding values as shown in the following examples:

\vspace{2em}

     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 1 from AMC v3.1]
You entered:
##########################
3.1415*(101)**2
##########################
AMC Evaluated it as: 32046.4415
  \end{tcbverbatim}


\vspace{2em}

Where that example shows us how to correctly compute the area of a circle whose radius is \texttt{101}. More examples shall help us appreciate this facility even more...

\vspace{2em}

     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 2 from AMC v3.1]
You entered:
##########################
0.5*(1 + 5**(1/2))
##########################
AMC Evaluated it as: 1.618033988749895
  \end{tcbverbatim}


\vspace{2em}


That last output shows that we can use AMC to compute \textbf{square-roots} or n-th roots even though we don't have a square-root operation supported, simply by using the idea of $\sqrt{5} = 5^\frac{1}{2}$ for example. And so, we see here that AMC can correctly compute the value of the \textbf{golden ratio} using the expression we have provided as shown above, and that is is \textbf{correct to 14 decimal places!}


\vspace{2em}

     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 3 from AMC v3.1]
You entered:
##########################
(5-2)*(5+3)
##########################
AMC Evaluated it as: 24
  \end{tcbverbatim}


\vspace{1em}


     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 4 from AMC v3.1]
You entered:
##########################
(5-2)*5+3
##########################
AMC Evaluated it as: 18
  \end{tcbverbatim}


\vspace{1em}

\vspace{1em}


     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 5 from AMC v3.1]
You entered:
##########################
5-2*5+3
##########################
AMC Evaluated it as: -2
  \end{tcbverbatim}

And then those last three outputs should help to demonstrate that indeed, we can trust AMC to be doing the right thing when we use complex expressions --- such as when we either use expressions containing terms in brackets Vs expressions without brackets. AMC respects standard mathematical operator precedence such as \textbf{BODMAS}, and shall correctly compute the legitimate value as we see in the three variations of trying to compute the \textbf{y-axis} value for a quadratic function with the equation $y = (x -2)(x+3) = x^2 + x - 6$, and so that, as we see in the first case, when $x=5$, and we try to evaluate the expression having substituted ``5" for $x$, we would correctly get the $y$ value as \texttt{24}. However, in the other two expressions omitting some or all of the brackets, the expression would indeed be wrong, and so the values AMC gives us, despite being correct for the given expressions, might not be what the person wanted. 

\vspace{2em}

And so, for someone that knows what they are doing, we see that TEA's mathematical evaluation instruction \textbf{R.:EXPR} shall always get us the legitimate answers in simplified for, for any arguments (\texttt{EXPR}), that we pass it as long as they are the correct expressions we intend to evaluate, and if they adhere to the expected TEA Mathematical Expression syntax.

\vspace{2em}

The last example output we shall share, concerns using TEA and particularly, this AMC application, to evaluate the correctness of logical comparisons or logical expressions --- which we expect to evaluate not to numbers as would be the case for arithmetic expressions, but instead to evaluate to boolean values. The following example demonstrate this facility, when we try to test whether one non-trivial arithmetic expression correctly reduces to the number ``5".

\vspace{2em}


     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 6 from AMC v3.1]
You entered:
##########################
5==(5e-1 * 100/10.0)
##########################
AMC Evaluated it as: true
  \end{tcbverbatim}

\vspace{1em}

And so, as we can indeed tell from that evaluation result, AMC is doing just the right thing!

\vspace{2em}


As a final illustration of the powers of this mathematics calculator, note that AMC also presents a feature typically found in all advanced programmable calculators: \textbf{allowing the result of the last computation to be used in the new/next expression via a variable name}. For AMC, this is via any of the variable names: \texttt{answer}, \texttt{ans} or \texttt{a}. So that, if one started out by evaluating the expression \texttt{300+10**2} --- which would result in \texttt{400}, and then for the immediate next expression, they type in an expression such as \texttt{answer * (1 - 0.5)}, then the result would indeed correctly evaluate as though the expression \texttt{400 * (1 - 0.5)} was the one evaluated, as you can tell from the following final AMC dump:


\vspace{2em}


     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 7 from AMC v3.1]
You entered:
#####################
answer * (1 - 0.5)
#####################
AMC Evaluated it as: 200
  \end{tcbverbatim}

\vspace{1em}


So, this program might be studied further, enhanced or applied by those interested, via the following links:


 \vspace{1em}

 \url{https://tea.nuchwezi.com/?i=2**5&fc=https://gist.githubusercontent.com/mcnemesis/0ae84d3bcb5a427dea869395a1278984/raw/advanced_mathematics_computer.tea}

\vspace{1em} 

OR

\vspace{1em} 

 \url{https://bit.ly/amcomputer}



\subsection{EXAMPLE 3: \textbf{CRYPTOGRAPHY:} INVISIBLE TEXT MESSAGES --- The GHOST Message Protocol }
\label{SECEXAMP3R}


We have already talked about the immense powers of rewriting systems such as TEA \textbf{REPLACE command space} in earlier sections of this chapter, however, perhaps the following example shall help to cement the potency and sheer magical power a simple replace command such as \texttt{r!:PATTERN:SUBSTITUTE} can have in communication, security, art and fantasy, when wielded properly and creatively so. However, before we dive into this special application, first, let's reflect upon two interesting quotes taken from two entirely different kinds of works --- both books hand-picked from the author's private book collection at Nuchwezi Research --- one, about hardcore science dealing with the material side of the living and the dead especially in the occident, the other, a significantly timeless contribution to the philosophical and spiritual heritage of the orient respectively:


\vspace{2em}

\begin{quotation}
\noindent \texttt{Bodies can be buried in graves, left on the ground, hidden in buildings, or burned and mutilated beyond any normal level of recognition. The job of forensics is to establish, beyond a shadow of a doubt, facts that are not obvious to the human eye...}\\
\hspace*{\fill} ---\textbf{Roxana Ferllini}, \textit{Raising the Dead: How the Forensics of the Future Are Solving the Murders of the Past}, 2003\cite{Ferllini2003}
\end{quotation}


\vspace{2em}

\begin{quotation}
\noindent \texttt{When body and mind dissolve,}\\\texttt{They do not exist anywhere,}\\\texttt{Any more than musical notes lay heaped up anywhere.}\\\texttt{All the elements of being come into existence}\\\texttt{After having been non-existent;}\\\texttt{And having come into existence pass away.}\\
\hspace*{\fill} ---\textbf{Jon Ortner}, \textit{Buddha}, 2003\cite{Ortner2003}
\end{quotation}


\vspace{2em}

Moreover, even though our work here is going to deal with a kind of writing system that specifically deals with trying to read ``the invisible" --- such as ghosts are typically believed to be --- and yet, as with the following definition given for the term \textbf{ghost} from one popular Windows desktop dictionary software, we shall somewhat be actually dealing with information that, despite being ``ordinarily invisible", is still somewhat visible when processed with ``a special kind of eye".



\vspace{2em}

\begin{quotation}
\noindent \textbf{Noun: ghost}\\\texttt{The visible disembodied soul of a dead person.}\\
\hspace*{\fill} ---\textbf{Antony Lewis and WordWeb Software}, \textit{WordWeb 10.51}, 2025\cite{WordWeb2025}
\end{quotation}


\vspace{2em}


\subsubsection{Brief Background on Ghost Message Software}
\label{SECBACKGHOSTMSG}

It is worth noting that, even though the new/modern Ghost Message implementation we are about to dive into somewhat technically deviates from the original idea that the author of this book, and also a researcher at Nuchwezi explored as early as in 2016\footnote{See original \textbf{Ghost Messages} on CodePen in HTML and JavaScript: \url{https://codepen.io/mcnemesis/pen/zqBmdL}} --- a live demonstration of that software still active at \url{https://ghost.nuchwezi.com/} --- and yet, the key differences between that and the new implementation is as such:

\vspace{2em}
\begin{enumerate}
\item The original focused on the idea of encoding ``hidden messages" in plain sight; meaning, an entirely different [and literal] text message could be encoded using ordinary visible text that totally has nothing to do with the hidden messages. For example, one could write poem about birds singing in trees, mentioning nothing at all about computers or mathematics, in such a way that when the sentences and words in the poem are processed using that ghost messages algorithm, one instead discovers encoded in the poem, a mathematical formula for computing logarithms for example!
\item{ Also, the technique of encoding in the original ghost messages protocol utilized a mapping between \textbf{number of words in a sentence} --- with a sentence having to be a sequence of space-delimited strings ending with a full-stop or question-mark --- and the index of a character in the alphabet. Thus, two different text sentences could both encode the same exact letter or phrase, such as ``hi." and "you." both encoding a single space `` ", and that ``hi there." and ``loving you." merely encoding an ``a", while a poem such as:

\vspace{1em}
{\ttfamily
\begin{verbatim}
Have you seen?
It's such a sleuth.
Hand OMG? No?
I think so, but meh..
\end{verbatim}
}
\vspace{1em}

Merely encodes the message ``bca d" --- perhaps about the BCA (Blackboard Adventures\footnote{See \url{https://t.me/bclectures}}) or about the \textit{vampiric} ``Bacardi" rum-liqueur\footnote{See \url{https://bacardi.com}}??

}
\end{enumerate}

\vspace{2em}


Anyways, let's move on to the new Ghost Messages protocol and implementation...

\subsubsection{Ghost Messages Protocol}

In the new Ghost Messages Protocol, the most important specifications are these:

\begin{enumerate}
\item We want to be able to encode almost any kind of normal text message --- including natural language with any kind of common punctuation; mathematical expressions including numbers, operators and brackets; computer source-code including use of common delimiters, mixed-case identifiers and even comments, etc!\footnote{Note that, the original Ghost Messages only could handle a limited alphabet of only lowercase letters a-z, the 10 digits and the space character.}
\item \textbf{The Ghost Message} should be a text file which, when opened and read using normal, unaided eyes, merely appears entirely empty, almost empty/blank or just meaningless!\footnote{We say ``meaningless" because, in case there are characters that we can't yet encode, perhaps we merely leave them to survive in the final GM-encoded message verbatim. This might produce files where some few symbols float about surrounded by just vast blank space!}
\item When the GM-encoded message is processed so as to decode or read it, the result should be as true to the original as possible --- meaning, for critical text files such as computer programs or mathematical expressions, we want the ``invisible text" to reproduce the same precision and correctness in the original file --- with numbers in the correct order, decimals and commas in the right place, new lines preserved, brackets, lower and upper-case letters and comments all well-preserved, etc.
\end{enumerate}

\vspace{2em}

Thus, this protocol and the associated software might not be trivial in any sense, and might need to undergo thorough testing to confirm and prove that we have truly adhered to the above specification, however, and thanks to perhaps divine inspiration\footnote{The author does indeed occasionally pray to, and invoke the \textit{Holy Ghost} --- with the current v1 of GM having been prototypes using a mere smartphone, and in bed, relaxing on a boring Sunday afternoon after prayers earlier on in the day...}, we have the following implementation in the TEA language..


\subsubsection{Ghost Messages TEA Source Code}


\vspace{2em}
{
 \Large
  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=TEA Program:  GHOST Messages Protocol v1.1, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]    
v:vABOUT:{#+##################+#
Ghost Messages Protocol
Encoder-N-Decoder v1.1
---------------------
Imagine coming across a [BLANK] text file full of "nothing" but space!
It's like looking at a ghost.. normal eyes world see nothing!! 
This program, helps you do that for any text message; re-write it 
using nothing but space (what we call the Ghost Message code). 
But also it can do the reverse.. help you read messages that are invisible to those without this superpower.
#+##################+#
Original idea started in the 2010s at Nuchwezi Research.
This is the TEA version, by Fut. Prof. Joseph Willrich Lutalo
#+##################+#}

---[INTRO]
y:vABOUT | i:

#the GM ordered alphabet sequence for reference...
#also includes Newline char, and # should always come last!
v:az:{abcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyz 0123456789.?*/+-()
!@:{}|[]ABCDEFGHIJKLMNOPQRSTUVWXYZ#} 

#---[Menu]
y*:|v:vIN
l:lPROMPT
y:vIN|f!:^$:lMENU 
i!:{Enter Message to Process:}|i:|v:vIN
j:lPROMPT

l:lMENU
i!:{Choose what to do:
1. Make Message Invisible
2. Read Invisible Message}|i:|v:vOPT
f!:^[12]:lMENU:lPROCESS

l:lPROCESS
#first store msg..
y:vIN|v:vMESSAGE 
#go process..
y:vOPT|f:1:lENCODER:lDECODER

#---[encoder]
l:lENCODER
y:vMESSAGE

# first, we deal with special char #
r!:{#}:{#################################################################################_}

# alpha..
r!:a:{#_}
r!:b:{##_}
r!:c:{###_}
r!:d:{####_}
r!:e:{#####_}
r!:f:{######_}
r!:g:{#######_}
r!:h:{########_}
r!:i:{#########_}
r!:j:{##########_}
r!:k:{###########_}
r!:l:{############_}
r!:m:{#############_}
r!:n:{##############_}
r!:o:{###############_}
r!:p:{################_}
r!:q:{#################_}
r!:r:{##################_}
r!:s:{###################_}
r!:t:{####################_}
r!:u:{#####################_}
r!:v:{######################_}
r!:w:{#######################_}
r!:x:{########################_}
r!:y:{#########################_}
r!:z:{##########################_}
r!:{ }:{###########################_}
# numerals...
r!:{0}:{############################_}
r!:{1}:{#############################_}
r!:{2}:{##############################_}
r!:{3}:{###############################_}
r!:{4}:{################################_}
r!:{5}:{#################################_}
r!:{6}:{##################################_}
r!:{7}:{###################################_}
r!:{8}:{####################################_}
r!:{9}:{#####################################_}
# symbols..
r!:{\.}:{######################################_}
r!:{\?}:{#######################################_}
r!:{\*}:{########################################_}
r!:{/}:{#########################################_}
r!:{\+}:{##########################################_}
r!:{-}:{###########################################_}
r!:{\(}:{############################################_}
r!:{\)}:{#############################################_}
r!:{\n}:{##############################################_}
r!:{!}:{###############################################_}
r!:{@}:{################################################_}
r!:{:}:{#################################################_}
r!:{\{}:{##################################################_}
r!:{\}}:{###################################################_}
r!:{\|}:{####################################################_}
r!:{\[}:{#####################################################_}
r!:{\]}:{######################################################_}

# UPPER ALPHA...
r!:{A}:{#######################################################_}
r!:{B}:{########################################################_}
r!:{C}:{#########################################################_}
r!:{D}:{##########################################################_}
r!:{E}:{###########################################################_}
r!:{F}:{############################################################_}
r!:{G}:{#############################################################_}
r!:{H}:{##############################################################_}
r!:{I}:{###############################################################_}
r!:{J}:{################################################################_}
r!:{K}:{#################################################################_}
r!:{L}:{##################################################################_}
r!:{M}:{###################################################################_}
r!:{N}:{####################################################################_}
r!:{O}:{#####################################################################_}
r!:{P}:{######################################################################_}
r!:{Q}:{#######################################################################_}
r!:{R}:{########################################################################_}
r!:{S}:{#########################################################################_}
r!:{T}:{##########################################################################_}
r!:{U}:{###########################################################################_}
r!:{V}:{############################################################################_}
r!:{W}:{#############################################################################_}
r!:{X}:{##############################################################################_}
r!:{Y}:{###############################################################################_}
r!:{Z}:{################################################################################_}

# handling special char # skipped here to prevent bug
#r!:{#}:{#################################################################################_}


#eliminate what's not encoded...
d!:{[#_]}
#then unmask..
r!:{#}:{ }
r!:{_}:{
}
#------------'_..!------------------------|
#NOTE: because copying pure space/ghost-messages
#might be denied, we pre+suffix a single dot.
#------------'_..!------------------------|
x:.|x!:.

# and that's it!
q!:
#---[DONE ENCODING]

#---[decoder]
l:lDECODER
y:vMESSAGE

# for GM, strip .-affixes
d:{^\.}|m!:|d:{^\.}|m!:

r!:{\n}:{_}
r!:{ }:{#}

# handle special char # first..
r!:{#################################################################################_}:{-HASH-} 

# UPPER ALPHA
r!:{################################################################################_}:{Z}
r!:{###############################################################################_}:{Y}
r!:{##############################################################################_}:{X}
r!:{#############################################################################_}:{W}
r!:{############################################################################_}:{V}
r!:{###########################################################################_}:{U}
r!:{##########################################################################_}:{T}

r!:{##################################################################_}:{L}
r!:{#################################################################_}:{K}
r!:{################################################################_}:{J}
r!:{###############################################################_}:{I}
r!:{##############################################################_}:{H}
r!:{#############################################################_}:{G}
r!:{############################################################_}:{F}

# symbols..
r!:{######################################################_}:]

r!:{######################################_}:.
# numerals
r!:{#####################################_}:9

r!:{##_}:b

r!:{-HASH-}:{#}

#------------'_..!------------------------|
# NOTE: in traditional nomenclature
# wht we've just done wld be akin to
# clairvoyance! the stuff o ghost hunters!
#------------'_..!------------------------|
#---[DONE DECODING]
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
  \caption{TEA APPLICATION: CRYPTOGRAPHY: GHOST Messages Protocol v1.1}
  \label{FIGR3}
}


\vspace{2em}

That program's source-code contains some ``abnormally" and ``unavoidably" long instruction lines that even though we might later be able to simplify them so as to make the code able to fit nicely on a page or screen such as most other TEA programs would (especially in their \textbf{sanitized form}), and yet, that version is written as such, to help especially highlight the intricacies of the underlying Ghost Message protocol encoding and decoding logic when implemented in straight-forward TEA. This program should really help one to come to truly appreciate and love TEA (as we shall see when we come to the examples under the next heading), however, there is no doubt, given this is just the version 1.1 prototype, that a future TEA version that is more terse, perhaps less trivial and more cryptic shall manifest, most likely based-off of this current implementation and specification.

\vspace{2em}

That said, and with the examples we are about to look at, one should note that, unlike the original/older Ghost Messages, the new version of the protocol somewhat requires that the messages be held or passed around in electronic text-files --- this, especially because, without a special way to encode different kinds of white-space characters on a literal paper (such as a pure new ghost-messages file printed on a blank A4 sheet), when scanned back with a computer or read using the eyes, shall merely appear just \textbf{blank}! Thus, the new protocol, or in its currently form, is meant for use on electronic devices --- on social media, in emails, in digital file attachments, etc. But those still interested in ghost messages using pen-and-paper\footnote{Such as the stuff of legendary computer scientist, also a renown grandfather of modern computer-aided cryptography, \textbf{Alan Turing}, a great inspiration to the inventor of TEA, and who without doubt, had much influence on him as depicted in the World War 2 movie --- \textbf{The Imitation Game (2014)} --- movie came out the same year Nuchwezi was founded!}, can of course revert to the original idea introduced in \textbf{\hyperref[SECBACKGHOSTMSG]{Section \ref{SECBACKGHOSTMSG}}} or improve on that.


\subsubsection{Testing the Ghost Messages TEA application}


Let us begin with a simple  text message such as just the common greeting in computer science --- ``hello world". When one encodes that message using the GM v1.1 TEA program, we obtain the following verbatim ghost message:


\vspace{2em}


     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 1 from GM v1.1 --- for ``hello world"]
.        
     
            
            
               
                           
                       
               
                  
            
    
.
  \end{tcbverbatim}

\vspace{1em}


And if we slightly modify that message so as to introduce some complications such as adding punctuation where the original didn't, introducing some numbers, mixing lower and upper-case letters, etc. We then have the following ghost message for the literal text ``Hello? 10.1 World!"



\vspace{2em}


     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 2 from GM v1.1 --- for ``Hello? 10.1 World!"]
.                                                              
     
            
            
               
                                       
                           
                             
                            
                                      
                             
                           
                                                                             
               
                  
            
    
                                               
.
  \end{tcbverbatim}

\vspace{1em}


At this juncture then, and \textbf{IMPORTANT} to note: one might wonder, \textbf{why are we having some ``visible" characters in our ghost message?} Well, this is for example demonstrated by the fact that the above two ghost messages both start and end with a single dot ``." character --- essentially, and opening and closing ``full-stop". The simple explanation for why this is so, is such that, after experimenting with the Ghost Messages Protocol via the TEA WEB IDE both on mobile and desktop computers, we found that some browsers or environments would not allow to copy and paste files which entirely contain only invisible characters or just white-space! Thus, as a work-around this, and somewhat in recognition of the original ghost-messages protocol, we chose to open and close the modern ghost-messages with \textbf{an optional single ``."}. This then means, when working on a ghost-messages text message held in a digital file or which one is sure to pass to the GM processor verbatim, one might decide to entirely strip out the opening and closing dots, so that, \textbf{the last message above} would be just as the following...

\vspace{2em}
     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample pure GHOST-MESSAGE from GM v1.1 --- for ``Hello? 10.1 World!"]
                                                              
     
            
            
               
                                       
                           
                             
                            
                                      
                             
                           
                                                                             
               
                  
            
    
                                               

  \end{tcbverbatim}

\vspace{1em}


One can appreciate how great this protocol is, by looking at the following screenshots of that message when seen in the Notepad text-editor on windows...

\subsubsection{UPDATED Ghost Messages Protocol}


Having realized that this application is in so much demand, and that it surely fills a gap no other existing software does, we have enhanced and updated it to \textbf{Ghost Messages v1.3} whose source-code one can find via the links shared above, and which extended version, especially features a longer \textbf{symbol set} spanning nearly all the important/common ASCII character symbols as shown here...


\vspace{2em}

\vspace{1em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=EXTENDED Ghost Messages Symbol Set, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
#the GM ordered alphabet sequence for reference...
#also includes Newline char, and # should always come last!
v:az:{abcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyz 0123456789.?*/+-()
!@:{}|[]ABCDEFGHIJKLMNOPQRSTUVWXYZ,"';&@%=^$~<>\_#}
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}


To appreciate just how robust and effective this program is, note that, it currently can encode (make invisible) but also decode (return from invisible to visible), special structured texts of the following kinds...

\vspace{2em}


\vspace{2em}


     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample INPUTS handled by Ghost Messages: C-Programs]
#include <stdio.h>
#include <stdbool.h>

// Function to check if a number is prime
bool isPrime(int num) {
    if (num < 2) return false;
    for (int i = 2; i * i <= num; i++) {
        if (num % i == 0) return false;
    }
    return true;
}

int main() {
    int n, count = 0, num = 1, nthPrime;

    printf("Enter n (to find the nth prime): ");
    scanf("%d", &n);

    while (count < n) {
        num++;
        if (isPrime(num)) {
            count++;
        }
    }

    nthPrime = num;
    printf("The %dth prime number is %d\n", n, nthPrime);

    return 0;
}

  \end{tcbverbatim}

\vspace{1em}


     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample INPUTS handled by Ghost Messages: Python-Programs]
def is_prime(num):
    if num < 2:
        return False
    for i in range(2, int(num**0.5) + 1):
        if num % i == 0:
            return False
    return True

def nth_prime(n):
    count = 0
    num = 1
    while count < n:
        num += 1
        if is_prime(num):
            count += 1
    return num

# Example usage
n = int(input("Enter n (to find the nth prime): "))
print(f"The {n}th prime number is {nth_prime(n)}")
  \end{tcbverbatim}

\vspace{1em}

name,radius_km,mass_kg,distanceFromSun_million_km

     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample INPUTS handled by Ghost Messages: TEA Programs!]
#############################################
# RANDOM VOWEL HYMN GENERATOR v1
#############################################
# Though legit vowel hymns ought be generated
# based on transforming the text of legit hymns
# or words, this generator instead generates 
#such hymns based on nothing really, but the
# outputs shall read and feel like legitimate
#vowel hymns nonetheless.
#-------------------------------------------
# For Theory: https://bit.ly/grimoirelumtauto
# For Example: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=p_THWo4MjIU
##############################################
v:vLINES:5 | v:vI:0 | v:vHYMN:{}
v:vLIMIT:10 | v:vGLUE:{ } | v:vALPHABET:{aeiou }
l:LOOP
p*!:vLIMIT:vGLUE:vALPHABET | v:vLINE #generate and store random string
g*:{
}:vHYMN:vLINE | v:vHYMN #add it to the hymn
y:vI | x!:{+1} | r.: | v:vI 
y:vI | x!:{>} | x*!:vLINES | r.: | f:^true$:FIN:LOOP #test if we must finish
l:FIN | y:vHYMN | k!:^$ #return clean hymn
  \end{tcbverbatim}


\vspace{2em}

I mean, just considering how vast the kinds of text --- structured or not, that we can process correctly using this \textit{weird encryption} just is daunting! Test and see for yourself: \url{https://bit.ly/use_ghost_messages}

\vspace{2em}

On the TEA Project Repository\footnote{See \url{https://github.com/mcnemesis/cli_tttt}} we have also included --- under the path:

\vspace{1em}
\texttt{sample\_TEA\_programs/cryptography/ghost\_messages\_protocol/test\_cases/}
\vspace{1em}
 
several example complete non-trivial input files that we have used to test both the encoding and decoding capabilities of this protocol, and it becomes clear... one can surely now share a complex, high-secret computer program (perhaps a virus or commercial algorithm?) as a securely encoded ghost message in say an email attachment, over social media, etc. And when the uninitiated open or try to process it, they'll basically see... \textit{emptiness!} Thus, one can use it to handle not just normal text writings (poetry, essays, letters, etc) but also C programs, Python code, JSON data, CSV, TEA program, numeric sequences, LaTeX code, arithmetic and algebraic expressions, etc. 

\vspace{2em}

Plus, the seasoned TEA user or programmer shouldn't find much difficulty in extending or customizing the GM protocol program to cater for their special kind of messages in case it doesn't already handle their particular message alphabets and or use case\footnote{As it currently is (as of \textbf{Ghost Messages v1.3}), in case one tries to make an input file invisible, and there were some characters the protocol couldn't handle, they shall merely stay visible in the final output so that one clearly knows that the protocol didn't handle some input values. This, of course ignoring the two mandatory dots; one at the start, and the other at the end of the output when the GM is used as is --- don't forget that these can be stripped out to make the output entirely invisible. GM shall be able to handle such input nonetheless.}.
