% TAZ SECTION: S
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\chapter{S: SALT}
\label{SECS}


\begin{table}[H]
  \centering
  \LARGE
	\begin{tabular}[t]{|p{0.2\textwidth}|p{0.5\textwidth}}
 
	\textbf{NAME} & \texttt{SALT}\\
	\hline
	\textbf{Purpose} & \begin{enumerate}
	\item Randomly Inject Space into strings
	\item Randomly Delete Characters from strings
	\item Randomly Injecting some Text into a string
	\item Randomly Deleting some Pattern from a string
	\end{enumerate}\\
	\hline
	              
\end{tabular}
\caption{General Objectives of TEA Primitive \textbf{S:}}
  \label{TABTAZS}
\end{table}


\section{SEMANTICS of S:}
\label{SECSEMS}


\begin{table}[H]
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3} % Optional: increases row height
\rowcolors{1}{lightgray}{white}   % Alternating row colors
\begin{tabular}{>{\bfseries}m{0.4\linewidth} | m{0.6\linewidth}} % 2 columns, 
\rowcolor{white}
\textbf{\makecell[l]{INSTRUCTION\\ SIGNATURE}} & \textbf{INSTRUCTION FUNCTION} \\
\hline

s: & Inject a SINGLE SPACE CHARACTER into AI at a random position. Salting the AI.\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{s:SALT:N:N2\\s:SALT:LIMIT:LLIMIT} & Return AI with the string SALT injected into it at some random Position. With N specified, injects at an index position (from 0) not greater than $N$ within SALT, injecting anywhere if $N > |AI|$. To precisely control where to operate, use $N2$, as a number to control the lower limit for the index search, so that, when $N = N2$, it basically means to precisely operate at index $N$, otherwise one can control the operating space more literally. Thus $N$ and $N2$ should be picked such that $N2 \leq N$.\\

s!: & Delete a SINGLE CHARACTER from AI at a random position. Unsalting AI.\\


\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{s!:REGEX:N:N2} & Randomly select one of the sections in AI matching REGEX, and delete it. With $N$ specified, only deletes an occurrence not later than the $N$th-occurrence of REGEX within AI, with $N2$, operates on the range between $N2$ and $N$, operating on exactly the Nth occurrence if $N=N2$. Thus $N$ and $N2$ should be picked such that $N2 \leq N$.\\


\makecell[l]{s*:vNAME:vSALT:vN:vN2} & Same as s:SALT:N:N2, but operating on the string stored in the vault with the name vNAME  instead of AI, and using string stored in vSALT as the salt. All parameters are read from vaults with the specified names. With only vNAME specified, operates like \texttt{s:} on \texttt{vNAME}.\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{s*!:vNAME:vREGEX:vN:vN2} & Same as s!:REGEX:N:N2, but operating on the string stored in the vault with the name vNAME  instead of AI.  All parameters are read from vaults with the specified names. With only vNAME specified, operates like \texttt{s!:} on \texttt{vNAME}.\\

 \hline
\end{tabular}
\caption{The Semantics of S:}
\label{TABSEMS}
\end{table}


\section{NOTES about S:}
\label{SECNOTES}
  
First, note that salting strings is not the same as substitution. In salting, the original string contents remain, but new content is injected at random (default) or at specified positions within the string. Unsalting on the other hand, leaves the original string with some sections of it randomly deleted. 


\vspace{2em}


Some examples follow. The first example demonstrates using salting without any parameters or arguments. Essentially, given some input, \texttt{s:} shall merely inject the single space at some point within, before or after the string. For illustration, we shall just use the basic string ``TEST".

\vspace{1em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=S: EXAMPLE 1: Salting using S:, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:TEST|s: # shall sometimes return "TE ST", " TEST", "T EST", etc.
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

The next example still demonstrates salting, but involves passing some argument to the salt command, as in \texttt{s:SALT}, so that the explicit string ``SALT" is injected into a random position in the AI --- and in the example, we still set the AI as ``TEST", and so that, where we were injecting the space character in the first example, now we shall inject something the command is invoked with as the parameter...

\vspace{1em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=S: EXAMPLE 2: Salting using S:SALT, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:TEST|s:__ # shall sometimes return "TE__ST", "__TEST", "T__EST", etc.
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

Note that, using this second approach, but wanting to modify the default case --- where we inject white-space, so that for example, instead of injecting a single white space we perhaps inject a new-line character, then we would adapt the second example above as such:


\vspace{1em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=S: EXAMPLE 3: Salting using S:SALT, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:TEST|s:{
}
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

Running that shall produce outputs such as ...


  \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Output from Example 3]
TE
ST
  \end{tcbverbatim}
  
    \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Output from Example 3]
TES
T
  \end{tcbverbatim}


    \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Output from Example 3]

TEST
  \end{tcbverbatim}
  
  
  \vspace{2em}
  
  On the other hand, if we were to test the unsalting command \texttt{s!:}, and still using the input ``TEST", we would have our code as such:
  
  
  \vspace{1em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=S: EXAMPLE 4: Unsalting using S!:, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:TEST|s!: # might sometimes return "TET", "EST", "TES", etc. Basically, unsalting
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}
  
  
  
\section{EXAMPLE APPLICATIONS of S:}
\label{SECEXAMPS}

  
Randomly modifying text or data is among the hallmarks of the \textbf{SALT} command, and so, our example applications shall attempt to especially leverage or demonstrate that.



\subsection{EXAMPLE 1: \textbf{CHILD EDUCATION:} The SIR GAME}
\label{SECEXAMP1S}

\begin{figure}[H]
  \centering
  \begin{subfigure}[b]{0.45\textwidth}
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{resources/images/sir_game_play_scenario1.jpg}
  \end{subfigure}
  \hfill
  \begin{subfigure}[b]{0.45\textwidth}
    \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{resources/images/sir_game_play_scenario3.jpg}
  \end{subfigure}
  \caption{\textbf{SIR GAME Scenarios} showing TEACHERS using the game to evaluate and entertain their young learners}
  \label{FIGSIRGAME}
\end{figure}

  
This application --- also a contribution to TEA programs suitable for children or learners of basic English literacy, is called the ``SIR Game" --- perhaps, because of the TEA primitives it employed in its original form (the first TEA program listing shared below). This program uses salting to create a simple child's game where they are tasked with filling-in the gap for a letter missing in a given 5-letter word, which is then marked by a ``?". 

\vspace{2em}

The original TEA code for this program was as such\footnote{This program was among the first shared TEA standard programs also part of the TEA example test applications collection: \url{https://github.com/mcnemesis/cli_tttt/tests/}}  
  
    \vspace{1em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=TEA Application: The SIR GAME, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
# randomly pick a word from the given list
I!:{HONEY TRICK WINDS GAMES} | a: | d:[ ].*$ 
# (="TRICK") for example
S:-:4 | # randomly inject "-" b4 4th pstn 
#(="TR-ICK") for example
R:.[-]:? | R:[-].:? | q!: | # returns "TR?CK"
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
      \captionof{figure}{The Original SIR GAME TEA source-code}
  \label{FIGTEASIRGAMEORIG}

\vspace{1em}
  
  However, now improved with both interactivity, scoring and a large set of words to pick from, the new version is as such:
  
  
  
  \vspace{2em}


{
 \Large
  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=TEA Program:  SIR GAME v2, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
v:vDESCRIPTION:{
###############################
# [new] SIR GAME v2: 
# by Fut. Prof. JWL @ Nuchwezi Research
###############################
WELCOME to a simple game fit for kids, and those learning spelling in English. THE RULES: playing is simple: 
You shall see a word with a MISSING lett?r, and if you guess it right, you score. You can also quit the game anytime if you like.}

#------[ DEFINE CONSTANTS ]
v:vDELIM:{
#####################
}
v:vMAINPROMPT:{
NEXT: [Q] QUIT | OR continue playing...}
v:vTHANKS:{Thanks for playing THE SIR GAME!}

#------[ DEFINE VARIABLES ]
v:vREPORT:{-|HAVE NOT PLAYED the SIR GAME yet|-}
v:vSCORE:{0} # hold current score
v:vMOVES:{0} # hold current moves

#------[ WELCOME MESSAGE ]
# show the welcome message
y:vDESCRIPTION | i*:
# then start game
j:lPLAY

#------[ THE GAME PLAY ]
l:lPLAYNEXT
y:vPROMPT | i*: | f:^[qQ]$:lFINISH

l:lPLAY #into the game...

# randomly pick a word from the given list
I!:{HONEY TRICK WINDS GAMES MONEY GRASS BABIES WATER BALLS PHONE INTERNET MUMMY HOUSE RAIN SHORTS KITTEN COLOUR MILK SHOES CUPS FORK EATING TABLE CHAIR WINDOW DOG RICE PLAY DIG WALLS TALK WALK FIND PHOTO WRITE KITE NUMBER LETTER PAPER CUTS GATES BANANA ICE PRICE HAND SING RING PIG PIN PEN HEN COW BIRD BAD SMILE HILL MEAL MEAT MEAN UGANDA CLASS TEACH APPLE STORY} 
| a: | d:[ ].*$ 
v:vWORD #store selected word
v!:vWORD | x!:{-1} | r.: | v:vNWORD #get its length-1
# randomly inject "-" b4 Nth pstn 
v:vSALT:{-}
S*:vWORD:vSALT:vNWORD | 
#(="TR-ICK") for example
R:.[-]:? | R:[-].:? | # returns "TR?CK"
v:vQUIZ

#------[ the QUIZ ]
l:lQUIZ 

y:vQUIZ | i*: | v:vANS # prompt and store response

#------[ the SCORING ]
#swap answer into quiz
v:vPATTERN:{?}
r*:vQUIZ:vPATTERN:vANS
z!: #capitalize result
v:vANSWORD #store
#compare...
v:vTESTER:{"__"==".."}|v:vP1:{__}|v:vP2:{..}
r*!:vTESTER:vP1:vWORD|v:vTESTER
r*!:vTESTER:vP2:vANSWORD|v:vTESTER
y:vTESTER | r.: | f:^true$:lWIN:lLOSS
l:lWIN # we won, update score +1
y:vANS | x!:{ (CORRECT)} | v:vANS
y:vSCORE | x!:{+1} | r.: | v:vSCORE | j:lREPORT 
l:lLOSS # we lost, update score -2
y:vANS | x!:{ (WRONG)} | v:vANS
y:vSCORE | x!:{-2} | r.: | v:vSCORE | j:lREPORT 

#------[ the REPORTING ]
l:lREPORT
#build report...
y:vMOVES | x!:{+1} | r.: | v:vMOVES #first, update moves thus far

v:v1:{The WORD: } | v:v2:{ | QUIZ: } | v:v3:{ | Your Answer: } | v:v4:{
TOTAL SCORE: } | v:v5:{ @MOVES: }
g*:{}:v1:vWORD:v2:vQUIZ:v3:vANS:v4:vSCORE:v5:vMOVES | v:vREPORT

x*!:vMAINPROMPT | v:vPROMPT

j:lPLAYNEXT # iterate...


#------[ QUIT ]
l:lFINISH
y:vREPORT | x*!:vDELIM | x*!:vTHANKS # show last report and quit
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
  \caption{TEA APPLICATION: SIR GAME v2}
  \label{FIGS1}
}


\vspace{2em}

When it is run on the command line, it shall still present a GUI interface --- unless all instances of \texttt{i*:} are replaced with just \texttt{i:} in that program's code. And so that, we can see how it feels to play the game via the screenshots below...


\begin{figure}[H]
  \begin{center}
   \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{resources/images/sir_game_welcome_screen.png}\\
   \caption{SIR GAME landing screen}
  \label{FIGSIRGAME1}
  \end{center}
\end{figure}


\begin{figure}[H]
  \begin{center}
   \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{resources/images/sir_game_quiz_screen.png}\\
   \caption{SIR GAME quiz screen}
  \label{FIGSIRGAME2}
  \end{center}
\end{figure}



\begin{figure}[H]
  \begin{center}
   \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{resources/images/sir_game_report_screen.png}\\
   \caption{SIR GAME report screen}
  \label{FIGSIRGAME3}
  \end{center}
\end{figure}


\vspace{2em}

So that, with that program and after some fun game play, we obtain results such as:


   \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 1 from SIR GAME v2]
The WORD: CHAIR | QUIZ: ?HAIR | Your Answer: s (WRONG)
TOTAL SCORE: 9 @MOVES: 15
#####################
Thanks for playing THE SIR GAME!
  \end{tcbverbatim}
  
  
  
     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 1 from SIR GAME v2]
The WORD: MEAN | QUIZ: MEA? | Your Answer: t (WRONG)
TOTAL SCORE: 1 @MOVES: 4
#####################
Thanks for playing THE SIR GAME!
  \end{tcbverbatim}
  
  
     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 1 from SIR GAME v2]
The WORD: MEAN | QUIZ: ME?N | Your Answer: a (CORRECT)

TOTAL SCORE: 4 @MOVES: 16

#####################

Thanks for playing THE SIR GAME!
  \end{tcbverbatim}

  
  
\vspace{2em}


Thus, as you can tell from those game-play reports, this game can truly be rewarding --- both the player, but also their friends, and especially teachers or for the case of kids, their parents --- especially given that they can use it to evaluate the performance and progress or spelling skills of their learners. Also, because adding to the list of test words isn't hard, teachers can modify this program to help teach and test learners about tricky words in almost any language!
  
  
  
  \begin{figure}[H]
  \begin{center}
   \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{resources/images/sir_game_play_scenario2}\\
   \caption{Especially the \textbf{WEB TEA SIR GAME} as an alternative to expensive and sometimes buggy child games.}
  \label{FIGSIRGAME2}
  \end{center}
\end{figure}
  
  
  
  
  So, this program might be studied further, enhanced or applied by those interested, via the following links:


 \vspace{1em}

 \url{https://tea.nuchwezi.com/?i=.&fc=https://gist.githubusercontent.com/mcnemesis/64f435e0805f9f3bcfbaed2ec9ef50e5/raw/}

\vspace{1em} 

OR

\vspace{1em} 

 \url{https://bit.ly/thesirgame}
  

\vspace{2em}

Finally, after realizing that the reports the program produces perhaps are even more important than the playing itself --- especially for the teachers, parents and evaluators, we then have the following version that also includes a computing of the rating of the player at the end of the game (only). 


  
  \vspace{2em}


{
 \Large
  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=TEA Program:  SIR GAME v3, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
v:vDESCRIPTION:{
###############################
# [new] SIR GAME v3: 
# by Fut. Prof. JWL @ Nuchwezi Research
###############################
WELCOME to a simple game fit for kids, and those learning spelling in English. THE RULES: playing is simple: 
You shall see a word with a MISSING lett?r, and if you guess it right, you score. You can also quit the game anytime if you like.}

#------[ DEFINE CONSTANTS ]
v:vDELIM:{
#####################
}
v:vMAINPROMPT:{
NEXT: [Q] QUIT | OR continue playing...}
v:vTHANKS:{Thanks for playing THE SIR GAME!}

#------[ DEFINE VARIABLES ]
v:vREPORT:{-|HAVE NOT PLAYED the SIR GAME yet|-}
v:vSCORE:{0} # hold current score
v:vMOVES:{0} # hold current moves

#------[ WELCOME MESSAGE ]
# show the welcome message
y:vDESCRIPTION | i*:
# then start game
j:lPLAY

#------[ THE GAME PLAY ]
l:lPLAYNEXT
y:vPROMPT | i*: | f:^[qQ]$:lFINISH

l:lPLAY #into the game...

# randomly pick a word from the given list
I!:{HONEY TRICK WINDS GAMES MONEY GRASS BABIES WATER BALLS PHONE INTERNET MUMMY HOUSE RAIN SHORTS KITTEN COLOUR MILK SHOES CUPS FORK EATING TABLE CHAIR WINDOW DOG RICE PLAY DIG WALLS TALK WALK FIND PHOTO WRITE KITE NUMBER LETTER PAPER CUTS GATES BANANA ICE PRICE HAND SING RING PIG PIN PEN HEN COW BIRD BAD SMILE HILL MEAL MEAT MEAN UGANDA CLASS TEACH APPLE STORY} 
| a: | d:[ ].*$ 
v:vWORD #store selected word
v!:vWORD | x!:{-1} | r.: | v:vNWORD #get its length-1
# randomly inject "-" b4 Nth pstn 
v:vSALT:{-}
S*:vWORD:vSALT:vNWORD | 
#(="TR-ICK") for example
R:.[-]:? | R:[-].:? | # returns "TR?CK"
v:vQUIZ

#------[ the QUIZ ]
l:lQUIZ 

y:vQUIZ | i*: | v:vANS # prompt and store response

#------[ the SCORING ]
#swap answer into quiz
v:vPATTERN:{?}
r*:vQUIZ:vPATTERN:vANS
z!: #capitalize result
v:vANSWORD #store
#compare...
v:vTESTER:{"__"==".."}|v:vP1:{__}|v:vP2:{..}
r*!:vTESTER:vP1:vWORD|v:vTESTER
r*!:vTESTER:vP2:vANSWORD|v:vTESTER
y:vTESTER | r.: | f:^true$:lWIN:lLOSS
l:lWIN # we won, update score +1
y:vANS | x!:{ (CORRECT)} | v:vANS
y:vSCORE | x!:{+1} | r.: | v:vSCORE | j:lREPORT 
l:lLOSS # we lost, update score -2
y:vANS | x!:{ (WRONG)} | v:vANS
y:vSCORE | x!:{-2} | r.: | v:vSCORE | j:lREPORT 

#------[ the REPORTING ]
l:lREPORT
#build report...
y:vMOVES | x!:{+1} | r.: | v:vMOVES #first, update moves thus far

#compute error rate
v:vEVALUATOR:{((..)/__)*100}|
r*!:vEVALUATOR:vP1:vMOVES|v:vEVALUATOR
r*!:vEVALUATOR:vP2:vSCORE|v:vEVALUATOR
y:vEVALUATOR | r.: | v:vRATING 
f!:\.:lRATEFINE
d!:{^.*\...} | v:vRATING | l:lRATEFINE

v:v1:{The WORD: } | v:v2:{ | QUIZ: } | v:v3:{ | Your Answer: } | v:v4:{
TOTAL SCORE: } | v:v5:{ @MOVES: } | v:v6:{ | RATING: } | v:v7:{%}
g*:{}:v1:vWORD:v2:vQUIZ:v3:vANS:v4:vSCORE:v5:vMOVES | v:vREPORT

x*!:vMAINPROMPT | v:vPROMPT

j:lPLAYNEXT # iterate...


#------[ QUIT ]
l:lFINISH
g*:{}:vREPORT:v6:vRATING:v7 | v:vREPORT #update report with rating info
y:vREPORT |  | x*!:vDELIM | x*!:vTHANKS # show last report and quit
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
  \caption{TEA APPLICATION: SIR GAME v3}
  \label{FIGS2}
}


And so that, it would produce more useful reports such as:

\vspace{2em}


     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 1 from SIR GAME v2]
The WORD: KITE | QUIZ: K?TE | Your Answer:  (WRONG)
TOTAL SCORE: 17 @MOVES: 20 | RATING: 85%
#####################
Thanks for playing THE SIR GAME!
  \end{tcbverbatim}

% TAZ SECTION: T
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\chapter{T: TRANSFORM}
\label{SECT}


\begin{table}[H]
  \centering
  \LARGE
	\begin{tabular}[t]{|p{0.2\textwidth}|p{0.5\textwidth}}
 
	\textbf{NAME} & \texttt{TRANSFORM}\\
	\hline
	\textbf{Purpose} & \begin{enumerate}
	\item Transform strings using certain [specific] methods.
	\item Strip leading white space from a string.
	\item Align [center or else] a multi-line text.
	\end{enumerate}\\
	\hline
	              
\end{tabular}
\caption{General Objectives of TEA Primitive \textbf{T:}}
  \label{TABTAZT}
\end{table}


\section{SEMANTICS of T:}
\label{SECSEMT}


\begin{table}[H]
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3} % Optional: increases row height
\rowcolors{1}{lightgray}{white}   % Alternating row colors
\begin{tabular}{>{\bfseries}m{0.4\linewidth} | m{0.6\linewidth}} % 2 columns, 
\rowcolor{white}
\textbf{\makecell[l]{INSTRUCTION\\ SIGNATURE}} & \textbf{INSTRUCTION FUNCTION} \\
\hline

\makecell[l]{t:\\t:STR}
 & Apply the rightmost triangulation transform to AI. With STR specified, instead operate on the given literal string STR.\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{t!:\\t!:STR} & Apply the leftmost triangulation transform to AI. With STR specified, instead operate on the given literal string STR.\\

t*:vNAME & Same as t:, but operating on the string stored in the vault with the name vNAME\\


\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{t*!:vNAME} & Same as t!:, but operating on the string stored in the vault with the name vNAME\\


\makecell[l]{t.:\\t.:STR\\t*.:vNAME} & Return the AI aligned in the center based on the longest line it contains. With STR, instead operate on literal string STR. The t*.:vNAME operates on string in vault vNAME. Result becomes AI\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{t!.:\\t!.:STR\\t*!.:vNAME} & Return the AI left-aligned or rather, with all lines having no leading, nor trailing white-space. With STR, instead operate on literal string STR. The t*!.:vNAME operates on string in vault vNAME. Result becomes AI\\

 \hline
\end{tabular}
\caption{The Semantics of T:}
\label{TABSEMT}
\end{table}


\section{NOTES about T:}
\label{SECNOTET} 
 
Some string transformations are so important in TEA, it was decided to make them primitives in the language so as to utilize them as first-level citizens of the language. Currently, the T-command space mostly implements the so-called ``Cetamol Triangulation", ``Cetamol Vertical Inversion Transformer"\cite{lutalo2024tea} transform on AI or strings held in vaults. Perhaps other transforms might also get this privilege in future TEA, or not. Time will tell, however, the inventor of the language chose to implement T: this way, and he has respectable reasons why it must be that way.

\vspace{2em}

A basic demonstration of the T-transform is demonstrated below using a command line invocation of TTTT:

\vspace{2em}

     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=T: EXAMPLE 1: Triangular Reduction Transform on the Command-line]
# implements the PARACETAMOL T-transform
tttt -i PARACETAMOL -c t:
PARACETAMOL
ARACETAMOL
RACETAMOL
ACETAMOL
CETAMOL
ETAMOL
TAMOL
AMOL
MOL
OL
L
  \end{tcbverbatim}


\vspace{2em}


Talking of different ways to transform inputs using TEA APIs, note that currently, TEA is on the WEB! Yes, so, the above example can also be run on any device, any operating system, without installing anything, simply by visiting the following URL in a modern web browser:

\vspace{2em}

\url{https://tea.nuchwezi.com?i=PARACETAMOL&c=t:&run}

\vspace{2em}

That shall effectively run the TEA program:

\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=T: EXAMPLE 2: Basic T-reduction program, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i:PARACETAMOL | t:
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}


And produce the same output as above.

\vspace{2em}


It should be interesting to note that this transform has potential roots in the literature and methods of Western Esotericism, in particular, in the occult science literature on crafting magic spells, such as in the work of Janet \& Farrar\cite{farrar1990spells}. We find several string transforms classified as ``pyramid-pattern" spells, some of which reduce a word one or two characters at a time, sometimes from the left, other times from the right. These ancient ideas have had use in creating potent incantations used in healing, blessing and protective magic in Europe and the Middle-East\cite{farrar1990spells}, and in TEA, the T-command space preserves this ancient, but useful class of word or phrase transformations, if for no other purpose, at least to curate this ancient wisdom for the modern explorer\footnote{Perhaps, and of interest to those who this useful, there are also many other ways that the TEA language has been applied to enabling and advancing work in esotericism --- refer to \cite{lutalo_2025_grimlumtauto} for more.}.

\vspace{2em}


Further, in an age where computer generated art such as with artificial intelligence is common-place, it feels that allowing for some means to create art even for a mere text processing language, using bare TEA primitives and no external libraries or power, would be worth exploring. The following example minimalist program generates some interesting art that looks like statistical graphs – bar charts, rendered without any special graphics facility but the text transformations possible with commands like \texttt{t:} and \texttt{r:}, and it bases on the same interesting sample input as the previous example---just the word ``PARACETAMOL".


\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=T: EXAMPLE 3: Islamic ASCII-art Generator based T-reduction, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
# an Islamic-art-generator twist of the PARACETAMOL Transform example
i!:PARACETAMOL| t: | h: | a!: | r!: | v:vART
v:vN:0|l:lS|y:vN|x!:{>5}|r.:|f:^true$:lF
y:vART
s:*|s:(|s:)
v:vART
y:vN|x!:{+1}|r.:|v:vN|j:lS
l:lF | y:vART 
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

And that program shall produce example art pieces such as shown hereafter...


\vspace{2em}

     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=T: SAMPLE OUTPUT 1 from EXAMPLE 3]
))..
.(.........).(
.*.(..(......................
)
..........*)(..........

.
...
...*.*)...
...(.*.*
  \end{tcbverbatim}


\vspace{2em}


     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=T: SAMPLE OUTPUT 2 from EXAMPLE 3]
..*)........
)).)*..(....*...

(........
.).........
....(..
...(
........
.
...(..)*.....*......
(...*
  \end{tcbverbatim}


\vspace{2em}



     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=T: SAMPLE OUTPUT 3 from EXAMPLE 3]
..*.....
)..(..
....(..
.*......(..*).*........(....(
)........
.
..*..).....(

........
.......*.).
.)
  \end{tcbverbatim}


\vspace{2em}

And as you can readily see and tell, those little artworks seem like they surely depict skylines in some hypothetical islamic city or township --- the stuff of \textbf{Aladdin} and ``1000 Arabian Nights!"

\vspace{2em}

And then we come to the \textbf{text-alignment} TEA transforms --- \texttt{t.:} and \texttt{t!.:} --- one for center alignment, the other for left-alignment. To demonstrate their use, we shall consider a simple out-of-alignment text such as this:


\vspace{2em}


     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=T: SAMPLE misaligned text]
 1. Hello World
2. What is happening?
        3. Where are you right now?
      4. Okay!
  \end{tcbverbatim}
  
  
 \vspace{2em}
 
 So, if we wish to align that text at the center, we shall use the following basic minimal program:
 
  \vspace{2em}
 
  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=T: EXAMPLE 4: Basic Center-alignment Program, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
t.:
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

Yup! It is that simple! The result, given the example input above, shall come out as such...

\vspace{2em}


     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=T: SAMPLE OUTPUT from EXAMPLE 4]
      1. Hello World       
   2. What is happening?   
3. Where are you right now?
         4. Okay!            
  \end{tcbverbatim}
  
  
 \vspace{2em}
 
 And as you can tell, that essentially works by aligning all sentences at the center, based on the longest line in the text.
 
 \vspace{2em}
 
 On the other hand, in case we wanted to left-align the text --- also equivalent to stripping out all leading white-space from each sentence, we would then use the following basic program:
 
 
   \vspace{2em}
 
  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=T: EXAMPLE 5: Basic Left-alignment Program, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
t!.:
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

And the output shall come out as such...


\vspace{2em}


     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=T: SAMPLE OUTPUT from EXAMPLE 5]
1. Hello World
2. What is happening?
3. Where are you right now?
4. Okay!          
  \end{tcbverbatim}
  
  
 \vspace{2em}
 
 
 But, if we instead wanted \textbf{right-alignment} --- which isn't as simple as using a single TEA command like the last two cases we saw, we would still be able to achieve that using a method such as the following \textbf{non-trivial} program:
 
 
 
   \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=T: EXAMPLE 6: ROBUST Right-alignment Program, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:{ 1. Hello World
2. What is happening?
        3. Where are you right now?
      4. Okay!}
t!.: | v:vLTEXT #store left-aligned text

# constants
v:vLGLUE:{=======} #newline delimiter
v:vEMPTY:{}
v:vPADCHAR:{ }
#v:vPADCHAR:{-} #uncomment to see how the padding worked
v:vNL:{
}
v:vSPACECHAR:{ }

# variables
v:vNLONGEST:{0} #store length of longest line
v:vRALIGNED_TEXT:{} # to hold right-aligned text

#--[begin processing..]
g*.:vLGLUE | v:vFLATTEXT | v:vFLATTEXT_ORIG #store flattened text
y:vLGLUE | x!:{.*$} | v:vLGLUE_EXT

l:lPROCESS_LINE
y:vFLATTEXT  | d*:vLGLUE_EXT | v:vLINE | y*!:vLINE | v:vNLINE 

y:vNLINE | x!:{>} | x*!:vNLONGEST | r.: | f:^true$:lSETMAX:lNEXTLINE
l:lSETMAX
y:vNLINE | v:vNLONGEST
l:lNEXTLINE
y:vFLATTEXT | d*!:vLGLUE_EXT | v:vFLATTEXT | r*:vFLATTEXT:vLGLUE:vEMPTY | v:vFLATTEXT

f:^$:lPROCESS_ALIGN
j:lPROCESS_LINE

l:lPROCESS_ALIGN
y:vNLONGEST 

y:vFLATTEXT_ORIG | v:vFLATTEXT #restore..

l:lPROCESS_LINE_ALIGN
y:vFLATTEXT | d*:vLGLUE_EXT | v:vLINE | y*!:vLINE | v:vNLINE
y:vNLINE | x!:{<} | x*!:vNLONGEST | r.: | f:^true$:lADDPAD:lNEXTLINE_ALIGN
l:lADDPAD
#build pad...
y:vNLONGEST | x!:{-} | x*!:vNLINE | r.: | v:vNPAD_LEN_MAX
v:vI:{0} | v:vPAD:{}
l:lSTART_PAD
g*:{}:vPAD:vPADCHAR | v:vPAD
v*!:vPAD | v:vNPAD_LEN
y:vNPAD_LEN | x!:{==} | x*!:vNPAD_LEN_MAX | r.: | f:^true$:lAPPLYPAD
j:lSTART_PAD
l:lAPPLYPAD
g*:{}:vPAD:vLINE | v:vLINE_PADDED
g*:{}:vRALIGNED_TEXT:vLINE_PADDED:vLGLUE | v:vRALIGNED_TEXT #add to aligned text...
j:lUPDATE_FLATTEXT

l:lNEXTLINE_ALIGN
g*:{}:vRALIGNED_TEXT:vLINE:vLGLUE | v:vRALIGNED_TEXT #add to aligned text...
l:lUPDATE_FLATTEXT
y:vFLATTEXT | d*!:vLGLUE_EXT | v:vFLATTEXT | r*:vFLATTEXT:vLGLUE:vEMPTY | v:vFLATTEXT
f:^$:lFIN_ALIGN
j:lPROCESS_LINE_ALIGN

l:lFIN_ALIGN
r*!:vRALIGNED_TEXT:vLGLUE:vNL | v:vRALIGNED_TEXT
y:vRALIGNED_TEXT #output result
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
 
 
\vspace{2em}

Which, against the same sample input text as before, would produce the output:



\vspace{2em}


     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=T: SAMPLE OUTPUT from EXAMPLE 6]
             1. Hello World
      2. What is happening?
3. Where are you right now?
                   4. Okay!
  \end{tcbverbatim}
  
  
 \vspace{2em}
 
 And if we wish to see how or if the padding indeed works as planned, we essentially uncomment that line overriding \texttt{vPADCHAR}, so that we then get the output as:
 
 
 \vspace{2em}


     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=T: SAMPLE OUTPUT from EXAMPLE 6]
-------------1. Hello World
------2. What is happening?
3. Where are you right now?
-------------------4. Okay!
  \end{tcbverbatim}
  
  
 \vspace{2em}
 
 So, that last example should indeed demonstrate and prove how vital and critically important such basic transforms as what we get with \texttt{t.:} and \texttt{t!.:} are! Perhaps, in the future, if there is demand, we shall also make the right-alignment text transform implemented in TEA in such a cumbersome way as we have seen in that program, as simple as a mere invocation of a basic TEA primitive.
 
 
\vspace{2em}

Also note that, oftentimes, one might get headaches over text processing not because their programs aren't doing the right thing, but merely because, the same --- correct text --- is displayed differently in different environments! For example, consider the above two last sample outputs --- one with the natural single space character, `` ", as the padding, and the other with the single dash, ``-", as the padding character. We see that even in our somewhat neutral verbatim text widgets they look different --- with the first output somewhat looking as though some of the lines were not properly right-aligned! And yet, when we paste the two versions of text output in a neutral standard text environment such as Microsoft's Notepad, we see that they both are similarly well right-aligned --- see in screenshot below. Thus, for such cases as these, one might want to instead work with the command-line TEA environment (see second screenshot), which also seems to be more compelling that the program does the right thing in both cases.


\begin{figure}[H]
  \begin{center}
   \includegraphics[trim=0cm 1cm 0cm 0cm, clip, width=0.9\textwidth,]{resources/images/notepad_right_align.png}\\
   \caption{Two Samples of Text Right-Alignment resulting from a TEA program, as shown in Microsoft Notepad}
  \label{FIGTEARIGHTALIGN1}
  \end{center}
\end{figure}




\begin{figure}[H]
  \begin{center}
   \includegraphics[trim=0cm 1cm 0cm 0cm, clip, width=0.9\textwidth,]{resources/images/tea_right_align_text_cli_case.png}\\
   \caption{Two Samples of Text Right-Alignment resulting from a TEA program, as shown via the command-line}
  \label{FIGTEARIGHTALIGN2}
  \end{center}
\end{figure}


The two cases compared via the TEA WEB IDE are not as satisfactory despite using the same exact TEA code/solution and same exact sample inputs as all the above cases! So, as you see in the outputs below, somehow, one might have no option but to compare the text outputs via copying it out of the IDE and testing from elsewhere --- in this case, we tried to simplify the text-area widget's styling as much as possible, and especially enforced styling using the monospace font-family, but still, the differences one notices can make debugging via inspection of text output quite painful if one doesn't understand the cause of the differences!


\begin{figure}[H]
  \begin{center}
   \includegraphics[trim=0cm 0cm 15cm 0cm, clip, width=0.9\textwidth,]{resources/images/tea_right_align_text_web_case.png}\\
   \caption{Sample of Text Right-Alignment resulting from the TEA program, as shown via TEA WEB IDE}
  \label{FIGTEARIGHTALIGN3}
  \end{center}
\end{figure}



\begin{figure}[H]
  \begin{center}
   \includegraphics[trim=0cm 0cm 15cm 0cm, clip, width=0.9\textwidth,]{resources/images/tea_right_align_text_web_case2.png}\\
   \caption{The other Sample of Text Right-Alignment resulting from same TEA program, as shown via TEA WEB IDE}
  \label{FIGTEARIGHTALIGN4}
  \end{center}
\end{figure}

 
\section{EXAMPLE APPLICATIONS of T:}
\label{SECEXAMPT}

  
As you might have noticed studying those examples covered in the last section, the \textbf{T-Command Space}, also merely called the ``TRANSFORM" commands, are very great when it comes to formatting text using TEA. Especially with regards to the artistic alignment instructions, one finds that \texttt{t:} can be very handy when there is need to come up with some artistic text based on just creative alignment, repetition, and careful processing of some basic starter text patterns --- and talking of which, our next application shall especially help draw the picture for you --- in fact, it is a great example for drawing abstract pictures using just Text Processing.



\subsection{EXAMPLE 1: \textbf{COMPUTATIONAL ART:} Abstract Text-Art Generator}
\label{SECEXAMP1T}



This program is for \textbf{generating abstract art}. It basically builds upon ideas we have already encountered in earlier TEA examples such as in the generation of ``Islamic Skyline Art" --- see \textbf{Example 3} in \textbf{\hyperref[SECNOTET]{Section \ref{SECNOTET}}}, but also builds on the non-trivial TEA code we used to implement the text right-alignment output as shown in \textbf{Example 6} of that same section. 

\vspace{2em}

The emphasis is on abstract art, but first, to put things into perspective, we'll briefly look into what the authorities consider ``art" to be, and as such, shall want to keep some of these ideas from a work by \textbf{Robert Wilkinson}\cite{wilkinson1991theories} in mind:\\




\noindent
\begin{minipage}{1\textwidth}
\vspace{1em}
\begin{quotation}
{\ttfamily

What the nature of art is[?]... For most of the last two millennia it has been assumed that to answer this question is the same as to define the term `art', and it has been taken for granted that there will be a quality or set of qualities shared by all works of art, as a result of the possession of which we apply the term `art' to them. In other words, what makes something a work of art is that it possesses a quality or qualities which only works of art have. There has been a small number of answers to the question of what this defining quality or qualities might be...

\vspace{1em}

[For example] a very early answer, and the one that held the field for longest, is that \textit{the defining property of art is that it is an imitation of nature.}... found in Plato... generally held to be derivable from some remarks by Aristotle in his \textit{Poetics}

\vspace{1em}

A second candidate put forward as the defining property of art is expression... According to expression theories, what is distinctive about art is that it is an expression of the artist's emotion such that the artist's emotion is transmitted to others, it being assumed that the experience of emotion is one we desire and rightly value...

\vspace{1em}

Other thinkers have contented that the distinctive feature of art is to be found in the formal properties of a work of art...

\vspace{1em}

Formal qualities of works of art... `expressive qualities'.. are two types of aesthetic quality... of which the most important is beauty... that beauty consists in a harmonious, symmetrical and balanced arrangement of the parts of an object.

}
\hspace*{\fill} ---\textbf{Robert Wilkinson}, \textit{Theories of Art and Beauty}, 1991\cite{wilkinson1991theories}
\end{quotation}
\vspace{1em}
\end{minipage}



 \vspace{2em}
 
 And because we wish to have a means to create art that is not just random (given that each other piece shall be created spontaneously and might never ever be repeated nor similar to any others), but also abstract(``pictures and sculptures which do not on the face appear to be imitations of anything"\cite{wilkinson1991theories}), but also beautiful --- concerning which aspect, despite our working in pure text, and only using basic motifs, shall undoubtedly attempt to preserve or enforce harmony, symmetry and balance so as to create aesthetically pleasing works, with or without explicit meaning.


{
 \Large
  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=TEA Program: ABSTRACT ASCII-ART GENERATOR v1, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
########################################
# ABSTRACT ASCII-ART GENERATOR v1
########################################
# Based off the TEA right-align transform code,
# This program is meant to create beautiful
# Abstract Art using basic text symbols
# and just two basic ideas; triangles 
# and rectangles. 
#----------------------------------------
# Copyright: Fut. Prof. J. Willrich Lutalo
# in Computational Art: https://t.me/jwlart
#########################################

#---[ FIRSt, BUILD TRIANGULAR ART PIECE ]
v:vPalette:{=-=|=-=|=-=|=-=|=-=}
a*!:vPalette # create random baseline pattern
t: #apply triangulation transform

#store starter text pattern
v:vLTEXT

#---[ THEN TRANSFORM INTO RECTANGULAR ART PIECE ]
# constants
v:vLGLUE:{=======} #newline delimiter
v:vEMPTY:{}
v:vPADCHAR:{ }
v:vPADCHAR:{-} #uncomment to see how the padding worked
v:vNL:{
}
v:vSPACECHAR:{ }

# variables
v:vNLONGEST:{0} #store length of longest line
v:vRALIGNED_TEXT:{} # to hold right-aligned text

#--[begin processing..]
g*.:vLGLUE | v:vFLATTEXT | v:vFLATTEXT_ORIG #store flattened text
y:vLGLUE | x!:{.*$} | v:vLGLUE_EXT

l:lPROCESS_LINE
y:vFLATTEXT  | d*:vLGLUE_EXT | v:vLINE | y*!:vLINE | v:vNLINE 

y:vNLINE | x!:{>} | x*!:vNLONGEST | r.: | f:^true$:lSETMAX:lNEXTLINE
l:lSETMAX
y:vNLINE | v:vNLONGEST
l:lNEXTLINE
y:vFLATTEXT | d*!:vLGLUE_EXT | v:vFLATTEXT | r*:vFLATTEXT:vLGLUE:vEMPTY | v:vFLATTEXT

f:^$:lPROCESS_ALIGN
j:lPROCESS_LINE

l:lPROCESS_ALIGN
y:vNLONGEST 

y:vFLATTEXT_ORIG | v:vFLATTEXT #restore..

l:lPROCESS_LINE_ALIGN
y:vFLATTEXT | d*:vLGLUE_EXT | v:vLINE | y*!:vLINE | v:vNLINE
y:vNLINE | x!:{<} | x*!:vNLONGEST | r.: | f:^true$:lADDPAD:lNEXTLINE_ALIGN
l:lADDPAD
#build pad...
y:vNLONGEST | x!:{-} | x*!:vNLINE | r.: | v:vNPAD_LEN_MAX
v:vI:{0} | v:vPAD:{}
l:lSTART_PAD
g*:{}:vPAD:vPADCHAR | v:vPAD
v*!:vPAD | v:vNPAD_LEN
y:vNPAD_LEN | x!:{==} | x*!:vNPAD_LEN_MAX | r.: | f:^true$:lAPPLYPAD
j:lSTART_PAD
l:lAPPLYPAD
g*:{}:vPAD:vLINE | v:vLINE_PADDED
g*:{}:vRALIGNED_TEXT:vLINE_PADDED:vLGLUE | v:vRALIGNED_TEXT #add to aligned text...
j:lUPDATE_FLATTEXT

l:lNEXTLINE_ALIGN
g*:{}:vRALIGNED_TEXT:vLINE:vLGLUE | v:vRALIGNED_TEXT #add to aligned text...
l:lUPDATE_FLATTEXT
y:vFLATTEXT | d*!:vLGLUE_EXT | v:vFLATTEXT | r*:vFLATTEXT:vLGLUE:vEMPTY | v:vFLATTEXT
f:^$:lFIN_ALIGN
j:lPROCESS_LINE_ALIGN

l:lFIN_ALIGN
r*!:vRALIGNED_TEXT:vLGLUE:vNL | v:vRALIGNED_TEXT
y:vRALIGNED_TEXT #output result
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
  \caption{TEA APPLICATION: ABSTRACT ASCII-ART GENERATOR (AAAG) v1}
  \label{FIGT1}
}


\vspace{2em}


That program is intentionally non-interactive, and processes no external inputs/arguments. It merely creates a random piece of art and then returns it as the final output, after which it then quits.

\vspace{2em}

Some examples of the art it creates (AAAG v1) are as shown in the following sample outputs...


\vspace{2em}

\newpage

     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 1 from AAAG v1]
=||-=--==-=|===|==-
-||-=--==-=|===|==-
--|-=--==-=|===|==-
----=--==-=|===|==-
----=--==-=|===|==-
-------==-=|===|==-
-------==-=|===|==-
-------==-=|===|==-
--------=-=|===|==-
----------=|===|==-
----------=|===|==-
-----------|===|==-
------------===|==-
-------------==|==-
--------------=|==-
---------------|==-
----------------==-
-----------------=-
-------------------
  \end{tcbverbatim}

\vspace{1em}


\newpage

     \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 2 from AAAG v1]
---=-=-==-=||=|-|-
====-=-==-=||=|-|-
-=====-==-=||=|-|-
--====-==-=||=|-|-
---======-=||=|-|-
----=====-=||=|-|-
-----====-=||=|-|-
------=====||=|-|-
-------====||=|-|-
--------====|=|-|-
---------=====|-|-
----------====|-|-
-----------====-|-
------------====|-
-------------====-
------------------
------------------
------------------
------------------
------------------
  \end{tcbverbatim}

\vspace{1em}

\newpage


However, one shall quickly realize that AAAG v1 somewhat lacks variety, and that might also help if we had some way to decide which kind of patterns as well as styles to use for the generation of our art. Thus, the following enhanced version, also interactive, shall allow us to do the following at minimum:

\vspace{2em}

\begin{enumerate}
\item Decide what style of Abstract Art we wish to create --- \textbf{Basic}, \textbf{Mirrored} and \textbf{Chaos}.
\item (only Chaos) Randomly decide which of several starter patterns/motifs to use for the seeding of our art-piece.
\item (only Chaos) Randomly decide what extra patterns or symbols the generator might throw into the generation process.
\item (only Chaos) Randomly decide if to shuffle the final art-piece or not.
\item Assign the generated art-piece some automatic, but relevant name --- based on user-specified art style, date, time and some entropy.
\end{enumerate}

\vspace{2em}


With that specification of an abstract art generator, and our original first implementation, we then come up with what we are referring to as \textbf{ABSTRACT TEXT-ART GENERATOR v2}, and whose TEA code  is as follows...


\vspace{2em}


{
 \Large
  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=TEA Program: ABSTRACT TEXT-ART GENERATOR v2, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
v:vDESCRIPTION:{
###############################
# ABSTRACT TEXT-ART GENERATOR v2
###############################
ATAG was originally based off of the TEA right-align transform code. The program is meant to create beautiful abstract text-art using basic motifs and just two basic ideas; triangulation and rectangles. 
###############################
CREDITS: Fut. Prof. J. Willrich Lutalo 
Computational Art: https://t.me/jwlart
###############################}

#---[ some variables ]
v:vARTSEED:{}
v:vARTWORK:{}
z.:DATE | v:vARTNAME

#---[ WELCOME MESSAGE ]
y:vDESCRIPTION | i*:

#---[ First, let's Build a PROMPT ]
l:lPICKSTYLE
v:vSTYLE_PROMPT:{Select TEXT-ART Style: | [a] Basic | [b] Mirror | [c] Chaos}
y:vSTYLE_PROMPT | i*: | t.: | z: | v:vSTYLE | f!:^[abc]$:lPICKSTYLE

#---[ Next, BUILD TRIANGULAR ART PIECE ]
#VARIANT 0: Basic Pallete
v:vPalette:{=-=|=-=|=-=|=-=|=-=} | y:vPalette | v:vARTSEED
a*!:vARTSEED | v:vARTSEED # create random baseline pattern
#VARIANT 1: rightmost triangulation
t: | v:vARTSEED #apply right-most triangulation transform

y:vSTYLE | f!:a:lSKIPBASIC
j:lBASICBASEPALETTE

l:lSKIPBASIC
y:vSTYLE | f!:c:lSKIPCHAOS

# chaos: should we tweak palette?
n!:1:0 | v:vUSE_RANDOM_PALETTE
y:vUSE_RANDOM_PALETTE| f:1:lTWEAK_PALETTE:lBASICBASEPALETTE
l:lTWEAK_PALETTE

# first, reset to basic palette mode...
v:vPalette:{=-=|=-=|=-=|=-=|=-=} | y:vPalette | v:vARTSEED
a*!:vARTSEED | v:vARTSEED # create random baseline pattern

#decide which to use:
n!:2:1 | v:vRANDOM_PALETTE_INDEX

y:vRANDOM_PALETTE_INDEX | f:2:lUSE_PAL2:lSKIP_PAL2
l:lUSE_PAL2
#VARIANT 0a: Basic Pallete 2
v:vPalette:{=*=|=-=|=*=|=-=|=*=} | y:vPalette | v:vARTSEED
j:lPALETTE_FINISHED
l:lSKIP_PAL2

y:vRANDOM_PALETTE_INDEX | f:3:lUSE_PAL3:lSKIP_PAL3
l:lUSE_PAL3
#VARIANT 0b: Basic Pallete 3
v:vPalette:{=*=|=-=+=*=+=-=|=*=} | y:vPalette | v:vARTSEED
j:lPALETTE_FINISHED
l:lSKIP_PAL3

l:lPALETTE_FINISHED
#make it fat or keep thin?
n!:1:0 | v:vRANDOM_PALETTE_SIZE

y:vRANDOM_PALETTE_SIZE | f:0:lSKIP_FATTENING
#VARIANT 0c: Fat Basic Pallete
v:vEXTRA_SYMBOLS:{#[]~:} | v:vGLUE:{} | v:vLLIMIT:{1}
v*!:vPalette | v:vNPALSIZE | n*:vNPALSIZE:vLLIMIT | v:vNEXTENSION_SIZE
p*!:vNEXTENSION_SIZE:vGLUE:vEXTRA_SYMBOLS | v:vEXTENSION
g*:{}:vPalette:vEXTENSION | a!: | v:vARTSEED
l:lSKIP_FATTENING

l:lBASICBASEPALETTE

y:vSTYLE | f:a:lPROCESSBASIC
y:vUSE_RANDOM_PALETTE| f:0:lFINISHED_TRIANGULATION # so we don't apply triangulation twice!

#use left or right triangulation?
n!:1:0 | v:vRANDOM_TRIANGULATION
y:vRANDOM_TRIANGULATION | f:0:lRIGHT_TRIANGULATION:lLEFT_TRIANGULATION
l:lRIGHT_TRIANGULATION
y:vARTSEED | t: | v:vARTSEED #apply right-most triangulation transform
j:lFINISHED_TRIANGULATION

l:lLEFT_TRIANGULATION
#VARIANT 2: leftmost triangulation
y:vARTSEED | t!: | v:vARTSEED #apply left-most triangulation transform

l:lFINISHED_TRIANGULATION

l:lSKIPCHAOS
y:vSTYLE | f!:[bc]:lSKIPMIRROR
f:b:lAPPLYMIRROR

#apply mirror or not?
n!:1:0 | v:vRANDOM_APPLY_MIRROR
y:vRANDOM_APPLY_MIRROR | f:0:lSKIPMIRROR

l:lAPPLYMIRROR
#VARIANT 3: invert triangulation
y:vARTSEED | m!: | v:vARTSEED

l:lSKIPMIRROR

l:lPROCESSBASIC
y:vARTSEED
#store starter text pattern
v:vLTEXT

#---[ THEN TRANSFORM INTO ART WORK]
# constants
v:vLGLUE:{=======} #newline delimiter
v:vEMPTY:{}
v:vPADCHAR:{ }
v:vPADCHAR:{-} #uncomment to see how the padding worked
v:vNL:{
}
v:vSPACECHAR:{ }

# variables
v:vNLONGEST:{0} #store length of longest line
v:vRALIGNED_TEXT:{} # to hold right-aligned text

#--[begin processing..]
g*.:vLGLUE | v:vFLATTEXT | v:vFLATTEXT_ORIG #store flattened text
y:vLGLUE | x!:{.*$} | v:vLGLUE_EXT

l:lPROCESS_LINE
y:vFLATTEXT  | d*:vLGLUE_EXT | v:vLINE | y*!:vLINE | v:vNLINE 

y:vNLINE | x!:{>} | x*!:vNLONGEST | r.: | f:^true$:lSETMAX:lNEXTLINE
l:lSETMAX
y:vNLINE | v:vNLONGEST
l:lNEXTLINE
y:vFLATTEXT | d*!:vLGLUE_EXT | v:vFLATTEXT | r*:vFLATTEXT:vLGLUE:vEMPTY | v:vFLATTEXT

f:^$:lPROCESS_ALIGN
j:lPROCESS_LINE

l:lPROCESS_ALIGN
y:vNLONGEST 

y:vFLATTEXT_ORIG | v:vFLATTEXT #restore..

l:lPROCESS_LINE_ALIGN
y:vFLATTEXT | d*:vLGLUE_EXT | v:vLINE | y*!:vLINE | v:vNLINE
y:vNLINE | x!:{<} | x*!:vNLONGEST | r.: | f:^true$:lADDPAD:lNEXTLINE_ALIGN
l:lADDPAD
#build pad...
y:vNLONGEST | x!:{-} | x*!:vNLINE | r.: | v:vNPAD_LEN_MAX
v:vI:{0} | v:vPAD:{}
l:lSTART_PAD
g*:{}:vPAD:vPADCHAR | v:vPAD
v*!:vPAD | v:vNPAD_LEN
y:vNPAD_LEN | x!:{==} | x*!:vNPAD_LEN_MAX | r.: | f:^true$:lAPPLYPAD
j:lSTART_PAD
l:lAPPLYPAD
g*:{}:vPAD:vLINE | v:vLINE_PADDED
g*:{}:vRALIGNED_TEXT:vLINE_PADDED:vLGLUE | v:vRALIGNED_TEXT #add to aligned text...
j:lUPDATE_FLATTEXT

l:lNEXTLINE_ALIGN
g*:{}:vRALIGNED_TEXT:vLINE:vLGLUE | v:vRALIGNED_TEXT #add to aligned text...
l:lUPDATE_FLATTEXT
y:vFLATTEXT | d*!:vLGLUE_EXT | v:vFLATTEXT | r*:vFLATTEXT:vLGLUE:vEMPTY | v:vFLATTEXT
f:^$:lFIN_ALIGN
j:lPROCESS_LINE_ALIGN

l:lFIN_ALIGN
r*!:vRALIGNED_TEXT:vLGLUE:vNL | v:vRALIGNED_TEXT
y:vRALIGNED_TEXT #output result
v:vARTWORK # store tentative final art piece

#---[ Post-Processing! ]
# shuffle output or not?
y:vSTYLE | f!:c:lFINISH
n!:1:0 | v:vRANDOM_SHUFFLE
y:vRANDOM_SHUFFLE| f:0:lFINISH
y:vARTWORK | a: | h!:{ } | r!:{ }:{}
v:vARTWORK # store shuffled piece

l:lFINISH
# include name of art-work...
y:vSTYLE | z!: | v:vSTYLE_LABEL
z.:TIME | g!: | x:{-} | v:vTIME_LABEL
p!:2:{}:{AEIOU} | v:vINSTANCE_LABEL
y:vARTNAME | d!:{(^.|[ ].)|.$} | g!: | x*:vSTYLE_LABEL | x*!:vTIME_LABEL | x*!:vINSTANCE_LABEL | v:vARTNAME
g*:{
}:vARTNAME:vARTWORK #show final art
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
  \caption{TEA APPLICATION: ABSTRACT TEXT-ART GENERATOR (ATAG) v2}
  \label{FIGT2}
}


\vspace{2em}


And so, to appreciate this art generator of ours, ATAG v2, check out some of the art works it outputs under the three categories/art-styles:

==
  \end{tcbverbatim}
  
  
 And so, as you can tell from those outputs, ATAG isn't a useless tool. A creative artisan could for example take those output works and further enhance them as such:
 
 \begin{itemize}
 \item The symbols could be colorized based on some special rules.
 \item The common pattern of the padding dashes could be varied as per some hue gradient independently of and contrasting against the triangular pattern symbols.
 \item Some creative substitution could be conducted in a richer editor so that some symbols are for example replaced by more appealing unicode characters, etc.
 \end{itemize}
 
 \subsubsection{MODIFIED ATAG Outputs}
 
 Using the post-processing ideas/suggestions above, we for example might end up with art works such as the following, that are merely enhancements of the basic ATAG outputs we have already seen in the examples above...
 
 
   \begin{figure}[H]
  \begin{center}
   \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{resources/pdfs/AF3J26-1736UI.pdf}\\
   \caption{Enlarged and colorized \textbf{AF3J26-1736UI}}
  \label{FIGSTEXAMPLEART1}
  \end{center}
\end{figure}
 
 
 
   \begin{figure}[H]
  \begin{center}
   \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{resources/pdfs/BF3J26-1747IO.pdf}\\
   \caption{Enlarged and colorized \textbf{BF3J26-1747IO}}
  \label{FIGSTEXAMPLEART2}
  \end{center}
\end{figure}
 
 
   \begin{figure}[H]
  \begin{center}
   \includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{resources/pdfs/CF3J26-1752OU.pdf}\\

\chapter{U: UNIQUEIFY}
\label{SECU}


\begin{table}[H]
  \centering
  \LARGE
	\begin{tabular}[t]{|p{0.2\textwidth}|p{0.5\textwidth}}
 
	\textbf{NAME} & \texttt{UNIQUEIFY}\\
	\hline
	\textbf{Purpose} & \begin{enumerate}
	\item Compute the unique projection of things.
	\item Compute the \textbf{modal sequence statistic} via two methods.
	\item Extract symbol sets from arbitrary text.
	\item Identify all the unique words in a text.
	\item Sort items (symbols or words) by their relative highest frequency --- a voting primitive.
	\end{enumerate}\\
	\hline
	              
\end{tabular}
\caption{General Objectives of TEA Primitive \textbf{U:}}
  \label{TABTAZU}
\end{table}


\section{SEMANTICS of U:}
\label{SECSEMU}


\begin{table}[H]
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3} % Optional: increases row height
\rowcolors{1}{lightgray}{white}   % Alternating row colors
\begin{tabular}{>{\bfseries}m{0.4\linewidth} | m{0.6\linewidth}} % 2 columns, 
\rowcolor{white}
\textbf{\makecell[l]{INSTRUCTION\\ SIGNATURE}} & \textbf{INSTRUCTION FUNCTION} \\
\hline

\makecell[l]{u:\\u.:}
 & Return AI reduced to only its UNIQUE WORDS, their order representing their relative frequency in AI, most frequent first, otherwise preserving order of first occurrence. With “.” Qualifier --- as in \texttt{u.:}, Uses the PMSS\cite{lutalo_2025_grimlumtauto} algorithm.\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{u:STR\\u.:STR} & Same as \texttt{u:} and \texttt{u.:}, but operating on the given string STR\\

\makecell[l]{u!:\\u!.:} & Return AI reduced to only its UNIQUE CHARACTERS, their order representing their relative frequency in AI, most frequent first, otherwise preserving order of first occurrence.\\


\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{u!:STR\\u!.:STR} & Same as \texttt{u!:} and \texttt{u!.:}, but operating on the given string STR\\


\makecell[l]{u*:vNAME\\u*.:vNAME} & Same as \texttt{u:}, but operating on the string stored in the vault with the name vNAME\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{u*!:vNAME\\u*!.:vNAME} & Same as \texttt{u!:} and \texttt{u!.:}, but operating on the string stored in the vault with the name vNAME\\

 \hline
\end{tabular}
\caption{The Semantics of U:}
\label{TABSEMU}
\end{table}

Concerning \textbf{\hyperref[TABSEMU]{Table \ref{TABSEMU}}}, note that there is one very vital feature related to modal sequences, that would be great to have in the TEA standard as a primitive just like we have done for the MSS --- this is the \textbf{sequence characteristic} measure (see \textbf{\hyperref[PROP6]{Proposal 6}}), and which perhaps might be made available with an extension of the U-command space with a qualifier such as ``?" --- so that we for example obtain the SC of AI using \texttt{u?:} by words, and with \texttt{u?!:} by characters, however, the risk here is that, this would introduce an extra symbol in the TEA command qualifier set (see \textbf{\hyperref[SECQUALIFY]{Section \ref{SECQUALIFY}}}), and this might somewhat pollute the otherwise already compact and minimal number of TEA command signatures possible (see \textbf{\hyperref[SECGRAM]{Section \ref{SECGRAM}}}). Thus, perhaps better we let go of this, and just settle with SC implementations such as shown in \textbf{\hyperref[LISTSC]{Figure \ref{LISTSC}}}.


\section{NOTES about U:}
\label{SECNOTEU} 
 
 
 First, concerning the fact that the TEA \textbf{Uniqueify} command space especially helps us compute modal sequence statistics, one shall need to know not just what these are or why they are important, but also that essentially, there are two ways to compute the modal sequence statistic (MSS); the basic, originally specified method\cite{transformatics} (also later referred to as ``BMSS" in \cite{lutalo_2025_grimlumtauto}) and a later, alternative algorithm (``PMSS", positional MSS) first developed and presented in a book on the mathematics used by magicians --- see \textbf{Appendix A}, esp. \textbf{Section A.1} and \textbf{Section A.2} in \cite{lutalo_2025_grimlumtauto}.
 
 
 \vspace{2em}
 
 
 First presented in \cite{transformatics} (\textbf{Section 4.1}), the concept of the \textbf{modal sequence statistic} (MSS) is founded on the problem of wanting to determine which of the unique symbols or elements in a given sequence occur either the most frequently, or otherwise the earliest relative to the other distinct members of the same sequence. It is essentially a statistical measure somewhat generalizing the traditional measure of a \textbf{mode}, and a most recent evolution of its formal definition is presented in \cite{lutalo_2025_trans_genetics} as such:
 
 \vspace{2em}
 
\begin{defn}[The \textbf{Modal Sequence Statistic}, $\overset{>}{\Theta}$]
\label{DEFMSSFINb}
\index{definition!Modal Sequence Statistic}
$ $\\
\index{modal sequence statistic!definition}
Given any sequence $\Theta$ that consists of well ordered finite collection of $n$ symbols, $\omega_i$, possibly with some being repeated --- essentially that we have the sequence $\Theta : \mathbb{N} \times \psi_{\omega} \quad \land \quad \invpi(\Theta) = n$, then the measure:

\vspace{1em}
\begin{equation}
\label{EQMSS}
\overset{>}{\Theta} = \langle \prod\limits_{\omega_i \in \psi_\omega} \omega_i \rangle 
\end{equation}
\vspace{1em}

is the \textbf{modal sequence statistic} (MSS) of the given sequence, and that $\psi_\omega \cong \psi(\Theta)$, such that, we can produce or generate $\overset{>}{\Theta}$ from $\Theta$ via the following transformer:

\vspace{1em}
\begin{transf}[The MSS Generator, $O_{mss}(\Theta)$]
\label{TRANSMSSGENb}
$ $\\
$\Theta \xrightarrow{O_{mss}(\Theta)} \overset{>}{\Theta} = \langle \prod\limits_{\omega_i \in \psi_\omega} \omega_i \rangle ;$\\
$\forall \omega_i \in \Theta \quad \exists \omega_i \in \overset{>}{\Theta}$\\
$\land \quad I(\omega_i,\overset{>}{\Theta}) < I(\omega_j,\overset{>}{\Theta})$\\ 
$\quad \implies \quad \invpi(\omega_i \in \Theta) > \invpi(\omega_j \in \Theta) \quad \lor \quad I(\omega_i,\Theta) < I(\omega_j, \Theta) \quad \forall i,j \in \mathbb{N}$\\
$\land \quad \psi_\omega \cong  \psi(\Theta) \cong \overset{>}{\Theta}$\\
$\land \quad \invpi(\psi(\Theta)) = \invpi(\overset{>}{\Theta}) \leq \invpi(\Theta)$
$\qed$
\end{transf}
\vspace{1em}


\end{defn}
\vspace{2em}
 
 
The beauty of that definition is that it not only presents us with a formula for how the MSS is expressed (\textbf{\hyperref[EQMSS]{Equation \ref{EQMSS}}}) but also how we might go about implementing either algebraic functions or computer programs that actually implement the MSS or that can compute it given some sequence (see \textbf{\hyperref[TRANSMSSGENb]{Transformer \ref{TRANSMSSGENb}}}). As for the differences between BMSS and PMSS mentioned above, the key points are that after obtaining the unique terms ($\psi(\Theta)$) in any sequence $\Theta$ such that $\invpi(\psi(\Theta)) \leq \invpi(\Theta) > 1$ --- which means that if we have more than one item in the sequence, then it might be that there are also multiple distinct items we need to process so as to establish the most frequent in the sequence --- which is how we then arrive at the MSS as specified in  \textbf{\hyperref[TRANSMSSGENb]{Transformer \ref{TRANSMSSGENb}}}. The 2025 treatise on magick, \textbf{Grimoire Lumtauto}\cite{lutalo_2025_grimlumtauto}, did give the matter a fair treatment, and presents a method of reducing which item in $\psi(\Theta)$ must occur earliest in the MSS, via the computation of a measure called the ``MSS rank". It is this rank measure that makes the BSS somewhat different from the PMSS, and that, for these two approaches --- which might sometimes produce similar results, but not necessarily always the same (see \textbf{Section A.3} in \cite{lutalo_2025_grimlumtauto}) --- the computation of the Rank, $R_i$ corresponding to some term $\omega_i \in \Theta$, we have the following two methods, one for BMSS (which also is used in \texttt{u:} and \texttt{u!:}) and the other for the PMSS (used in \texttt{u.:} and \texttt{u!.:}):


\vspace{2em}
BMSS Rank Function:
\vspace{1em}
\begin{multline}
\label{EQTNBMSSRANK}
Rank(\theta_i \in \psi(\Theta): > f_i, < i^*) = R_i \implies R_i = -f_i + \epsilon \times i^*
\end{multline}
\vspace{1em}


PMSS Rank Function:
\vspace{1em}
\begin{multline}
\label{EQTNPMSSRANK}
Rank(\theta_i \in \psi(\Theta): > f_i, < \breve{i}) = R_i \implies R_i = (\invpi(\psi(\Theta)) - \breve{i}) \times f_i
\end{multline}
\vspace{2em}


So, that's about it for the underlying mathematical theories, and so, back to the computer science...

\vspace{2em}

Uniquefying things is critical and primitive in TEA. Outputs of the U-command space can help in making critical decisions about things just by looking at their ordered base elements. For example, it is a common fact that in the English language, the letter ``e" occurs the most across all words in a typical lexicon\footnote{In fact, it is very interesting to note that there is a general agreement about which letters occur the most frequent in most samples of English writing, and these typically are in the order $\langle E, T, A, O, I, N, S, R, H, D, ..., Q, J, Z\rangle$ with corresponding approximate frequencies (as percentages) as  respectively $\langle 13\%, 9\%, 8\%, 7.7\%, 7.3\%, 6.9\%, 6.3\%, 6\%, 5.9\%, 4.3\%, ..., 0.1\%, 0.5\%, 0.07\% \rangle$\cite{copilot_assistant}.}. Now, such interesting analysis can be done primitively in TEA using the U-commands---especially because they not only tell us what the unique elements in a string are, but also which ones are most common, which ones rarest. Very useful foundational statistical analysis for free and that so many kinds of statistical processing problems become simpler to reason about using TEA.


\vspace{2em}

\fbox{\begin{minipage}{\textwidth}

\textbf{IMPORTANT:}\\

Note that, while working on the TAZ, a parallel and important project emerged, concerning exploring applications of Transformatics (the mathematics underlying the TEA language) in Esoteric Science, and it was discovered, there is a genuine need for an alternative way to compute the MSS. Thus, the extra variations in this command, that use the PMSS algorithm that relies on computing a rank/weight for each symbol instead of just relying on their frequency and order of first-occurrence as the original U: commands behave. There are some useful applications for this alternative as demonstrated in \cite{lutalo_2025_grimlumtauto}. 

\end{minipage}}
\vspace{2em}


A basic example of how to use \texttt{U:} is the problem of determining the winner at an election given the votes data. In this example TEA program, we assume a vote for candidate represented by letter A, appears as just the letter ``A" in the list of votes, for candidate B, by ``B", and so on. Thus, a list of votes from a polling station, for 3 candidates W, C and A can be represented by the string ``AWCCAWAWAAAAACCWACCCCWCACCA". The following TEA program then, would help automatically rank the candidates by their votes, with the winner appearing first in the result. 



\vspace{1em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=U: EXAMPLE 1: Tallying Votes (Democratic Sorting), breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
I!:{AWCCAWAWAAAAACCWACCCCWCACCA} | u!:
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}


Should return exactly ``ACW" and not ``CAW" because although A and C each have 11 votes and W has only 5, A occurs before C in the input. Essentially computes the modal sequence statistic of the input. A proof demonstration of this TEA program can be obtained using the ``-d" DEBUG flag to the TEA interpreter TTTT when being invoked with the TEA program as shown in the screenshot below:

\vspace{2em}

\begin{figure}[H]
  \begin{tcolorbox}[myterminalstyle, title=TEA Applied to Tallying Votes]
  \begin{lstlisting}
 
EXPERIMENTS|< 13:56:15 $>* tttt -c "I!:{AWCCAWAWAAAAACCWACCCCWCACCA} | u!:"
ACW
EXPERIMENTS|< 13:56:21 $>* tttt -c "I!:{AWCCAWAWAAAAACCWACCCCWCACCA} | u!:" -d
No explicit INPUT found, using STDIN!
INPUT:
 None
CODE:
 I!:{AWCCAWAWAAAAACCWACCCCWCACCA} | u!:
---------[ IN TEA RUNTIME ]

#2 of ['I!:{AWCCAWAWAAAAACCWACCCCWCACCA} ', ' u!:']
##2 of ['I!:{AWCCAWAWAAAAACCWACCCCWCACCA} ', 'u!:']
CLEAN TEA CODE TO PROCESS:
I!:{AWCCAWAWAAAAACCWACCCCWCACCA}
u!:

---<< EXTRACTED TEA LABEL BLOCKS:
{}

Executing Instruction#0 (out of 2)
Processing Instruction: I!:{AWCCAWAWAAAAACCWACCCCWCACCA}
PRIOR MEMORY STATE: (=, VAULTS:{})
RESULTANT MEMORY STATE: (=AWCCAWAWAAAAACCWACCCCWCACCA, VAULTS:{})
Executing Instruction#1 (out of 2)
Processing Instruction: u!:
PRIOR MEMORY STATE: (=AWCCAWAWAAAAACCWACCCCWCACCA, VAULTS:{})
--[util]-| Computing Unique Character Projection for [AWCCAWAWAAAAACCWACCCCWCACCA]
--[util]-| Unique Char Tally [[('A', 11), ('C', 11), ('W', 5)]]
RESULTANT MEMORY STATE: (=ACW, VAULTS:{})
ACW

             
  \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
  \caption{TEA EXAMPLE: demonstrating use of \texttt{U!:} for ``democratic sorting" on the command-line}
\end{figure}


Note that the example TEA program, because of its use of the special BASH character ``!" \cite{FSF2023Bash}, requires some clever treatment of the TEA source code when being used on the command-line. Thus, the actual command-line invocation of this example might sometimes call for use of the following modified TTTT invocation syntax:

\vspace{2em}

 \begin{tcolorbox}[myterminalstyle, title=TEA Applied to Tallying Votes (Alternative CLI Invocation Syntax)]
  \begin{lstlisting}
 
tttt -c "I!"":{AWCCAWAWAAAAACCWACCCCWCACCA}|u!":
             
  \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{2em}


Otherwise would raise Bash-runtime errors such as: \hl{-bash: ": unrecognized history modifier}

\vspace{2em}

The work-around is to first disable history modifier functionality by running command ``set +H", then the code can be entered normally as:


\vspace{2em}

 \begin{tcolorbox}[myterminalstyle, title=TEA Applied to Tallying Votes (Normal CLI Invocation Syntax)]
  \begin{lstlisting}
 
tttt -c "I!:{AWCCAWAWAAAAACCWACCCCWCACCA} | u!:"
             
  \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{2em}

Otherwise, another great example is the following, this time demonstrating how u: can serve the useful purpose of computing a modal sequence statistic for a list of words.


\vspace{1em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=U: EXAMPLE 2: Compute MSS for list of words, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
#compute and return modal sequence statistic of the provided words list
v:vI:{CAT DOG CAT FISH CAT COW COW FISH COW DUCK} | u*:vI
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}


That program should return ``CAT COW FISH DOG DUCK", which helps us tell that the word ``CAT" occurs most frequent, or at least earliest in the provided list, while ``DUCK" occurs least number of times, or last in the original input.


\vspace{2em}


In essence, both \texttt{u:} and \texttt{u!:} compute the important, modal sequence statistic of the provided input. For those unfamiliar with the statistic or why it is important, consult Lutalo’s mathematical research paper\cite{transformatics} on transformatics and sequence statistics for details.

 
\section{EXAMPLE APPLICATIONS of U:}
\label{SECEXAMPU}

  
Especially because the \textbf{U}-command space is about statistical processing of sequences, we shall want to explore some applications that might leverage this. Particularly, reducing sequences to their unique projections based on some criteria such as with words or letters might find some applications in various problems, some of them perhaps merely of a creative kind, but nonetheless worth bringing up as we see in the following sections.



\subsection{EXAMPLE 1: \textbf{COMPUTATIONAL MYSTICISM:} MAGICAL VOWEL HYMN GENERATOR}
\label{SECEXAMP1U}


The following application is inspired by and intimately related to an idea first explored by Ugandan Metal Band \textbf{MAZERA} on their 2025 \textbf{XMas Metal} album. Particularly, that album featured a work known as ``Vowel Hymns" --- see: [audio] \url{https://t.me/mazeraworks/1152} and [audio-video] \url{https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=p_THWo4MjIU}, which among other exciting things involved a song made of only singing vowels! This idea is also somewhat related to the related idea of \textit{sigilization} as employed in and rigorously explored in \textbf{Grimoire Lumtauto}\cite{lutalo_2025_grimlumtauto}. Particularly, even though ``normal" sigils are meant for expression as pictures (such as heavily demonstrated in that book), there are some cases where a sigil is meant to be only expressed in other ways such as with sounds --- mantras, conjurations, \textit{aural sigils} etc. One such creative case then, is when a message is reduced not to its ``magical source" as is presented in that book, but is instead reduced to or mapped to alternate forms such as its ``vowel source" --- like the magical source, could be based on the message's MSS, and unlike traditional sigilization protocols (such as those of \textbf{Austin O. Spare}) that eliminate all vowels from a message meant to be sigilized, this approach does the reverse --- eliminates all consonants so that, the resulting message is merely a sequence of pure sounds --- results in so-called \textit{angelic mantras}. Such is the logic or theory underlying vowel hymns. 


\vspace{2em}

The following program then, is based on that idea of reducing an inpute sequence to just its ``vowel source" --- the result is a very tiny string because we operate on the MSS (itself already a reduction of the possibly verbose message to just its statistical summary).
 

   \vspace{1em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=TEA Application: MAGICAL VOWEL SOURCE GENERATOR, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
###| MAGICAL VOWEL SOURCE  |###
#################################
#Yes! Like Mantra/Aural-Sigils
#Stuff you can sing or chant
#Using only vowels, and which
#can make magical things happen!
#Examples in GRIMOIRE LUMTAUTO:
#https://bit.ly/grimoirelumtauto
#################################

i:{***your message or lyrics should go here***}
d!:[0-9a-z-A-z]
r!:0:O | r!:1:I #replace some number with letters
z: #loa
u!.: #compute PMSS
d!:[aeiou] #reduce to only vowels
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
      \captionof{figure}{The VOWEL SOURCE generator}
  \label{FIGTEAVOWELSRC}

\vspace{1em}
  

If we apply that program to a common hymn phrase such as the first line of the traditional Christmas Carol hymn ``Joy to the World" --- i.e. the line ``Joy to the world! the Lord is come!" --- we shall simply obtain the output ``oei"! 

\vspace{2em}

If we apply that program to the first line of the Uganda national anthem (reproduced in full in \textbf{Chapter 10} of \cite{lutalo2025rock}), we have the sentence ``Oh Uganda! May God uphold thee" reduced to just ``oaue"!

\vspace{2em}

So, clearly, it starts to look like, for the case actually generating a hymn (as opposed to say just an aural sigil or mantra), we might need to have a program capable of giving us a longer output --- because, well, who wants to just sing just 5 or less letters over and over again! And thus, first, we consider another vowel hymn generator program, also currently part of the TEA standard programs, that doesn't process any input sequences, but does produce compelling long pieces as we shall soon see. The essential code for this other vowel hymn program is as such:


  \vspace{1em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=TEA Application: RANDOM VOWEL HYMN GENERATOR v1, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
#############################################
# RANDOM VOWEL HYMN GENERATOR v1
#############################################
# Though legit vowel hymns ought be generated
# based on transforming the text of legit hymns
# or words, this generator instead generates 
#such hymns based on nothing really, but the
# outputs shall read and feel like legitimate
#vowel hymns nonetheless.
#-------------------------------------------
# For Theory: https://bit.ly/grimoirelumtauto
# For Example: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=p_THWo4MjIU
##############################################
v:vLINES:5 | v:vI:0 | v:vHYMN:{}
v:vLIMIT:10 | v:vGLUE:{ } | v:vALPHABET:{aeiou }
l:LOOP
p*!:vLIMIT:vGLUE:vALPHABET | v:vLINE #generate and store random string
g*:{
}:vHYMN:vLINE | v:vHYMN #add it to the hymn
y:vI | x!:{+1} | r.: | v:vI 
y:vI | x!:{>} | x*!:vLINES | r.: | f:^true$:FIN:LOOP #test if we must finish
l:FIN | y:vHYMN | k!:^$ #return clean hymn
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
      \captionof{figure}{A RANDOM VOWEL HYMN GENERATOR v1}
  \label{FIGTEAVOWELHYMNGEN}

\vspace{1em}


Note that the program is essentially what we already covered well in \textbf{\hyperref[SECEXAMP1P]{Section \ref{SECEXAMP1P}}}, and that we see several examples of what it can produce in that section too. However, for the case of generating vowel hymns based on some input text, we might instead want to consider a program such as:

\vspace{2em}


  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=TEA Application: A VOWEL RANDOM HYMN ENCODER v1, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
#############################################
# VOWEL RANDOM HYMN ENCODER v1
#############################################
# Shall generate a random vowel hymn based on 
# transforming the input text.
# outputs shall read and feel like 
# legitimate vowel hymns.
##############################################

v:vINPUT #grab the input
# generate vowel source from it..
d!:[0-9a-z-A-z]
r!:0:O | r!:1:I #replace some number with letters
z: #loa
u!.: #compute PMSS
d!:[aeiou] #reduce to only vowels
v:vVOWELSRC # store computed vowel source

v:vLINES:5 | v:vI:0 | v:vHYMN:{}
v:vLIMIT:10 | v:vGLUE:{ } | 

# use existing vowel source as hymn alphabet...
y:vVOWELSRC | v:vALPHABET
# add a single space char to the alphabet though...
y:vALPHABET | x!:{ } | v:vALPHABET

# proceed to generate associated hymn
l:LOOP
p*!:vLIMIT:vGLUE:vALPHABET | v:vLINE #generate and store random string
g*:{
}:vHYMN:vLINE | v:vHYMN #add it to the hymn
y:vI | x!:{+1} | r.: | v:vI 
y:vI | x!:{>} | x*!:vLINES | r.: | f:^true$:FIN:LOOP #test if we must finish
l:FIN | y:vHYMN | k!:^$ #return clean hymn
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
      \captionof{figure}{A VOWEL RANDOM HYMN ENCODER v1}
  \label{FIGTEAVOWELHYMNENCODE}

\vspace{1em}


Interestingly, and perhaps making sense, that last program does combine the earlier two programs so that, given some input, it uses the input's corresponding vowel source to generate a random vowel hymn. Thus, for the earlier example of \textbf{processing just the first line Christmas Carol} ``Joy to The World", we would obtain vowel hymns such as the following:


\vspace{2em}
  
   \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample Vowel Hymn OUTPUT 1]
ioieee o o
 ii eoioe
eeeei o ei
ee ooii i 
eoooieoeoo
 iii oeeoi
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 

\vspace{2em}


\vspace{2em}
  
   \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample Vowel Hymn OUTPUT 2]
eie eeo ii
 oi eeoei
io oee i e
eiio e oi 
oeeoe eoee
 iei e oii
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 

\vspace{2em}


And for the case of processing the first line of the Uganda national anthem, we would obtain hymns such as...



\vspace{2em}
  
   \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample Vowel Hymn OUTPUT 3]
ue ao ee a
oeueo e u
 ueaa uae 
eue auae e
ueaauouuoa
eoeeee auu
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 

\vspace{2em}


\vspace{2em}
  
   \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample Vowel Hymn OUTPUT 4]
uouau u uo
eo aouu
 ooaeeoe 
 o uueooo
 oauueeoeo
oo e aeo o
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 

\vspace{2em}

Finally, note that unlike the above examples that somewhat operate on the statistical summary of the input --- essentially the MSS, that we might still be able to generate a vowel hymn from some input text, with just a very minimal TEA technique --- selective filtering. In this case for example, we might merely take the input sequence and kickout just the consonants such as we see in the following little program:



  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=TEA Application: VOWEL HYMN TRANSFORMER v1, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
#############################################
# VOWEL HYMN TRANSFORMER v1
#############################################
# Shall generate a vowel hymn based on 
# transforming the input text.
# Outputs is a vowel hymn that reflects
# the structure of the input text.
# Example: try applying it to UGANDA anthem!
##############################################

d:[bBcCdDfFgGhHjJkKlLmMnNpPqQrRsStTvVwWxXyYzZ]
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
      \captionof{figure}{VOWEL HYMN TRANSFORMER v1}
  \label{FIGTEAVOWELHYMNTRANS}

\vspace{1em}


Which, if we apply it to the complete Uganda national anthem, whose text is specified in \cite{lutalo2025rock}, we would obtain the following very interesting vowel hymn --- a \textbf{UGANDA National Anthem Vowel Hymn}!



\vspace{2em}
  
   \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=UGANDA National Vowel Anthem]
O Uaa! a o uo ee,
e a ou uue i  a.
Uie, ee,
o ie
oee e' aa a.

O Uaa! e a o eeo,
Ou oe a aou e ie.
A i eiou a,
A ou ou' a
I eae a iei e' ie.

O Uaa! e a a ee u,
 u a eie oi o.
o ou o ea a,
e' aa a;
e ea o Aia' o.
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 

\vspace{2em}


\fbox{
\begin{minipage}{0.9\textwidth}
\textbf{INTERNET Community Challenge:} \textit{Sing the UGANDA National Vowel Anthem!}\\

Following the interesting discovery of this strange projection of the Uganda national anthem into a vowel hymn, a call was made on various ICs for especially Ugandan netizens to go explore and experience for themselves, what it feels like singing their national anthem as a vowel hymn. 


In case you are likewise interested, go checkout the call talkshow via: \url{https://youtube.com/shorts/8yUa_oPgqfU} and for those that need an accompanying instrumental version of the anthem so they can sing their vowel version alongside the standard anthem tune, the links to the audio are 

\begin{itemize}
\item (YouTube: audio-video) \url{https://youtube.com/shorts/X025Pw6pDCs}
\item (UIC: audio) \url{https://t.me/ugandanow/5665}
\end{itemize}

\end{minipage}}
