  %\caption{TEA DEBUGGER: Debugging a TEA program involving Memory Read-Modify-Write operations}
\vspace{2em}



\subsubsection{HTTP POST with RAW/ARBITRARY non-FORM DATA}
\label{SECHTTPPOSTDATA}


In the previous sections, we have discussed and explored the typical HTTP POST scenarios --- particularly, when, either via literal HTML-based forms or via programmatic methods such as using either JavaScript, TEA or any language or tool (such as Linux/Unix command \texttt{curl}) with a web interaction interface, we use a non-interactive or non-graphical method to build a payload that we then forward to some resource over the network as an HTTP post message. In these cases, the conventional method is to structure any such message as a collection of one or more key-value parameters that are typically encoded as HTML form data --- using the special HTTP header \texttt{Content-Type:application/x-www-form-urlencoded}

\vspace{2em}


However, and as we have already encountered in some of the examples above, there are [genuine] situations when the programmer wishes to send data to some server, service or REST API endpoint as nothing but form-encoded data. In such cases, such as in the way data payloads meant to be processed by a web service such as AHAsend.com\footnote{A full example is included in \textbf{\hyperref[SECEXAMP1W]{Section \ref{SECEXAMP1W}}}.} (that allows programs to use its WEB APIs to send emails) must be sent with not only special HTTP headers such as for authorization, but that the way the data (such as the e-mail message package) to be processed by the service must be pre-formatted in a special/pre-defined way --- such as JSON, XML, CSV, etc. and that in many such cases, also the standard/conventional data-type header ``Content-Type" used when posting the data must be explicitly modified.

\vspace{2em} 

In such cases, TEA's \textbf{WEBIFY} command space has provided a special signature of the \texttt{W:} primitive that would allow TEA programs to make an HTTP POST in which the payload is sent as a verbatim/raw string/text as-is, and with the default content type set to ``application/json" given many such cases involve sending data to a WEB API processing JSON, but with the option to set custom content-type still available when required. As indicated in \textbf{\hyperref[TABSEMW]{Table \ref{TABSEMW}}}, the necessary signature for HTTP DATA POST is \texttt{w*@:vURL:vDATA}, and the following two examples --- one posting using normal method \texttt{W*!:}, and the other using this non-Form method should serve well to demonstrate the differences.


  \vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=W: EXAMPLE 6: A Simple HTTP POST - using normal Form-based POSTING, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
# first, set data we'll post
v:vDATA:{{
"message": "hello world",
"id": 101
}}

# make normal form-based http post...
v:vURL:http://httpbin.org/post
w*!:vURL:vDATA
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}


That example, as we see from the output of the useful echo output from HTTPBIN.org, proves that, even even though our data was a JSON payload for example, we have it posted as form-encoded data...


 \vspace{2em}
 \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 6 from EXAMPLE 6]
{
  "args": {}, 
  "data": "", 
  "files": {}, 
  "form": {
    "vDATA": "{\n\"message\": \"hello world\",\n\"id\": 101\n}"
  }, 
  "headers": {
    "Accept": "*/*", 
    "Accept-Encoding": "gzip, deflate", 
    "Accept-Language": "en-US,en;q=0.9", 
    "Content-Length": "73", 
    "Content-Type": "application/x-www-form-urlencoded", 
    "Host": "httpbin.org", 
    "Origin": "http://localhost", 
    "Referer": "http://localhost/", 
    "User-Agent": "Mozilla/5.0 (Windows NT 10.0; Win64; x64) AppleWebKit/537.36 (KHTML, like Gecko) Chrome/146.0.0.0 Safari/537.36", 
    "X-Amzn-Trace-Id": "Root=1-69ca5d70-2dc50bc12ba1de110950d5da"
  }, 
  "json": null, 
  "origin": "102.85.239.169", 
  "url": "http://httpbin.org/post"
}
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 \vspace{2em} 


Whereas, if we switch to the raw-data/non-form http post method, we have the following slightly modified TEA program...



  \vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=W: EXAMPLE 7: A Simple HTTP POST - using non-Form-based POSTING, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
# first, set data we'll post
v:vDATA:{{
"message": "hello world",
"id": 101
}}

# make non-form-based http post...
v:vURL:http://httpbin.org/post
w*@:vURL:vDATA
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

And so that the output, still from the same HTTP echo service, is as ..


 \vspace{2em}
 \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 7 from EXAMPLE 7]
{
  "args": {}, 
  "data": "{\n\"message\": \"hello world\",\n\"id\": 101\n}", 
  "files": {}, 
  "form": {}, 
  "headers": {
    "Accept": "*/*", 
    "Accept-Encoding": "gzip, deflate", 
    "Accept-Language": "en-US,en;q=0.9", 
    "Content-Length": "39", 
    "Content-Type": "application/json", 
    "Host": "httpbin.org", 
    "Origin": "http://localhost", 
    "Referer": "http://localhost/", 
    "User-Agent": "Mozilla/5.0 (Windows NT 10.0; Win64; x64) AppleWebKit/537.36 (KHTML, like Gecko) Chrome/146.0.0.0 Safari/537.36", 
    "X-Amzn-Trace-Id": "Root=1-69ca5e92-5d8f23f025a8c3557429493d"
  }, 
  "json": {
    "id": 101, 
    "message": "hello world"
  }, 
  "origin": "102.85.239.169", 
  "url": "http://httpbin.org/post"
}
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 \vspace{2em} 


Thus, we see that, with \texttt{W*@:}, not only that the data payload is sent verbatim, but that even without having to explicitly set a custom HTTP header for content type, the TEA command defaults to a useful ``application/json". Moreover, in case the data we were sending was of some other type other than JSON --- such as plain text or perhaps CSV, we can likewise add the necessary header and still use the non-form WEBIFY as such:


 \vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=W: EXAMPLE 8: A Simple HTTP POST - using non-Form-based POSTING, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
# first, set data we'll post
v:vDATA:{
id, message
101, hello world
102, this is a test
103, 
110, final csv test
}

# make non-form-based http post of CSV data
v:Content-Type:text/csv
w*.:Content-Type #sets the type header..

v:vURL:http://httpbin.org/post
w*@:vURL:vDATA
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

And so that the output, still from the same HTTP echo service, is as ..


 \vspace{2em}
 \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 8 from EXAMPLE 8]
{
  "args": {}, 
  "data": "\nid, message\n101, hello world\n102, this is a test\n103, \n110, final csv test\n", 
  "files": {}, 
  "form": {}, 
  "headers": {
    "Accept": "*/*", 
    "Accept-Encoding": "gzip, deflate", 
    "Accept-Language": "en-US,en;q=0.9", 
    "Content-Length": "76", 
    "Content-Type": "text/csv", 
    "Host": "httpbin.org", 
    "Origin": "http://localhost", 
    "Referer": "http://localhost/", 
    "User-Agent": "Mozilla/5.0 (Windows NT 10.0; Win64; x64) AppleWebKit/537.36 (KHTML, like Gecko) Chrome/146.0.0.0 Safari/537.36", 
    "X-Amzn-Trace-Id": "Root=1-69ca64c9-4efab8b4797d7bc175c4966b"
  }, 
  "json": null, 
  "origin": "102.85.239.169", 
  "url": "http://httpbin.org/post"
}
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 \vspace{2em} 

  

And with that final example, we thus wrap-ip our discussions concerning TEA WEBIFY POST commands... \texttt{W!:}, \texttt{W*!:}, and \texttt{W*@:} that enable HTTP POST operations such as sending data in vaults to a remote server or endpoint via anywhere but in the URL --- this later case being what the following section then covers.


\subsection{HTTP GET Requests}

Next, let us look at the TEA mechanisms for making \textbf{HTTP GET requests}. The following example demonstrates this facility using the interesting \texttt{W*:} that knows to post everything in the vaults via HTTP GET automatically!


  \vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=W: EXAMPLE 9: A Simple HTTP GET - real - with no explicit parameters, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
v:A:123|v:vTest:{some value}|i:http://httpbin.org/get|w*:
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}


Which shall return the following...


  
 \vspace{2em}
 \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 9 from EXAMPLE 9]
{
  "args": {
    "A": "123",
    "vTest": "some value"
  },
  "headers": {
    "Accept-Encoding": "identity",
    "Host": "httpbin.org",
    "User-Agent": "Python-urllib/3.10",
    "X-Amzn-Trace-Id": "Root=1-66c83059-040326d238fdaff202c56b4c"
  },
  "origin": "41.210.147.213",
  "url": "http://httpbin.org/get?A=123&vTest=some+value"
}
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 \vspace{2em} 
 
Which should help to illustrate that with the TEA HTTP GET interface that reads vaults --- \texttt{W*:} --- that, even though the AI only contains the base url --- in this case just \texttt{http://httpbin.org/get}, and yet, the call builds a dynamic URL for the request using all available parameters in the calling program's memory!

\vspace{2em}

Of course, once the data-privacy concerns start to an issue, but also the fact that for TEA programs with very many or very long data in the vaults, the automatically constructed HTTP GET url could become unwieldy, and so that, the careful program should instead opt for versions such as \texttt{W*:vURL}, and especially use \texttt{W*:vURL:v1:v2:...:vLASTPARAM} so that only the specified vaults are used to construct the effective url's query parameters.

\vspace{2em}

And then, concerning the vault-less versions of WEBIFY, we shall only look at one example and then move on.


\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=W: EXAMPLE 10: A Simple HTTP GET - vault-less calls, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:https://freeipapi.com/api/json/ | w:
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}


That TEA program essentially just takes the AI when W: is invoked, uses it as the URL and performs an HTTP GET to that endpoint, returning the result as IO. On the command-line/with CLI TEA/tttt, we see this work out as such:


\vspace{2em}
 \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=W: EXAMPLE 11: Testing WEB functionality on the Command-line]
tttt -c "i!:https://freeipapi.com/api/json/|w:"
{"ipVersion":4,"ipAddress":"41.210.143.35","latitude":-0.07722000000000001,"longitude":31.4567,"countryName":"Uganda","countryCode":"UG","capital":"Kampala","phoneCodes":[256],"timeZones":["Africa\/Kampala"],"zipCode":"-","cityName":"Sembabule","regionName":"Central Region","continent":"Africa","continentCode":"AF","currencies":["UGX"],"languages":["en","sw"],"asn":"20294","asnOrganization":"MTN Uganda","isProxy":false}
  \end{tcbverbatim}
\vspace{2em}


However, and this is important to note concerning HTTP calls to various endpoints; sometimes, some endpoints either block some particular ORIGIN DOMAINs or CLIENTs. And thus, the same code above, when run on the WEB TEA instead results in the following error:


  
 \vspace{2em}
 \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 11]
[ERROR]: NetworkError: Failed to execute 'send' on 'XMLHttpRequest': Failed to load 'https://freeipapi.com/api/json/'.
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 \vspace{2em}


Moreover, currently, TEA's WEB functions attempt to perform \textbf{synchronous HTTP calls} first of all, but also, TEA doesn't yet allow the user to modify or set any special HTTP request headers when using the W: utilities\footnote{\textbf{FUTURE WORK:} More research and experimentation is due concerning how best to implement/design this TEA instruction/command-space. Stay on the look-out for future advances...}. 


\subsubsection{HTTP GET with CUSTOM HEADERS}


For the case of using custom headers with TEA HTTP calls, the method we have already encountered in \textbf{\hyperref[SECHTTPPOSTHEADERS]{Section \ref{SECHTTPPOSTHEADERS}}} still holds. However, we can readily tell how it would work, with the following basic example:


  \vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=W: EXAMPLE 12: A Simple HTTP GET -  with Custom HTTP Headers, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
# first, set some vaults and use them to set custom http headers
v:vTEST:hello
v:Content-Type:text/plain
v:Authorization:{Bearer 12345APIKEYblablah...}

# only sets specified vaults as custom headers to future http calls
w*.:Authorization:vTEST 

v:A:123|v:vTest:{some value}|i:http://httpbin.org/get|w*:
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}


Which, as you can tell from the following output, does exactly what the code wanted the program to do --- in this case, we ensure to only specify two particular custom headers.


 \vspace{2em}
 \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 12 from EXAMPLE 12]
{
  "args": {
    "A": "123",
    "Authorization": "Bearer 12345APIKEYblablah...",
    "Content-Type": "text/plain",
    "vTEST": "hello",
    "vTest": "some value"
  },
  "headers": {
    "Accept-Encoding": "identity",
    "Authorization": "Bearer 12345APIKEYblablah...",
    "Host": "httpbin.org",
    "User-Agent": "Python-urllib/3.10",
    "Vtest": "hello",
    "X-Amzn-Trace-Id": "Root=1-69c1517e-0619c4e62c9286213dcbe5ad"
  },
  "origin": "41.210.155.247",
  "url": "http://httpbin.org/get?vTEST=hello&Content-Type=text%2Fplain&Authorization=Bearer+12345APIKEYblablah...&A=123&vTest=some+value"
}
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 \vspace{2em} 
 

Also, and worth noting here --- that, even though by normal convention, we typically write TEA VAULTS using the naming convention \textbf{vNAME}, such as we have done in this example for the custom header \texttt{vTEST}, however, when the vaults are to be used to specify HTTP headers, this convention needs be relaxed, so that we use the exact header name required --- such as when we set the API-authentication header via \texttt{v:Authorization:{Bearer 12345APIKEYblablah...}}.

\vspace{2em}

And then, finally, concerning the case when we had to set up custom headers such as in this example, but also the earlier one. Note that, when we use the WEBIFY methods that read all currently set vaults for the http call --- such as \texttt{w*:} in this case, that even the vaults that we had set for the custom headers shall get into the data processed by the call! This can be dangerous when the headers contained some sensitive data such as keys, etc. Thus, the following example would be safer..



  \vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=W: EXAMPLE 13: A Simple HTTP GET -  with Custom HTTP Headers, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
# first, set some vaults and use them to set custom http headers
v:vTEST:hello
v:Content-Type:text/plain
v:Authorization:{Bearer 12345APIKEYblablah...}

# only sets specified vaults as custom headers to future http calls
w*.:Authorization:vTEST 

v:A:123|v:vTest:{some value}|i:http://httpbin.org/get
v:vURL

# make HTTP GET call with explicitly specified parameters (safer)
w*:vURL:A:vTest
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

Which for example prevents the custom headers from leaking into the HTTP query parameters as you can tell from the following output...



 \vspace{2em}
 \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 13 from EXAMPLE 13]
{
  "args": {
    "A": "123", 
    "vTest": "some value"
  }, 
  "headers": {
    "Accept": "*/*", 
    "Accept-Encoding": "gzip, deflate", 
    "Accept-Language": "en-US,en;q=0.9", 
    "Authorization": "Bearer 12345APIKEYblablah...", 
    "Host": "httpbin.org", 
    "Origin": "http://localhost", 
    "Referer": "http://localhost/", 
    "User-Agent": "Mozilla/5.0 (Windows NT 10.0; Win64; x64) AppleWebKit/537.36 (KHTML, like Gecko) Chrome/146.0.0.0 Safari/537.36", 
    "Vtest": "hello", 
    "X-Amzn-Trace-Id": "Root=1-69c15674-2589468b1d2a11cb0cefd952"
  }, 
  "origin": "41.210.155.247", 
  "url": "http://httpbin.org/get?A=123&vTest=some+value"
}
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 \vspace{2em} 
 


 
\section{EXAMPLE APPLICATIONS of W:}
\label{SECEXAMPW}

  
Among very ancient (read \textit{older}) ideas and memes circulated around and begrudgingly curated by both hackers and general programmers in the computing and software development communities since the early days of the Internet and online communities is the following [generally unattributed] quote:


\begin{minipage}{1\textwidth}
\vspace{1em}
\begin{quotation}
{\ttfamily

The highest stage of any programming language or system is when it can send email.

}
\end{quotation}
\vspace{1em}
\end{minipage}

It is believed\cite{copilot_assistant} that very likely, this quote first appeared somewhere on \textbf{Usenet} or some such related early core-mailing list. Sometimes it also is phrased as:


\begin{minipage}{1\textwidth}
\vspace{1em}
\begin{quotation}
{\ttfamily

Every sufficiently advanced programming language eventually has an email library.

}
\end{quotation}
\vspace{1em}
\end{minipage}

The essential idea here, and which is somewhat related to estimating or judging a system's --- especially a programming language such as TEA, C++, Python or Ruby --- maturity, is that, just like most such technology platforms must demonstrate their usability and practicability via a simple application such as the famous ``Hello World"\cite{lutalo2023dnapsoe}, a program that merely show how to print a simple string and nothing else, for mature and advanced systems, the simple application that can send an email is the ultimate demonstration of the platform or system's power and readiness for real-world use.

\vspace{2em}
For the TEA language, we thus intend to demonstrate that all the bits necessary to make this happen, are essentially well catered for by a thoughtful use of the \textbf{WEBIFY} command space. Thus, we are going to go on a journey, an adventure in web programming and data communications, aimed at manifesting a minimal, but practically useful email client, that at minimum can allow us to compose and send an actual email message over the Internet, to any destination around the world.


\subsection{EXAMPLE 1: \textbf{DATA COMMUNICATION:} EMAIL POSTING SERVICE}
\label{SECEXAMP1W}


We shall implement a TEA program that can allow us to send text that is specified as the input to the program (or otherwise user-specified text at run-time), to a custom email address and with a custom message subject and a sender email address --- the basic ingredients for any SMTP recipe and any basic email payload.

\vspace{2em}

Especially because in TEA we do not have room to deal with bloated low-level algorithms, networking primitives and direct interface to external libraries, the method we are about to demonstrate shall essentially utilize existing WEB APIs for sending Emails --- via \textbf{RESTful}\footnote{A ``RESTful" API is one that follows the principles of REST (Representational State Transfer), an architectural style introduced by Roy Fielding in 2000 \cite{copilot_assistant}. It uses standard web protocols like HTTP, is stateless, and is designed to be simple, scalable, and efficient.} \textbf{Application Programming Interfaces}. Also, because most such APIs are typically either restricted or protected via a need for access-credentials and origin white-listing, etc.\footnote{Especially, and given that without such defenses, email can readily be abused so that hackers and especially criminals (black-hats) might use the services to send either SPAM e-mails or to thwart security and send phishing attacks, click-bait, scams, etc.}, we shall also demonstrates how setting custom [security] HTTP headers in TEA can be useful...


\vspace{2em}

\subsubsection{TEA E-EMAILER Source Code}


The basic application is going to be called \textbf{E-MAILER}, and the essential, fully-tested source-code for version 1.6 of our next standard TEA application is as such:

\vspace{2em}

{
 \Large
  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=TEA Program: E-MAILER v1.6, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
#!/usr/bin/tttt -fc
#######################################
v:vABOUT:{@@@@@@| E-MAILER v1.6 |@@@@@@
Is a basic solution that enables you to readily send an email over the Internet without requiring any other sophisticated email clients. It just works anywhere TEA works. However, we basically help send the email on your behalf, in collaboration with Austrian AHASend... Since 2026}
#######################################
# Copyright 2026 Fut. Prof. JWL, 
# Nuchwezi Research: Internet Services
#######################################

#---[CONSTANTS]
v:vEmailerFooter:{

@@@@@@[
 Sent using TEA E-Mailer v1.6 
]@@@@@@}
# first, store input user might have provided from outside this program
y*: | v:vExternalText
# basic menu/guide
v:vPROMPTBody:{Copy and Paste (or type-in: single-line) THE MESSAGE to be sent:}
v:vPROMPTSubject:{Copy and Paste (or type-in) THE SUBJECT for this email:}
v:vPROMPTTo:{Specify THE DESTINATION address for this email:}
v:vPROMPTFrom:{Specify THE ORIGIN/REPLY-to/CC address for this email:}
v:vDELIM:{
#-------------------
}
v:vPATTERN:{==_==}
v:vEMAIL_REGEX:{^[A-Za-z0-9._%+-]+@[A-Za-z0-9.-]+\.[A-Za-z]{2,}$}
v:vEMAILER_ADDRESS:{noreply@nuchwezi.com} # the actual sender

#---[VARIABLES]
v:vMailBody:{}
# initially, we want to use external input 
# as default email-body/message
y:vExternalText|t!.:|v:vMailBody

l:lRESET
v:vMailSubject:{}
v:vMailFrom:{}
v:vMailTo:{}
v:vACTIntercept:0

#---[INTRODUCTIONS]
l:lINTRO
y:vABOUT|i*:

#---[BUILD E-MAIL PAYLOAD]
#check if we already have the email message, otherwise prompt..

l:lPROMPTBody
# test for body using cardinality [regex] test instead of regex on potentially problematic text..
y:vMailBody|t!.:|v:|y!:
f!:^0$:lHASBody
y:vPROMPTBody | i*: | t!.: | v:vMailBody 
f:^$:lPROMPTBody:lHASBody

#we got the message
l:lHASBody
y:vMailBody | x*!:vEmailerFooter | v:vMailBody

# prompt for the subject..
l:lPROMPTSubject
y:vMailSubject|t!.:
f!:^$:lHASSubject
y:vPROMPTSubject | i*: | t!.: | v:vMailSubject 
f:^$:lPROMPTSubject:lHASSubject 

#we got the subject
l:lHASSubject 
y:vMailSubject|t!.:|z*:|v:vMailSubject

# prompt for the destination..
l:lPROMPTTo
y:vMailTo|t!.:
f*:vEMAIL_REGEX:lHASTo
y:vPROMPTTo | i*: | t!.: | v:vMailTo 
f*!:vEMAIL_REGEX:lPROMPTTo:lHASTo 

#we got the destination address
l:lHASTo 
y:vMailTo|t!.:|g:|v:vMailTo

# prompt for the origin/from/reply-to/cc..
l:lPROMPTFrom
y:vMailFrom|t!.:
f*:vEMAIL_REGEX:lHASFrom
y:vPROMPTFrom | i*: | t!.: | v:vMailFrom 
f*!:vEMAIL_REGEX:lPROMPTFrom:lHASFrom 

#we got the origin address
l:lHASFrom 
y:vMailFrom|t!.:|g:|v:vMailFrom

#---[PREVIEW E-MAIL PAYLOAD]
# we shall join the bits so that we see 
# what it all looks like before we send it out
v:vS:{SUBJECT: }|v:vT:{TO: }|v:vF:{FROM: }|v:vNL:{
}|v:vPREAMBLE:{Before Sending, Please CONFIRM:}
v:vMENU_ACTION:{
=------[ CHOSE NEXT ACTION ]------=
1. EXIT/return payload
2. SEND/returns payload + sends out e-email
3. SEND+COMPOSE/sends e-mail + start next e-mail
4. COMPOSE/discard + restart..}

g*:{}:vS:vMailSubject:vDELIM:vT:vMailTo:vDELIM:vF:vMailFrom:vDELIM:vMailBody | v:vEMAILPreview

g*!:vNL:vMENU_ACTION:vDELIM:vPREAMBLE:vDELIM:vEMAILPreview | v:vPREVIEW_PROMPT

l:lPromptAction
# present what we have...
y:vPREVIEW_PROMPT| i*: | t!.: | v:vOPT

#---[PROCESS E-MAIL PAYLOAD]
#process what action user-chose...
y:vOPT | f!:^[1234]$:lPromptAction
f!:1:lNACT1
y:vEMAILPreview | q!:

l:lNACT1
y:vOPT | f!:2:lNACT2
l:lSENDEMAIL
# send out the email...
# first, prepare essential parameters...
v:vURL_MAILSERVICE:{https://utility.nuchwezi.com/sendemail}
v:Content-Type:{application/json}

# build a JSON payload...
i!:{{}

# NOTE: AHAsend requires that people not be allowed to impersonate
# arbitrary origin/from email addresses. Thus, we shall have to 
# use ours as the sender --- until further notice!
#"from":{ "email":"hello@yourdomain.com"},
# BECAUSE of E-Mail Security, we shall offer
# to send on user's behalf - via our own address
x!:{"from":{ "email":"}| x*!:vEMAILER_ADDRESS| x!:{"},}
# or explicitly send as user-set from?
#x!:{"from":{ "email":"}| x*!:vMailFrom | x!:{"},}
# "to": [{"email": "user@example.com"}],
x!:{"to": [{"email": "}| x*!:vMailTo | x!:{"}],}
# As an Experimental feature, SENDER could be included in CC
# We might also configure explicit reply-to address??
# (exploring e-email routing options that is... )
x!:{"cc": [{"email": "}| x*!:vMailFrom | x!:{"}],}
x!:{"reply_to": {"email": "}| x*!:vMailFrom | x!:{"},}
# the rest of the parameters...
x!:{"subject":"}| x*!:vMailSubject | x!:{",}
x!:{"text_content":"}|

#we shall want to ensure we string-escape this value first..

v: #store current Payload in default vault

y:vMailBody | r!:{"}:{\"} | v:vMailBody_esc

# continue building payload...

y:

x*!:vMailBody_esc 

| x!:{"} | x!:"}"
v:vJSON_EMAILPayload

# FIX: found that most JSON validators can't accept literal new-lines
r!:{
}:{\n}
v:vJSON_EMAILPayload

#q!: # inspect payload??

# set http headers...
w*.:Content-Type
# perform the http JSON data POST call...
w*@:vURL_MAILSERVICE:vJSON_EMAILPayload

#q!: # check what we got from api??

# return email payload and quit?
y:vACTIntercept | f:1:lNACT3
y:vEMAILPreview | q!:

l:lNACT2
y:vOPT | f!:3:lNACT3
v:vACTIntercept:1
# go to send logic...
j:lSENDEMAIL

l:lNACT3
# discard and restart e-mail composition...
v:vMailBody:{}
j:lRESET
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
  \caption{TEA APPLICATION: E-MAILER v1.6 --- a simple and special E-Mail sending client written using the TEA language}
  \label{FIGWAPP1}
}


\subsubsection{TEA E-EMAILER In Action..}

That program is currently part of the TEA standard programs, and shall build payloads using user-provided data, after careful validation and pre-processing as such (this output was actually from an earlier version):


\vspace{2em}
 \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT from EMAILER v1.4]
SUBJECT: Hi There Buddy
#-------------------
TO: test@me.com
#-------------------
FROM: joe@nuchwezi.com
#-------------------
This is test

@@@@@@[ Sent using TEA E-Mailer v1.4 ]@@@@@@
  \end{tcbverbatim}
\vspace{2em}

Moreover, we are using a somewhat commercial and and still unfamiliar/under-development Email service for this --- \textbf{AHAsend.com} --- thus, user experiences might vary, and that perhaps we shall need more testing to see whether this is the correct WEB API for making our client work. Otherwise, and important to note concerning how we make this all work:

\begin{enumerate}
\item First, most standard E-Mail security protocols are strict about how SMTP services can be utilized; generally, sending emails via anonymous servers is restricted or flagged as SPAM, and most WEB APIs for email sending would not allow that.
\item To work-around such restrictions, we implemented our own email sending endpoint via \texttt{https://utility.nuchwezi.com/sendemail} that somewhat accepts a payload aligned with AHASend's v2 payloads for the \textbf{conversational messaging} --- such that, instead of allowing our users to directly send as any email address they wish, we instead send on their behalf (as \texttt{noreply@nuchwezi.com}), and instead use their \textbf{TO} and \textbf{FROM} as the \textbf{to} and \textbf{cc} of our AHASend payload. Also, starting with Emailer v1.6, we use the AhaSend v2 API parameter ``reply\_to", to use the user's FROM address as the \textbf{REPLY-TO} for the email we send, so that, even though we actually use our \texttt{noreply@nuchwezi.com} to send the email, the recipient can readily click ``Reply" in their email client, and it will work just as usual --- the response shall directly address the correct destination address and not our service address. Overall works pretty well, and is less likely to be abused or flagged as SPAM.\footnote{Tests have proven that this service is quite reliable, and that we might never need to implement our own SMTP (\textbf{Simple Mail Transfer Protocol}) processors as part of say the utility.nuchwezi.com service, nor switch to another provider other than the European/Austrian AhaSend.com}
\item The E-Mailer service then pretty-formats the out-going message, so that it contains some indication of where (EMAILER-footer message) it was sent from.
\end{enumerate}

\vspace{2em}

With the \textbf{Emailer v1.5} code as shared above (we all as in the links to be shared hereafter), an email sent using the service would look something like the following (stripped of the headers --- SUBJECT, TO and FROM, that actually don't appear as part of the email message itself):


\vspace{2em}

   \begin{figure}[H]
  \centering
  \begin{tcolorbox}[myterminalstyle, title=Sample OUTPUT from EMAILER v1.5]
  \begin{verbatim}
Dear Waltz,

Your mail reached me in bits..

TL;DR 

I recommend bit.ly/run_emailer

But FYI, here's an ACK;

There used to be a time
We passed time in rhyme..

We mostly gathered around
Scattered souls that ICs found
And quizzed each other;
Fantasies and phobias,
Vocabs, trodden hoods and such. 

Desmond from Rutgers Rite,
Lenin Mackenzie, a sloth,
Barbra Lee, she knew all the jokes,
Catan Su a stunning poet
and Jewels Poor, an overlord,
And several other folks
Previously amateurs on Discord.

But the ICs have changed!
Folks show up dressed in emoticons
Such as one brute that goes by 
A tranny 
And this nagging handle...
IA almost banned them from forum;
..(@/?__+!;#£#@)(.,.)

There's no transactions N'mo
It's kkk.. C, I C, U K! LMO
X-Twitter tweenz souls 
N' overall unnecessary panic!

Old sleuths still snail and verbose to make no point,
But we cherish Plato,
Miniature Michaelangelo's
and despirited Miltons when they can fit
Inside of a few MBs.

This is The State of The @;

@@@@@@[
 Sent using TEA E-Mailer v1.5
]@@@@@@
  \end{verbatim}
  \end{tcolorbox}
  \caption{An [emoticon-free] version of a special e-mail (a poem?) titled ``Dear Waltz", that demonstrates how mails sent using TEA Emailer v1.5 would look like.}
  \label{FIGWEXAv15}
\end{figure}

\vspace{2em}

That example was chosen partly for its quirkiness too! The actual, verbatim and original version of that email message (originally written via a mobile/smart-phone\footnote{In preparing this book, bits of that email message, such as the lines containing the weird user names expressed using only emoticons, could not even be processed well in our book typesetting environments LaTex/XeLatex/LuaTex and neither could powerful and mature word processors such as Microsoft Word nor Google Docs render them! Thus, the other/alternative rendering shared as a screenshot hereafter, demonstrates how, even though the mail message were readily processed via TEA Emailer, and delivered, we had to taken a screenshot to do the original message justice --- via the Telegram app}) is as shown in the screen-shot below...


\begin{figure}[H]
  \begin{center}
  %\includegraphics[trim=2cm 8cm 2cm 8cm, clip, width=0.9\textwidth,]{resources/pdfs/ProteinSynthesisStateMachine.pdf}\\
   \includegraphics[height=0.6\textheight,]{resources/images/emailer_1.5_example_dear_waltz}\\
   \caption{``Dear Waltz" a poetic email formatted via Emailer 1.5}
  \label{FIGWEXAv15b}
  \end{center}
\end{figure}


So, basically that's it! Tests we have done using the above email client app in TEA have already delivered actual emails into our in-boxes! So, cheers and Congs to TEA getting past the infancy phase, and into ready-for-production maturity!

\vspace{2em}

To access this service, use any of the options:

\vspace{2em}

\begin{enumerate}
\item Find Emailer on the list of standard TEA programs via:\\ \url{https://tea.nuchwezi.com}
\item Directly load and run Emailer via: {\normalsize \url{https://tea.nuchwezi.com/?i=\%20&fc=https://gist.githubusercontent.com/mcnemesis/a084d7a413226f430113662c33964bea/raw/&run} }
\item{Obtain the TEA Emailer source-code (and other related codes/programs) via the TEA GitHub Project:\\
\url{https://github.com/mcnemesis/cli_tttt}}
\item{ Find the Emailer code on GitHub Gists:\\
\url{https://gist.github.com/mcnemesis/a084d7a413226f430113662c33964bea}}
\item{ Or use the official shortened link to share or load Emailer:\\
 \url{https://bit.ly/run_emailer}
 }
\end{enumerate}

% TAZ SECTION: X
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\chapter{X: XENOGRAFT}
\label{SECX}


\begin{table}[H]
  \centering
  \LARGE
	\begin{tabular}[t]{|p{0.2\textwidth}|p{0.5\textwidth}}
 
	\textbf{NAME} & \texttt{XENOGRAFT}\\
	\hline
	\textbf{Purpose} & \begin{enumerate}
	\item Affix things to things
	\item Adding an Explicit Prefix or Suffix to AI
	\item Multiplying Text --- such as affixing a string to itself
	\item Reducing a Text --- such as halving it 
	\item Another way to dynamically build parametric Texts
	\end{enumerate}\\
	\hline
	              
\end{tabular}
\caption{General Objectives of TEA Primitive \textbf{X:}}
  \label{TABTAZX}
\end{table}


\section{SEMANTICS of X:}
\label{SECSEMX}


\begin{table}[H]
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3} % Optional: increases row height
\rowcolors{1}{lightgray}{white}   % Alternating row colors
\begin{tabular}{>{\bfseries}m{0.4\linewidth} | m{0.6\linewidth}} % 2 columns, 
\rowcolor{white}
\textbf{\makecell[l]{INSTRUCTION\\ SIGNATURE}} & \textbf{INSTRUCTION FUNCTION} \\
\hline

x: & Return AI with AI affixed to itself. Essentially, multiplying AI\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{x:PREFIX} & Prefix  PREFIX to AI. That becomes AI\\

x!: & Return Half of AI. Essentially, reducing AI, and somewhat reversing the effects of \texttt{x:}\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{x!:SUFFIX} & Affix  SUFFIX to the end of AI\\


\makecell[l]{x*:vPREFIX\\x*:vPREFIX:vSTR} & Prefix the string in vault  vPREFIX to the string in vault  vSTR . That becomes AI. Without  vSTR , operates on AI\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{x*!:vSUFFIX:vSTR} & Suffix the string in vault  vSUFFIX to the string in vault  vSTR. That becomes AI. Without  vSTR, operates on AI\\


 \hline
\end{tabular}
\caption{The Semantics of X:}
\label{TABSEMX}
\end{table}


\section{NOTES about X:}
\label{SECNOTEX}
  

\textbf{Xenografting is the only direct and simplest way to affix strings to other strings in TEA.} Of course, the Glue command (see \textbf{\hyperref[SECG]{Chapter \ref{SECG}}}) already allows some kind of xenografting especially with its \texttt{g*:} command space. For example, one could both prefix and suffix the string ``\verb!---!" to any other string, to easily turn it into a title in some text environments. The following example TEA program does this using pure X-primitives:


\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=X: EXAMPLE 1: Transforming Text into Special Formats using X:, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
v:vHEADLINE:{Interoperability Is Possible} 
v:vAFFIX:{---} 
x*:vAFFIX:vHEADLINE 
v:vHEADLINE 
x*!:vAFFIX:vHEADLINE
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

While the following does the same, using pure G-primitives:


\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=X: EXAMPLE 2: Transforming Text into Special Formats using G:, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
v:vHEADLINE:{Interoperability Is Possible} 
v:vAFFIX:{---} 
g*:{}:vAFFIX:vHEADLINE:vAFFIX
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

Both programs should return the result 


\vspace{2em}
 \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 1 from EXAMPLE 1 and EXAMPLE 2]
---Interoperability Is Possible---
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 \vspace{2em} 
 
 
 Otherwise, simple demonstrations of what \textbf{X:} is all about include the following simple TEA programs...
 
 
 \vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=X: EXAMPLE 3: Several Mini-Programs Demonstrating Capabilities of X, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:World|x:{Hello } 
#q!: # (=Hello World, VAULTS:{})
i!:World|x!:{Hello } 
#q!: # (=WorldHello , VAULTS:{})
i!:Hello World|x: 
#q!: # (=Hello WorldHello World, VAULTS:{}) doubles the input
i!:Hello World|x!: 
#q!: #(=Hello, VAULTS:{}) halves the input
i!:TEST|v:vI:Hello World|x*:vI 
#q!: #= Hello WorldTEST
i!:TEST|v:vI:Hello World|x*!:vI 
#= TESTHello World
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{1em}

In the above program, uncommenting any of the ``cut-point" lines shall let the program terminate after the last X-command instruction, and one should find that anywhere TEA works, the output shall be just as indicated in the comments showing the state of the TEA environment at the moment.



\section{EXAMPLE APPLICATIONS of X:}
\label{SECEXAMPX}

  
In testing how to best apply the \textbf{XENOGRAFT} commands, definitely, applications such as \textbf{ZHA} --- the TEA powered chatbot (see \textbf{\hyperref[SECEXAMP1F]{Section \ref{SECEXAMP1F}}}) --- that we saw earlier readily comes to mind; mostly because, many text-based chatbots require or employ prompts and interfaces where user names or handles are affixed to messages. However, chatbots aren't the only legitimate case where we might apply xenografting as one might have noticed when studying many of the examples already encountered in earlier sections of this book.


\vspace{2em}

In the next section, we are going to explore an application that somewhat significantly utilizes this command. The application is a dictionary utility, and it essentially can allow one to build a list of definitions which can then be exported out of the app for purposes of using them elsewhere.


\subsection{EXAMPLE 1: \textbf{E-LEARNING:} FREE DICTIONARY | LEXICON  }
\label{SECEXAMP1X}



There is not much to say about the inspiration or design of this program. Basically, we wish to be able to do the following minimal tasks:

\vspace{2em}

\begin{enumerate}
\item When presented with a word or phrase, look-up its definition and present it to the user.
\item In case the user wishes to have another query handled, simply keep track of the last definition and repeat the process.
\item Finally, allow the user to either return the last query result or a list of all the definitions resolved thus far, and quit.
\end{enumerate}

\vspace{2em}

For purposes of looking-up the definitions, we shall not have to worry about having to setup a custom database of terms and their definitions ourselves, because several services already exist out there, that one can interact with via the Internet, and luckily, we have already dealt with the intricacies of interacting with WEB APIs in \textbf{\hyperref[SECW]{Chapter \ref{SECW}}}. Thus, we shall simply focus on making the above specification come to life, and for now, shall focus on using a WEB API known as \textbf{Free Dictionary}\footnote{See \url{https://dictionaryapi.dev/}}.


\vspace{2em}

The essential source code of this TEA program is as such:

   \vspace{1em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=TEA Application: Free Dictionary | LEXICON v1, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
#!/usr/bin/tttt -fc
v:vABOUT:{
*****************************
FREE LEXICON v1
*****************************
Use this app to simply look-up terms or build a list of definitions.
It is based on Dictionary API that mostly focuses on standard terms, thus, some queries might have no resolutions. Feel free to proceed...}

---[ CONSTANTS ]
v:vFOOTER:{PROCESSED VIA FREE LEXICON @ tea.nuchwezi.com}
v:vDELIM:*****************************
v:vQUERY_PROMPT:{Enter Word or Phrase to Look-Up:}
v:vNL:{
}
v:vPREFIX_QUERY:{QUERY: }
v:vPREFIX_DEFINITIONS:{
DEFINITION(s):
}
v:vMENU_NEXT:{PICK next ACTION > 
1: Return last definition + Quit
2: Return all definitions + Quit
3: Process Next Query (default)}

---[ VARIABLES ]
v:vQUERY:{} # what to define
v:vLIST_RESULTS:{}

---[ INTRO ]
y:vABOUT | i*:

---[ MENU ]
l:lQUERY_PROMPT
y:vQUERY_PROMPT | i*: | t.!: | v:vQUERY

---[ PROCESS ]
y:vQUERY | f:^$:lQUERY_PROMPT

x*:vPREFIX_QUERY:vQUERY
v:vPRESENT_QUERY

# dictionary api requires call of format:
# https://api.dictionaryapi.dev/api/v2/entries/en/<term>
v:vURL:https://api.dictionaryapi.dev/api/v2/entries/en/
x*:vURL:vQUERY | v:vURL # update url

# then make call..
w*:vURL

v:vAPI_RESULTS # store result from API

h!:,
k:definition
h!::
k!:{"definitions"|"definition"}
r!:{:"}:{->}
r!:{."}:{.}
v:vDEFINITIONS

v:vPREFIX_DEFINITIONS:{
DEFINITION(s):
}

x*:vPREFIX_DEFINITIONS:vDEFINITIONS 
v:vPRESENT_DEFINITION

---[ PRESENT ]
g*!:vNL:vDELIM:vPRESENT_QUERY:vPRESENT_DEFINITION
v:vANSWER

i*: # present current definition...

g*!:vNL:vLIST_RESULTS:vANSWER | v:vLIST_RESULTS # store defn

---[ FINISH ]
g*!:vNL:vNL:vDELIM:vFOOTER | v:vUSE_FOOTER

y:vMENU_NEXT | i*: | t.!: | v:vOPT
f!:^[123]$:lQUERY_PROMPT

f!:1:lNPROC1
y:vANSWER | x*!:vUSE_FOOTER | q!:

l:lNPROC1
f!:2:lNPROC2
y:vLIST_RESULTS | x*!:vUSE_FOOTER | q!:

l:lNPROC2
j:lQUERY_PROMPT
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
      \captionof{figure}{TEA Program Source-Code: Free Dictionary | LEXICON v1}
  \label{FIGTEALEXICON}

\vspace{1em}


This program is indeed quite useful as one shall establish from the kinds of outputs it can present:


 \vspace{2em}
 \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 1 from LEXICON v1 --- a list of definitions]

*****************************
QUERY: wining

DEFINITION(s):
->To entertain with wine.
->To drink wine.
->A session of drinking wine socially.
*****************************
QUERY: hello world

DEFINITION(s):

*****************************
QUERY: relax

DEFINITION(s):
->To calm down.
->To make something loose.
->To become loose.
->To make something less severe or tense.
->To become less severe or tense.
->To make something (such as codes and regulations) more lenient.
->(of codes and regulations) To become more lenient.
->To relieve (something) from stress.
->To relieve from constipation; to loosen; to open.
*****************************
QUERY: mates

DEFINITION(s):
->The conclusive victory in a game of chess that occurs when an opponent's king is threatened with unavoidable capture.
->(by extension) Any losing situation with no escape; utter defeat.
->To put the king of an opponent into checkmate.
->(by extension) To place in a losing situation that has no escape.
->A fellow
->(especially of a non-human animal) A breeding partner.
->A friend
->Friendly term of address to a stranger
->In naval ranks
->A ship's officer
->A first mate.
->A technical assistant in certain trades (e.g. gasfitter's mate
->The other member of a matched pair of objects.
->A suitable companion; a match; an equal.
->To match
->To copulate.
->To pair in order to raise offspring"

->To match oneself against; to oppose as equal; to compete with.
->To fit (objects) together without space between.

->The abovementioned plant; the leaves and shoots used for the tea"

->An evergreen tree
->A beverage
->A cup of this drink.

*****************************
PROCESSED VIA FREE LEXICON @ tea.nuchwezi.com
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 \vspace{2em}
 
 
 And when one opts to only have the program return just the last definition, the output is something like...
 
 
  \vspace{2em}
 \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 2 from LEXICON v1 --- a single definition]
*****************************
QUERY: lexicon

DEFINITION(s):
->The vocabulary of a language.
->A dictionary that includes or focuses on lexemes.
->A dictionary of Classical Greek
->The lexicology of a programming language. (Usually called lexical structure.)"
->Any dictionary.
->The vocabulary used by or known to an individual. (Also called lexical knowledge)"
->A vocabulary specific to a certain subject.

*****************************
PROCESSED VIA FREE LEXICON @ tea.nuchwezi.com
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 \vspace{2em}


\subsubsection{Lexicon V2: dealing with restrictions and glitches}

While developing this application, a glitch we had never seen before manifested --- suddenly, the TEA code that previously worked unmodified on both WEB and CLI (such as the version 1 of this program shared in the last section) started to show strange behaviors on the commandline; 

\vspace{2em}

\begin{enumerate}
\item For example, calls to the Dictionary API suddenly started to fail when made inside the TEA program, and yet they were working well on the commandline when conducted via the \texttt{curl} commandline tool.
\item Then we noticed that the TEA \textbf{INTERACT} commands that present a GUI --- essentially \texttt{i*:} --- suddenly showed glitches on the input dialog either over the input box or entirely across the window widget!
\end{enumerate}

\vspace{2em}


Those two anomalies, very telling and most likely originating outside of TEA or the TEA runtime, caused us to be concerned, and so, we have written another version of this application that works around those two errors. This version essentially keeps the same logic as the original, but does two other things:

\vspace{2em}

\begin{enumerate}
\item It prevents the usual TEA GUI from being presented when the program is being on the command-line. Essentially, all calls to \texttt{i*:} are merely swapped with calls to \texttt{i:}
\item It forces all WEBIFY calls to be made using a different User-Agent than the default --- in this case, we detect if we are on the command-line, and then force HTTP calls to use the curl User-Agent header.
\end{enumerate}

\vspace{2em}

With those changes made, the final program --- still able to work as is on both the WEB and CLI, and with the same original behavior on the WEB as version 1 was, but with no GUI prompts when used on the command-line, is as follows:

\vspace{2em}

   \vspace{1em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=TEA Application: Free Dictionary | LEXICON v3, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
#!/usr/bin/tttt -fc
v:vABOUT:{
*****************************
FREE LEXICON v3
*****************************
Use this app to simply look-up terms or build a list of definitions.
It is based on Dictionary API that mostly focuses on standard terms, thus, some queries might have no resolutions. Feel free to proceed...}

# Windows/Microsoft could be under-attack (noticed while testing this app)
# thus this [hack-proof] version that forces all interact commands to use 
# i: and not i*: so that for command-line TEA, we need not use any GUI 
# (thus no Py/Tk widgets)
# otherwise, original version of TEA LEXICON v1 is at:
# https://gist.github.com/mcnemesis/6bb975c3051bd0b9ebece8c5a73ce336

---[ CONSTANTS ]
v:vFOOTER:{PROCESSED VIA FREE LEXICON @ tea.nuchwezi.com}
v:vDELIM:*****************************
v:vQUERY_PROMPT:{Enter Word or Phrase to Look-Up:}
v:vNL:{
}
v:vPREFIX_QUERY:{QUERY: }
v:vPREFIX_DEFINITIONS:{
DEFINITION(s):
}
v:vMENU_NEXT:{PICK next ACTION > 
1: Return last definition + Quit
2: Return all definitions + Quit
3: Process Next Query (default)}
# shall want to know where program is being run from
z.:PLATFORM | v:vENVIRONMENT

---[ VARIABLES ]
v:vQUERY:{} # what to define
v:vLIST_RESULTS:{}

---[ INTRO ]
y:vABOUT | i:

---[ MENU ]
l:lQUERY_PROMPT
y:vQUERY_PROMPT | i: | t.!: | v:vQUERY

---[ PROCESS ]
y:vQUERY | f:^$:lQUERY_PROMPT

x*:vPREFIX_QUERY:vQUERY
v:vPRESENT_QUERY

# dictionary api requires call of format:
# https://api.dictionaryapi.dev/api/v2/entries/en/<term>
v:vURL:https://api.dictionaryapi.dev/api/v2/entries/en/
x*:vURL:vQUERY | v:vURL # update url

# we shall want to pretend we are curl if in CLI-TEA
y:vENVIRONMENT | f!:CLI:lNCLI
v:User-Agent:curl/7.68.0
w*.:User-Agent

l:lNCLI

# then make call..
w*:vURL

v:vAPI_RESULTS # store result from API

h!:,
k:definition
h!::
k!:{"definitions"|"definition"}
r!:{:"}:{->}
r!:{."}:{.}
v:vDEFINITIONS

v:vPREFIX_DEFINITIONS:{
DEFINITION(s):
}

x*:vPREFIX_DEFINITIONS:vDEFINITIONS 
v:vPRESENT_DEFINITION

---[ PRESENT ]
g*!:vNL:vDELIM:vPRESENT_QUERY:vPRESENT_DEFINITION
v:vANSWER

i: # present current definition...

g*!:vNL:vLIST_RESULTS:vANSWER | v:vLIST_RESULTS # store defn

---[ FINISH ]
g*!:vNL:vNL:vDELIM:vFOOTER | v:vUSE_FOOTER

y:vMENU_NEXT | i: | t.!: | v:vOPT
f!:^[123]$:lQUERY_PROMPT

f!:1:lNPROC1
y:vANSWER | x*!:vUSE_FOOTER | q!:

l:lNPROC1
f!:2:lNPROC2
y:vLIST_RESULTS | x*!:vUSE_FOOTER | q!:

l:lNPROC2
j:lQUERY_PROMPT
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
      \captionof{figure}{TEA Program Source-Code: Free Dictionary | LEXICON v3 --- addressing noticed environment glitches for CLI}
  \label{FIGTEALEXICONv2}

\vspace{1em}


\vspace{2em}

To access this service, use any of the options:

\vspace{2em}

\begin{enumerate}
\item Find LEXICON on the list of standard TEA programs via:\\ \url{https://tea.nuchwezi.com}
\item Directly load and run LEXICON via: {\normalsize \url{https://tea.nuchwezi.com/?fc=https://gist.githubusercontent.com/mcnemesis/e35820d78f71984d53ccb986c621665e/raw/free_lexicon_ng.tea&run} }
\item{Obtain the LEXICON source-code (and other related codes/programs) via the TEA GitHub Project:\\
\url{https://github.com/mcnemesis/cli_tttt}}
\item{ Find the LEXICON code on GitHub Gists:\\
\url{https://gist.github.com/mcnemesis/e35820d78f71984d53ccb986c621665e}}
\item{ Or use the official shortened link to share or load LEXICON:\\
 \url{https://bit.ly/free_lexicon}
 }
\end{enumerate}

\vspace{2em}




%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
% TAZ SECTION: Y
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\chapter{Y: YANK}
\label{SECY}


\begin{table}[H]
  \centering
  \LARGE
	\begin{tabular}[t]{|p{0.2\textwidth}|p{0.5\textwidth}}
 
	\textbf{NAME} & \texttt{YANK}\\
	\hline
	\textbf{Purpose} & \begin{enumerate}
	\item Reading Memory
	\item Returning Data previously stored in a Vault --- essentially \textbf{the companion commands to \texttt{V:*} command space}.
	\item Accessing Initial External User Input from anywhere in a TEA program
	\item Determining the Length of Data in Vaults
	\item Reading Persistent (TEA Database) Storage.
	\end{enumerate}\\
	\hline
	              
\end{tabular}
\caption{General Objectives of TEA Primitive \textbf{Y:}}
  \label{TABTAZY}
\end{table}


\section{SEMANTICS of Y:}
\label{SECSEMY}


\begin{table}[H]
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3} % Optional: increases row height
\rowcolors{1}{lightgray}{white}   % Alternating row colors
\begin{tabular}{>{\bfseries}m{0.4\linewidth} | m{0.6\linewidth}} % 2 columns, 
\rowcolor{white}
\textbf{\makecell[l]{INSTRUCTION\\ SIGNATURE}} & \textbf{INSTRUCTION FUNCTION} \\
\hline

y: & Return whatever is currently stored in the default vault (the unnamed vault).
\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{y:vNAME} & Return whatever is stored in vault with name vNAME\\

y!: & Return the length of whatever is currently stored in the default vault (the unnamed vault).\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{y!:vNAME} & Return whatever is currently stored in vault vNAME\\


\makecell[l]{y*:} & Return whatever was the external, user provided initial input to the TEA program – the initial AI\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{y*:vNAME} & Return whatever is currently stored in vault vNAME\\

\makecell[l]{y*!:vNAME} & Return the length of whatever is currently stored in vault vNAME.\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{y*!:} & Return the length of whatever was the external, user provided initial input to the TEA program – the initial AI\\

\makecell[l]{y@:NAME} & Return whatever is stored in \textbf{TEA DATABASE} with name NAME\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{y!@:NAME} & Return the length of whatever is currently stored in DATABASE record with name NAME.\\

\makecell[l]{y!@:} & Return How Many Records are Currently stored in \textbf{TEA DATABASE}\\

\makecell[l]{y@:} & Return \textbf{RECORD NAMES} of all that is currently stored in \textbf{TEA DATABASE}\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{y.@:} & Return \textbf{RECORD INDICES} of all that is currently stored in \textbf{TEA DATABASE}\\


 \hline
\end{tabular}
\caption{The Semantics of Y:}
\label{TABSEMY}
\end{table}


\section{NOTES about Y:}
\label{SECNOTEY}


Yanking is essentially the opposite operation of vaulting in TEA. Generally, the reason a program writes to memory --- such as via use of variables, pointers, files, etc. is so that later, such stored data can then be read-out/retrieved --- often via a mechanism that references a particular record in memory via the name or identifier that was used to save, store or write it. Thus, while the \textbf{VAULT} commands help create or write-to spaces in a program's memory (what in other programming languages might be referred to as registers, variables or cells) where we can store data or information, and yet, the vault-commands cannot be used to retrieve such data when required! Instead, data saved using V-commands must later be accessed using \textbf{YANK} commands.

\vspace{2em}

The Y-command also allows TEA programs to correctly reference original user-provided input anywhere in a TEA program via a special command\footnote{This command, unlike any other YANK command is special because, there is usually no way to yank something that hadn't been previously stored using a vault command --- even for the DEFAULT VAULT that has no name; to be able to call \texttt{y:}, \texttt{v:} should have been invoked earlier on. However, for the command \texttt{y*:}, it doesn't matter whether any VAULT command was used before, all that matters is that the TEA program was invoked, and that there is or wasn't any externally specified input to the program --- when none, it essentially just returns EMPTY SPACE, ``".} signature \texttt{y*:}. First, this helps to waive the idiomatic inclusion of default inputs or the initialization of AI/Active Input as part of common practice in TEA programming---such as with the use of \texttt{i!:}, \texttt{v:} and \texttt{w!:} commands early-on in the program source, by instead offering a direct means for the program to access the original, externally-specified user-provided input any time it is needed. 


\vspace{2em}


Also, the Y-inverse commands\footnote{So-called because originally, TEA only had ``!" as the only command qualifier, and that for any primitive such as \texttt{Y:}, the qualified version, \texttt{Y!:} would be referred to as ``the inverse" signature. However, later, more qualifiers arrived --- see \textbf{\hyperref[SECQUALIFY]{Section \ref{SECQUALIFY}}}, and so, this idea might sometimes be only used sparingly.}, just like \texttt{V!}, also allow for a straightforward way to determine the size of data in TEA --- for Y, only possible with strings already held inside of some vault --- such as via \texttt{Y!:vNAME}, while \texttt{v!:STR} also can help determine the length of explicit, inline string values\footnote{For example, \texttt{v!:"hello"} would return ``5", just like \texttt{v!:hello}. But to use Yank for that, one would need a program such as \texttt{v:vVAL:"hello"|y!:vVAL}}.


\vspace{2em}


A useful test of the Y: facility is the following program that should greet one with ``Hello X" if one responds to the associated prompt with the name ``X":


\vspace{2em}
  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=Y: EXAMPLE 1: Custom Greeting or Hello World, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:What is your name?|i:|v:vNAME|v:vGREET:{Hello }|v:vG:{}|g*::vGREET:vNAME|v:vGRT|i:TEST|y:vGRT
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
\vspace{2em}


\subsubsection{TEA DATABASE operations}


Away from in-memory and temporal storage such as we find common in TEA when using basic vaults, there is the option to use persistent/long-term storage in TEA, and this, just like with the \texttt{V@:} commands in the VAULT command space, is likewise supported by the \texttt{Y@:} commands. These commands essentially allow the YANK instruction to allow us to read out values from the \textbf{TEA DATABASE} that were previously written using Vault commands.

\vspace{2em}

Of course, the cool, or rather, special feature of these DATABASE reading instructions is that, unlike vault commands, a TEA program might attempt to read a Database record it didn't create or which it isn't sure exists, without error. Essentially, one TEA program might store a record into the TEA database (which storage, as a rule, is tied to the current machine and user for CLI TEA, and otherwise to the current web browser for WEB TEA), and can be read out irrespective of which TEA program wrote it, as long as one knows the right database record.

\vspace{2em}

Thus, assuming we ran the following program earlier on...

\vspace{2em}
  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=EXAMPLE 2: Saving a User's Name to the TEA DATABASE, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:What is your name?|i:|v@:dNAME_USER_NAME
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
\vspace{2em}


We then can later run a different TEA program that fetches that stored user name and greets the user with it, otherwise re-prompting for a name if none was stored yet!

\vspace{2em}
  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=Y: EXAMPLE 3: Retrieving a User's Name from the TEA DATABASE, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
y@:dNAME_USER_NAME | v:vUSERNAME # fetch from database and store in vault

f!:^$:lGREET
l:lPROMPT
i!:What is your name?|i: | t!.:
f:^$:lPROMPT

v@:dNAME_USER_NAME # store name into database

# use stored name to present custom greeting...
l:lGREET
i!:{Hello } | x*!:vUSERNAME | i:
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
\vspace{2em}


And as with \texttt{V!@:dNAME}, we can use \texttt{Y!@:dNAME} to return the length of what is stored in the database record with name dNAME. Example is as such...


\vspace{2em}
  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=Y: EXAMPLE 4: Retrieving a Record's Length from the TEA DATABASE, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
# store value "this is a menu" under key "MENU" in the DB
v@:MENU:{this is a menu}

# check (even in a different program or after quiting)
# how long a value in DB record "MENU" is
Y!@:MENU # should return "14"
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
\vspace{2em}



\section{EXAMPLE APPLICATIONS of Y:}
\label{SECEXAMPI}

  
There is not much to explore concerning YANK that we have not already encountered in the other earlier examples and [standard] applications, however, and for purposes of wanting to demonstrate what Yank can readily make possible, we shall take one existing example from the current TEA demonstration programs --- particularly, the case of a \textbf{Textbox Drawing} TEA program, and adapt it into a more robust, and mature TEA standard utility.

\vspace{2em}

Essentially, we want to take the following basic TEA program:

\vspace{2em}
  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=TEA Sample Program: Draw Text Box, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
# Given some text, shall return a text box drawn around it
f!:^$:lPROCESS # don't prompt if there's already some input
i!:Enter some text: |i:
l:lPROCESS
v:vIN
v:vP:.|v:vS:-
r*!:vIN:vP:vS
x:--|x!:--
v:vBTOP
v:vSTART:
v:vBLR:{|}
g*:{ }:vBLR:vIN:vBLR
v:vIN
g*:{_ }:vSTART:vBTOP:vIN:vBTOP
h!:_
r!:_:|
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
\vspace{2em}

Which produces outputs such as the following...


  \vspace{2em}
 \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 1 from Basic Text-Box Drawing TEA Program]
 ---------------
 | hello world |
 ---------------
  \end{tcbverbatim}
  
  \vspace{2em}
 

 \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 2 from Basic Text-Box Drawing TEA Program]
 --------------------------------------
 | hello world, this is a longer text |
 --------------------------------------
  \end{tcbverbatim}


  \vspace{2em}
 
 
 But which program, when presented with say text spanning multiple lines or very long text, might otherwise present some chaotic stuff such as we see in the next output...
 
 
   \vspace{2em}
 

 \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 3 from Basic Text-Box Drawing TEA Program]
  ---------------
---------------------
----------------
 | hello world, 
this is a longer text
on three lines |
 ---------------
---------------------
----------------
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 \vspace{2em}
 

And so, we shall use the idea (but not the exact code\footnote{For example, and interesting to note concerning that text-box drawing TEA program; much as it contains some memory-writing and reading instructions, such as keeping some variables in vaults via use of the \texttt{v:*} commands, and yet, because there are many ways TEA allows most instructions to access data in vaults without having to first directly/manually yank it using \texttt{y:*} commands, one finds that the program surprisingly contains no trace or need of the YANK command-space!}) to implement a TEA program capable of accepting arbitrary text, and presenting it inside of beautiful text boxes. This might have applications related to crafting payloads for sharing with friends via social media, in fancy emails, or as Text-Art or even beautiful poetry among others!


\subsection{EXAMPLE 1: \textbf{CALLIGRAPHY:} Text Beautifier}
\label{SECYEXAMP1}


In designing and implementing our application, which we are referring to as \textbf{TEXT Beautifier}, we want to ensure we address the following minimal specification:

\vspace{2em}

\begin{enumerate}
\item \textbf{PHASE 1:} The program either processes externally provided text input or otherwise prompts the user for text input to be processed.
\item \textbf{PHASE 2:} The program starts by taking the given text and re-formating it into a beautiful block of text of a fixed maximum length (which user can optionally adjust to their liking).
\item \textbf{PHASE 3:} The program then presents user with the option of return the final text immediately, or otherwise presenting it with a bounding text box drawn around it.
\item \textbf{PHASE 4:} The program presents the formatted text with a basic text-box drawn around it. User can chose to immediately return that or further beautify the text.
\item \textbf{PHASE 5:} The program then presents beautified text-box (with pretty corners for example), and asks the user if they wish to return that or otherwise customize the textbox with their own options.
\item \textbf{PHASE 6:} In case user chose to customize the beautification, they have option to specify custom icon/symbol to be used to draw the corner, horizontal bar and vertical bars of the text box.
\item Loop back to \textbf{PHASE 5:}   
\end{enumerate}

\vspace{2em}


This program has been written!

\vspace{2em}

Essentially, v1 of the essential (and now standard) TEA program is as follows...


\vspace{2em}

{
 \Large
  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=TEA Program: TEXT BEAUTIFIER v1, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
#!/usr/bin/tttt -fc
v:vABOUT:{
++--------------------++
+ TEXT BEAUTIFIER v1 +
++--------------------++
+ draws nice boxes 
+ around your text!
+----------------------+}
##############################
# Architect: Fut. Prof. JWL
# https://bit.ly/profjwl
##############################

+---[CONSTANTS]
v:vPROMPT_TEXT:{Please Enter|Type|Paste the text to be processed:}
v:vNL:{
}

+---[VARIABLES]
v:vIN:{} # to hold main raw text
#v:vTEXT:{}
v:vTEXTBOX:{}
v:vCORNER:{+}
v:vHBAR:{-}
v:vVBAR:{|}
v:vNMAXLINE_LEN:26
v:vWRAPPED_TEXT:{}
v:vIGNORE_EXTERNAL_TEXT:0 # effective if not 0

+---[PHASE 1: get text]
l:lINTRO
y:vABOUT | i:

l:lPROMPT
y:vIGNORE_EXTERNAL_TEXT | f!:0:lIGNORE_EXTERNAL_TEXT
y*: | t!.: | v:vIN # external input text
l:lIGNORE_EXTERNAL_TEXT
# check if we already have text...
y:vIN | f!:^$:lPROCESS_TEXT
y:vPROMPT_TEXT | i: | t!.: | v:vIN
f!:^$:lPROCESS_TEXT # re-check...
j:lPROMPT

+---[PHASE 2: re-format text]
l:lPROCESS_TEXT
# ensure we are starting frm scratch
v:vWRAPPED_TEXT:{} 

y:vIN 
g.:=% # first, mask all new lines..
v:vIN 

# check if text exceeds max line size..
y!:vIN | v:vNTEXT_LEN
g*:{>}:vNTEXT_LEN:vNMAXLINE_LEN | r.: 
f:true:lWRAP_TEXT:lNO_TEXT_WRAP_NEEDED

# wrap long texts...
l:lWRAP_TEXT

v:vINDEX_LINE:0
y!:vIN | v:vIN_REMAINDER_LEN

l:lSTART_WRAP

# did we reach end of text yet?
y:vIN_REMAINDER_LEN | x!:{<= 0} | r.: 
f:true:lFINISHED_WRAP

# did we reach end of line yet?
# (nl>0) && (nl%maxl==0)?
v:vBO:(|v:vBE:)
v:vG:{>0)&&(}|v:vMOD:{%}|v:vTEST_SUF:{==0}
g*:{}:vBO:vINDEX_LINE:vG:vINDEX_LINE:vMOD:vNMAXLINE_LEN:vTEST_SUF:vBE | r.: 
f:true:lSTART_NEXT_LINE:lCONTINUE_LINE
l:lSTART_NEXT_LINE
g*:{}:vWRAPPED_TEXT:vNL | v:vWRAPPED_TEXT
# advance indices...
y:vINDEX_LINE | x!:{+1} | r.: | v:vINDEX_LINE

l:lCONTINUE_LINE

y:vIN | d!:^. | v:vCHAR # pop char
y:vIN | r:.:{} | v:vIN # stash remainder..

g*:{}:vWRAPPED_TEXT:vCHAR 
#x:[|x!:]|q!:
| v:vWRAPPED_TEXT

# advance indices...
y:vINDEX_LINE | x!:{+1} | r.: | v:vINDEX_LINE
y!:vIN | v:vIN_REMAINDER_LEN

# process next char...
j:lSTART_WRAP

i!: interlude | q!:

l:lFINISHED_WRAP
y:vWRAPPED_TEXT 
# reverse new-line masking...
r!:{=%|=[\n]%}:{
}
v:vWRAPPED_TEXT

j:lPRESENT_TEXT

+---[PHASE 3: present text]
l:lNO_TEXT_WRAP_NEEDED
# using available text as is!
# ensure we actually have text...
y:vIN | f:^$:lPROMPT
y:vIN | v:vWRAPPED_TEXT

l:lPRESENT_TEXT

# show what we have and decide next step...
v:vPRESENT_PROMPT:{DECIDE NEXT STEP:
1. Return Formatted Text as is
2. Proceed to Draw Text Box (default)
+----------------------+
Formatted Text Follows...
+----------------------+
}

y:vPRESENT_PROMPT | x*!:vWRAPPED_TEXT | i: | t!.: | v:vOPT
f!:^[12]$:lDRAW_TEXTBOX
f!:^1$:lDRAW_TEXTBOX

y:vWRAPPED_TEXT | q!:

+---[PHASE 4: draw basic text-box]
l:lDRAW_TEXTBOX

#draw top and bottom border first...
v:vBORDER_LINE:{}
v:vNINDEX:0

l:lSTART_DRAW_BORDER
y:vBORDER_LINE | x*!:vHBAR | v:vBORDER_LINE 
y:vNINDEX | x!:+1 | r. | v:vNINDEX
y:vNINDEX | x!:> | x*!:vNMAXLINE_LEN | r.:
f:true:lDONE_DRAW_BORDER
j:lSTART_DRAW_BORDER
l:lDONE_DRAW_BORDER

y:vBORDER_LINE | v:vDECO_BORDER_LINE

l:lPRESENT_BORDERED_TEXT
# add it to the text...
g*!:vNL:vDECO_BORDER_LINE:vWRAPPED_TEXT:vDECO_BORDER_LINE 
v:vTEXTBOX # store current text box

# show what we have and decide next step...
v:vPRESENT_PROMPT:{DECIDE NEXT STEP:
1. Return Text-Box as is
2. Customize Border Line (CORNERS)
3. Draw Complete Text Box (default)

}

y:vPRESENT_PROMPT | x*!:vTEXTBOX | i: | t!.: | v:vOPT
f!:^[12]$:lDRAW_FULL_TEXTBOX
f!:^1$:lCUSTOMIZE_BORDER

y:vTEXTBOX | q!:

+---[PHASE 5: draw custom text-box]
l:lCUSTOMIZE_BORDER

g*:{}:vCORNER:vBORDER_LINE:vCORNER | v:vDECO_BORDER_LINE

i!:{CUSTOMIZE CORNERS:
Current is (}| x*!:vCORNER | x!:{) to produce:
} | x*!:vDECO_BORDER_LINE 
x!:{
OPTIONS: 
.LEAVE BLANK (preserves current corners)
.ENTER VALUE (adopts your provided corner)
} 
| v:vPRESENT_PROMPT

y:vPRESENT_PROMPT | i: | v:vOPT
f:^$:lPRESERVE_CORNER
y:vOPT 
#| d!:^. #ensure we only keep 1 char for corner?
| v:vCORNER

g*:{}:vCORNER:vBORDER_LINE:vCORNER | v:vDECO_BORDER_LINE

# first go see what it looks like
j:lPRESENT_BORDERED_TEXT 

l:lPRESERVE_CORNER
# then return to next option...

l:lDRAW_FULL_TEXTBOX

# first, get formatted, unbounded text
y:vWRAPPED_TEXT 

# pad it as necessary?...
#t.: | t.!: | v:vWRAPPED_TEXT 

# mask all new lines..
g.:=_=_=%_%_% # first, mask all new lines..
v:vTEMP_WTEXT

# inject line delimiters...
v:vREGEX_HEND:{=_=_=}
v:vREGEX_HSTART:{%_%_%}
v:vREGEX_START:{^}
v:vREGEX_END:{$}
v:vEMPTY:{}

r*:vTEMP_WTEXT:vREGEX_START:vVBAR | v:vTEMP_WTEXT
r*:vTEMP_WTEXT:vREGEX_END:vEMPTY | v:vTEMP_WTEXT

# split em up...
h*!:vTEMP_WTEXT:vREGEX_HEND | v:vTEMP_WTEXT

# swap em...
r*!:vTEMP_WTEXT:vREGEX_HSTART:vVBAR | v:vTEMP_WTEXT
# we could also draw right vertical border
# but for now, shall merely relax it...
r*!:vTEMP_WTEXT:vREGEX_HEND:vEMPTY | v:vTEMP_WTEXT

# add decor borders to the text...
g*!:vNL:vDECO_BORDER_LINE:vTEMP_WTEXT:vDECO_BORDER_LINE 
v:vTEXTBOX # store current text box

+---[PHASE 6: user configurations]
l:lCUSTOMIZE_BEAUTIFIED_BORDER

# show what we have and decide next step...
v:vPRESENT_PROMPT:{DECIDE NEXT STEP:
1. Return Text-Box as is
2. Make Further Customizations (default)

}

y:vPRESENT_PROMPT | x*!:vTEXTBOX | i: | t!.: | v:vOPT
f!:^[1]$:lPROMPT_CUSTOMIZATION_HBAR

y:vTEXTBOX | q!:

l:lPROMPT_CUSTOMIZATION_HBAR

# first, construct current horizontal border...
g*:{}:vCORNER:vBORDER_LINE:vCORNER | v:vDECO_BORDER_LINE

i!:{CUSTOMIZE H-BAR:
Current is (}| x*!:vHBAR | x!:{) to produce:
} | x*!:vDECO_BORDER_LINE 
x!:{
OPTIONS: 
.LEAVE BLANK (preserves current h-bar)
.ENTER VALUE (adopts your provided h-bar)
} 
| v:vPRESENT_PROMPT

y:vPRESENT_PROMPT | i: | v:vOPT
f:^$:lPRESERVE_HBAR
y:vOPT 
#| d!:^. #ensure we only keep 1 char for h-bar?
| v:vHBAR_new

# first integrate new customizations
r*!:vBORDER_LINE:vHBAR:vHBAR_new | v:vBORDER_LINE
# override old h-bar too
y:vHBAR_new | v:vHBAR

g*:{}:vCORNER:vBORDER_LINE:vCORNER | v:vDECO_BORDER_LINE

l:lPRESERVE_HBAR
# then go to next customization...

l:lPROMPT_CUSTOMIZATION_VBAR

# first, construct current vertical border...
v:vTEMP_TEXT:{This is sample text...}
g*:{}:vVBAR:vTEMP_TEXT | v:vDECO_SAMPLE_LINE

i!:{CUSTOMIZE V-BAR:
Current is (}| x*!:vVBAR | x!:{) to produce:

} | x*!:vDECO_SAMPLE_LINE
x!:{

OPTIONS: 
.LEAVE BLANK (preserves current v-bar)
.ENTER VALUE (adopts your provided v-bar)
} 
| v:vPRESENT_PROMPT

y:vPRESENT_PROMPT | i: | v:vOPT
f:^$:lPRESERVE_VBAR
y:vOPT 
#| d!:^. #ensure we only keep 1 char for v-bar?
| v:vVBAR_new

# first integrate new customizations
# override old v-bar too
y:vVBAR_new | v:vVBAR

l:lPRESERVE_VBAR
# then go final presentations...

+---[FINAL CUSTOMIZATIONS:]

# show what we have and decide next step...
v:vPRESENT_PROMPT:{DECIDE NEXT STEP:
1. Modify MAX Line Length
2. CHANGE the TEXT
3. Go Re-DRAW Text-Box (default)
}

y:vPRESENT_PROMPT | i: | t!.: | v:vOPT
f!:^[12]$:lNO_FURTHER_CUSTOMIZATIONS

y:vOPT | f!:^1$:lMODIFY_TEXT
# customize max line length...
l:lCUSTOMIZE_LINE_LENGTH

i!:{CUSTOMIZE MAX LINE LEN:
Current is (}| x*!:vNMAXLINE_LEN | x!:{) to produce:
} | x*!:vBORDER_LINE
x!:{
OPTIONS: 
.LEAVE BLANK (preserves current)
.ENTER VALID NUMBER (adopts your provided)
} 
| v:vPRESENT_PROMPT

y:vPRESENT_PROMPT | i: | v:vOPT
f:^$:lNO_FURTHER_CUSTOMIZATIONS
y:vOPT | t!.: | v:vOPT
f!:^[1-9][0-9]*$:lCUSTOMIZE_LINE_LENGTH
y:vOPT | v:vNMAXLINE_LEN 

# return to processing text..
j:lPROCESS_TEXT

# modifying text...
l:lMODIFY_TEXT
y:vOPT | f!:^2$:lNO_FURTHER_CUSTOMIZATIONS

v:vIGNORE_EXTERNAL_TEXT:1 # so we force prompt
v:vIN:{} # reset current text record
# return to text prompt...
j:lPROMPT

l:lNO_FURTHER_CUSTOMIZATIONS
# go see what it looks like
j:lPRESENT_BORDERED_TEXT
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
  \caption{TEA APPLICATION: TEXT BEAUTIFIER --- a configurable and special text-box drawer implemented using TEA}
  \label{FIGYAPP1}
}


\vspace{2em}


The program does work just like we specified, and shall produced varied outputs depending on user configuration or the defaults. For example, the default maximum line length is set to 26 (so that we can always ensure we can produce a text-box containing a complete line of all the 26 letters of the alphabet. Otherwise, check out what some of the interesting outputs of this program are:


 \vspace{2em}
 \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 1 from TEXT BEAUTIFIER v1]
---------------------------
abcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyz
0123456789
---------------------------
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 \vspace{2em}



 \vspace{2em}
 \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 2 from TEXT BEAUTIFIER v1]
@+@---------------------------@+@
| abcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyz
| 0123456789
@+@---------------------------@+@
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 \vspace{2em}
 
 
  \vspace{2em}
 \begin{tcbverbatim}[title=Sample OUTPUT 3 from TEXT BEAUTIFIER v1]
@@~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~@@
# This is the day that the Lord 
# has made!
# We will rejoice! We 
# will rejoice and be glad inde
# ed!
@@~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~@@
  \end{tcbverbatim}
 \vspace{2em}
 
 
 And thus is the program quite useful! 
 
 \vspace{2em}

To access this service, use any of the options:

\vspace{2em}

\begin{enumerate}
\item Find TEXT BEAUTIFIER on the list of standard TEA programs via:\\ \url{https://tea.nuchwezi.com}
\item Directly load and run TEXT BEAUTIFIER via: {\normalsize \url{https://tea.nuchwezi.com/?i=\%20&fc=https://gist.githubusercontent.com/mcnemesis/6a53c63dc1159e14ad287a44a92341e3/raw/&run} }
\item{Obtain the TEXT BEAUTIFIER source-code (and other related codes/programs) via the TEA GitHub Project:\\
\url{https://github.com/mcnemesis/cli_tttt}}
\item{ Find the TEXT BEAUTIFIER code on GitHub Gists:\\
\url{https://gist.github.com/mcnemesis/6a53c63dc1159e14ad287a44a92341e3}}
\item{ Or use the official shortened link to share or load TEXT BEAUTIFIER:\\
 \url{https://bit.ly/draw_textbox}
 }
\end{enumerate}

\vspace{2em}
