%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\chapter{Z: ZAP}
\label{SECZ}


\begin{table}[H]
  \centering
  \LARGE
	\begin{tabular}[t]{|p{0.2\textwidth}|p{0.5\textwidth}}
 
	\textbf{NAME} & \texttt{ZAP}\\
	\hline
	\textbf{Purpose} & \begin{enumerate}
	\item Especially Do things in TEA using External Powers
	\item The \textbf{Evaluate} command for non-TEA code
	\item Case Transforms for Text
	\item Introspect the TEA Environment
	\end{enumerate}\\
	\hline
	              
\end{tabular}
\caption{General Objectives of TEA Primitive \textbf{Z:}}
  \label{TABTAZZ}
\end{table}


\section{SEMANTICS of Z:}
\label{SECSEMZ}


\begin{table}[H]
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3} % Optional: increases row height
\rowcolors{1}{lightgray}{white}   % Alternating row colors
\begin{tabular}{>{\bfseries}m{0.4\linewidth} | m{0.6\linewidth}} % 2 columns, 
\rowcolor{white}
\textbf{\makecell[l]{INSTRUCTION\\ SIGNATURE}} & \textbf{INSTRUCTION FUNCTION} \\
\hline

\makecell[l]{z:}
 & Return AI transformed to ALL \textbf{lowercase}\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{z:CMD} & \makecell[l]{
Invoke the system command CMD passing AI as only input, set the\\
result as IO iff the command successfully executed, otherwise\\
return EMPTY STRING.\\\\
On the WEB/WEB TEA, CMD is any valid code or string that can\\ 
be evaluated in JavaScript using eval, and the AI is passed as\\
  \texttt{var AI=`CURRENT\_AI'}}\\

z!: & Return AI transformed to ALL \textbf{UPPERCASE}\\


\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{z!:CMD} & Same as \texttt{z:CMD}, but will return an Error Message from the command execution if it didn't execute or if there were errors.\\


\makecell[l]{z*:} & Return AI transformed to \textbf{Title Case}\\

\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{z*:vCMD\\z*!:vCMD} & Same as \texttt{z:CMD} and \texttt{z!:CMD}, but using the command string stored in the vault with name vCMD\\


\makecell[l]{z.:\\z.:NAME} & \makecell[l]{
Return information about the TEA environment as values delimited\\
by ``;" in the strict order:\\
 PLATFORM:VALUE;VERSION:VALUE;DATE:VALUE;TIME:VALUE;\\
If the parameter NAME is specified, returns the value corresponding\\
 to the given environment parameter in case it matches, otherwise \\
 just returns the entire set of environment parameters.\\\\
For example, when TEA is running on the web, and \texttt{Z.:PLATFORM}\\
 is invoked, just returns ``WEB" as the only value, \\
 otherwise returns ``CLI".}\\


\rowcolor{lightgray}\bfseries \makecell[l]{Z*.:vNAME} & Like \texttt{Z.:NAME}, but uses value in vault vNAME to determine the value for NAME.\\




 \hline
\end{tabular}
\caption{The Semantics of Z:}
\label{TABSEMZ}
\end{table}


\section{NOTES about Z:}
\label{SECNOTEZ} 


The Z-command space is for bringing external (think Unix, System or non-TEA) powers into a TEA program. But also, that means, much of its outputs and evaluation mechanics (when used in the forms \texttt{z:CMD}, \texttt{z!:CMD}, \texttt{z*:vCMD} and \texttt{z*!:vCMD}) are outside the direct control of TEA or the TEA runtime, and thus might sometimes be harder to predict or determine up-front, and yet, among the TEA primitives, it is one of the simplest to use --- for those coming to TEA from other languages or who have good knowledge of the underlying environments --- and is arguably one of the \textit{most magical} TEA commands!

\vspace{2em}


Also, because \textbf{ZAP} allows a TEA program to set the value of the ACTIVE input to whatever that command returns from executing an external command or code, it then becomes a potential way to read external values into the TEA program, and also might allow for writing the ACTIVE input to some external memory store\footnote{Think; since TEA doesn't natively support reading or writing to files, networks, databases, etc. A clever, and thoughtful use of ZAP could make all of these possible, when this power is placed in the hands of a seasoned or well-informed programmer or hacker.}. Other input commands such as \texttt{i:VAL} and \texttt{w:URL} can be likened to \texttt{z:CMD} in this regard, with the exception that any potential data or values that might be introduced into a TEA program using the \texttt{i:VAL} primitive are exactly what are explicitly stated in the TEA program code as the value \texttt{VAL}.

\vspace{2em}


The earlier sections of this specification already contain several example TEA programs using the Z-primitive. But, perhaps, it should be noted, that, just like the network accessing commands in the W-command space might not do what they are expected to do when the program is being run without access to a network --- such as when a program meant to post data to a server runs while the host device has no data connection, also, in some operating environments --- such as when a TEA program is being run in an environment that doesn't expose the underlying system shell --- such as trying to run CLI TEA on some un-rooted mobile devices --- in which case, even though the environment might be running on Linux underneath, but that space might not accessible to TEA programs by default or without special workarounds. While, in other environments such as Windows or desktop/server-side LINUX, such access is guaranteed to be possible most times. Thus, Z-primitives should be used cautiously --- with special care to distinguish between when ZAP is meant to be used in the context of WEB TEA vs CLI TEA\footnote{Something that, interestingly, the \texttt{Z.:PLATFORM} command was created for. For example, see the \textbf{Personality Calculator} program in \textbf{\hyperref[SECEXAMP1Q]{Section \ref{SECEXAMP1Q}}} that helps compute a person's astrological profile, but which leverages this \texttt{Z.:PLATFORM} command to cleverly craft useful tests that would make the computations simpler depending on the platform/environment under which the TEA program is being run. In this case, the environment introspection is being utilized to help run optimal TEA code, and not some external/non-TEA code!}, and perhaps only minimally or not at all\footnote{Earlier on, while TEA was still in its infancy, some times one found that they would need to fall-back to an external General-purpose programming language for some tricky computations such as non-trivial mathematical logic --- see for example the case of the \textbf{N-SIGIL} TEA application (\textbf{\hyperref[SECEXAMP1C]{Section \ref{SECEXAMP1C}}}) that significantly leverages JavaScript in computing special qr-code like graphics corresponding to numbers.} if possible, so as to ensure that one's TEA programs shall do what they are expected to do portably or across all environments. Z-command space (particularly the external command calls) should mostly be kept for advanced, non-trivial cases that are expected to only be required in special environments one is sure about.

\vspace{2em}


Z: basically makes it possible to perform some basic system programming in TEA. For a simple example demonstrating the power of the Zap facility, here is a program that would generate random numbers from words, and which would only work as desired in CLI TEA (basically, in Linux Environments):


\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=Z: EXAMPLE 1: Random Numbers from Words, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
# essentially, generate random numbers
# from words/alphabetical expressions...

i:abcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxy z
a!:
d:[ ].*$
r!:[aeiou]:0
r!:[bcdfghjklmnpqrstvwxyz]:1
x:{ibase=2; }
x:{echo '}
x!:{' | bc}
v:vCMD
c:
z*:vCMD
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{2em}

Where we assume the system or shell command `bc' exists. Also, this example shows how we can use TEA to construct custom or special system commands, and then use them to solve problems based on user-provided data. For example this program constructs a call such as

\vspace{2em}

{
\LARGE
  \begin{tcolorbox}[myterminalstyle, title=Dynamically Constructed BC language Command]
  \begin{lstlisting}
echo 'ibase=2; 1100011011111101111' | bc             
  \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
}

\vspace{2em}

Which, when passed to the Z-command returns ``407535" on a system where the ``bc" command is accessible. The interestingly logic or trick here, when contrasting this method of generating random numbers to how TEA programs normally would generate random numbers (such as via use of the \textbf{NUMBER} command \texttt{n:} --- see \textbf{\hyperref[SECN]{Chapter \ref{SECN}}}), is that we take an expression, shuffle it, then map it to binary expression, and then use bc to convert from binary to decimal!

\vspace{2em}

A slightly modified version of this program is shown below, which would take any user input and return it as a number --- \textbf{not as a random number this time, but as the numeric equivalent of a given word or alphabetical expression}. It can for example show that the name ``Jesus Christ" is equal to the number 1403!


\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=Z: EXAMPLE 2: Numeric Expression Equivalent of Alphabetical Expressions, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
# program converts provided word to a consistent number
# -------------------------
# NOTE: also accepts command-line parameters (just like any TEA script)
# via -i "value" or -fi "input file path"
# -------------------------
i:test # interesting.. try "Jesus Christ" and see!
g!: # first eliminate all whitespace
z: # then turn everything to lowercase
r!:[aeiou]:0 | r!:[bcdfghjklmnpqrstvwxyz]:1
x:{ibase=2; } | x:{echo '} | x!:{' | bc}
v:vCMD | c: | z*:vCMD
# Magic!
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{2em}


An otherwise simple, and likely potentially widely supported system command is the ``pwd" command for returning the current working directory. We can see Z: working with an example invoking this command via TEA:


\vspace{2em}

{
  \begin{tcolorbox}[myterminalstyle, title=Z: EXAMPLE 3: Calling System Commands from TEA on the CLI]
  \begin{lstlisting}
EXPERIMENTS|< 18:26:34 $>* pwd
/mnt/e/LAB/EXPERIMENTS
EXPERIMENTS|< 18:26:36 $>* tttt -c "z:pwd"
/mnt/e/LAB/EXPERIMENTS
EXPERIMENTS|< 18:26:50 $>*             
  \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
}

\vspace{2em}


Another useful demonstration is with combining use of TEA vaults with the Z-utility, such as in this example:

\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=Z: EXAMPLE 4: Obtain Custom Formatted Date/Time-stamp in TEA on the CLI, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:{+'%A %d, %b %Y'}|v:vCMD:{date}|z*:vCMD
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{2em}

Which should return something like  ``Friday 23, Aug 2024".


\vspace{2em}


For those testing Z: via TEA on the WEB (where we can't readily run the same stuff we can run via Z on the command-line TEA, since there is no bash, no Linux/Unix commands), examples for using Z: to run JavaScript from TEA are as follows:



\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=Z: EXAMPLE 5: Running JavaScript from TEA on the WEB, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:2|z:AI+3 
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{2em}


That shall return ``23" since, as we can see in the TEA debug output for this call, the system sets the internal variable AI to a string value ``2" based on the AI at the time Z: is called, and so, whatever we write in the CMD against AI refers to the string value, unless we marshal it manually. See the debug output below for illustration:


\vspace{2em}

{
  \begin{tcolorbox}[myterminalstyle, title=Debugging WEB TEA (EXAMPLE 5)]
  \begin{lstlisting}
Processing Instruction: z:AI+3
PRIOR MEMORY STATE: (=2, VAULTS:{})
***[SYSTEM CMD]: EVALUATING via JavaScript:

---[START CMD]---

 const AI = "2"; AI+3 

---[END CMD]---
***[SYSTEM CMD RESULT]: 23
RESULTANT MEMORY STATE: (=23, VAULTS:{})             
  \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
}

\vspace{2em}


So, to instead treat it as an integer and thus return say ``5", we should re-write that program as:


\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=Z: EXAMPLE 6: Running JavaScript from TEA on the WEB, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:2|z:Number(AI)+3 #=5
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{2em}

The debug output shall enlighten us:



\vspace{2em}

{
  \begin{tcolorbox}[myterminalstyle, title=Debugging WEB TEA (EXAMPLE 5)]
  \begin{lstlisting}
Processing Instruction: z:Number(AI)+3
PRIOR MEMORY STATE: (=2, VAULTS:{})
***[SYSTEM CMD]: EVALUATING via JavaScript:

---[START CMD]---

 const AI = "2"; Number(AI)+3 

---[END CMD]---
***[SYSTEM CMD RESULT]: 5
RESULTANT MEMORY STATE: (=5, VAULTS:{})          
  \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
}

\vspace{2em}


Running invalid JavaScript or if any exception occurs during evaluation of that CMD, then with cases like \texttt{Z!:CMD}, we would get the result containing the error:



\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=Z: EXAMPLE 7: ZAP Calls Exposing External Evaluation Errors, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:2|z:Numbers(AI)+3 # (=, VAULTS:{})
i!:2|z!:Numbers(AI)+3  # (=[ERROR]: ReferenceError: Numbers is not defined, VAULTS:{})
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{2em}


And for the case of commands held in vaults, we see the following as a good example:


\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=Z: EXAMPLE 8: ZAP Calls Referencing Commands Held in Vaults, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:2|v:vCMD:Number(AI)+3|z*:vCMD #  (=5, VAULTS:{"vCMD":"Number(AI)+3"})
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{2em}


{\LARGE \textbf{WARNING:}} BE CAREFUL with the POWER of ZAP --- not just on WEB, but anywhere! Take for example the following simple program that, when run on the TEA WEB IDE, shall modify the WEB IDE itself! Yes, it shall insert the text ``Hello \textbf{World:5}" somewhere towards the bottom of the page in RED, and after that, unless you reload the page, most things will just stop working!


\vspace{2em}

  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=Z: EXAMPLE 9: HACKING External Environment via TEA, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
i!:2|v:vCMD:{var R=Number(AI)+3;document.body.innerHTML += "<div style=\'color:red;\'>Hello <b>World:" + R +"</b></div>";R}|z*:vCMD
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}

\vspace{2em}

The effect of the above hack can be seen as shown in the following screenshot...

\vspace{2em}

\begin{figure}[H]
  \begin{center}
  %\includegraphics[trim=2cm 8cm 2cm 8cm, clip, width=0.9\textwidth,]{resources/pdfs/ProteinSynthesisStateMachine.pdf}\\
   \includegraphics[width=0.9\textwidth,]{resources/images/screenshot_hack_tea_web_ide_via_ZAP.png}\\
   \caption{TEA WEB IDE hacked via a ZAP TEA program!}
  \label{FIGTEAZEXA}
  \end{center}
\end{figure}


\subsection{Environment and Platform Introspection using ZAP}

Before closing the discussion notes about the ZAP command space, it shall be worth noting that we can use the \texttt{Z.:*} commands to find out about where and when a TEA program is being run. These commands are available and behave the same anywhere TEA runs, and have the following syntax and purposes:

\vspace{2em}

\begin{enumerate}
\item \texttt{Z.:PLATFORM} --- returns the [short] name of the particular [TEA] platform from which the current program is being run. The only two possible values for this are either ``WEB" or ``CLI", corresponding to either TEA being run in a Web Browser or via the command-line/desktop/etc. respectively.
\item \texttt{Z.:VERSION} --- returns the current numerical version name of the TEA language being used to run the program. This value should likewise match what is seen as reported on the TEA WEB IDE when a TEA program is run via the online TEA IDE environment, or via the \texttt{tttt -v} result when invoked via the command-line. Examples are like ``1.4.4"
\item \texttt{Z.:DATE} --- returns the current date formatted as ``Tuesday, 07 April 2026".
\item \texttt{Z.:TIME} --- returns the current time formatted as ``16:21:50" (HH:MM:ss)
\item \texttt{Z.:TIMESTAMP} --- returns the current time-stamp as seconds since epoch formatted as ``1775577265978"
\item{ \texttt{Z.:} --- \textbf{return all platform/environment parameters}: without having to explicitly indicate which of the parameters to return, this command signature sets AI to all the current values for each of the parameter names indicated above, and in these, separated using a single semi-colon as such 

\begin{figure}[H]
  \centering
  \begin{tcolorbox}[myterminalstyle, title=TEA Environment/Platform Parameters]
  \begin{lstlisting}
PLATFORM:WEB;VERSION:1.4.5;DATE:Tuesday, 07 April 2026;TIME:18:54:25;TIMESTAMP:1775577265978
  \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
  \caption{The TEA Platform Parameters as returned by the \texttt{Z.:} instruction}
  \label{FIGZPARAMS}
\end{figure}

}
\end{enumerate}



 
\section{EXAMPLE APPLICATIONS of Z:}
\label{SECEXAMPZ}

  
Having come to the final command space in the TEA instruction set, we must acknowledge that by this time, almost all of the intricacies of TEA programming have already been touched upon in the earlier chapters and examples leading up to this. However, and because ZAP is the TEA command especially related to or oriented towards enabling TEA programs to work with external powers and or facilities, in demonstrating the powers and standard applications of this command space, we shall embark on something truly ambitious; an attempt at \textbf{creating an Operating System in TEA!} This task, also somewhat related to reminiscent of fundamentals core-applications or projects in disciplines such Computer Architecture, Systems Programming and or Basic Software Engineering, shall likewise bring on the horizon the idea of \textbf{creating a new programming language using TEA!} Sounds crazy? Sounds exciting or impossible? Well, that's the stuff we are actually going to embark on in the \sout{rest of this chapter...} next [bonus] chapter.



%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
% END-TAZ
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%



\chapter{TEA Operating System (TOS)}
\label{SECTOS}



\begin{figure}[H]
  \begin{center}
  %\includegraphics[trim=2cm 8cm 2cm 8cm, clip, width=0.9\textwidth,]{resources/pdfs/ProteinSynthesisStateMachine.pdf}\\
   \includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth,]{resources/images/tea_cup_computer3.jpg}\\
   \caption{Welcome to the TOS: \textbf{TEA Operating System}!}
  \label{FIGTEACOMPUTER3}
  \end{center}
\end{figure}


Before we dive into this arcane task, it shall be worth bringing to mind one excerpt from my favorite reference book concerning hardcore classic Operating System basics and Computing Architecture concepts --- ``Advanced Computer Architecture: A Systems Design Approach" by \textbf{Richard Y. Kain}:


\noindent
\begin{minipage}{1\textwidth}
\vspace{2em}
\begin{quotation}
{\ttfamily

Computer systems are complex entities; their hardware and software are designed to support a few basic illusions that make them understandable to human beings. An important illusion is that a virtual machine provides functional behavior, conforming to all of the illusions it supports. But every virtual and real machine must have limits and boundaries. The methods by which these boundaries are enforced are very important.

}
\hspace*{\fill} ---\textbf{Richard Y. Kain}, \textit{Advanced Computer Architecture: A Systems Design Approach}, 1999\cite{kain1999advanced}
\end{quotation}
\vspace{2em}
\end{minipage}


and he surely wrote the kind of book that any serious, advanced engineer in computing would love to read and re-read especially when thinking about systems at a core, fundamental level. Moreover, and as we have decided to embark on the [daunting] task of creating an operating system, perhaps, it is worth mentioning another piece of wisdom that Kain shared in his book, but which he clearly attributes to classical philosopher \textbf{Rene Descartes}:


\noindent
\begin{minipage}{1\textwidth}
\vspace{2em}
\begin{quotation}
{\ttfamily

It is not enough to have a good mind. The main thing is to use it well.

}
\end{quotation}
\vspace{2em}
\end{minipage}


\fbox{\begin{minipage}{\textwidth}
\LARGE

\textbf{In-BRIEF: Richard Yerkes Kain, a ``Grandfather" in Computer Architecture:}\\


Richard Yerkes Kain is a computer scientist and author best known for his textbook \textit{Advanced Computer Architecture: A Systems Design Approach} (Prentice Hall, 1996)\cite{kain1999advanced}. He also wrote earlier works such as \textit{Automata Theory: Machines and Languages} (1972)\cite{kain1972automata}. Beyond his publications, little biographical information is publicly available, but records indicate he was affiliated with the University of Minnesota in Minneapolis, MN. He was born 
in 1936, and his later life includes references to creative works such as photography (\textit{Ethiopia}) and travel books (\textit{Norway})\cite{copilot_assistant}.

\textbf{Known Works}
\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Advanced Computer Architecture: A Systems Design Approach} (1995/1996) \\
  A comprehensive textbook covering instruction set design, memory organization, processor architecture, 
  and the relationship between hardware and software. Widely used in graduate and advanced undergraduate 
  courses in computer engineering.
  
  \item \textbf{Automata Theory: Machines and Languages} (1972) \\
  Focused on formal languages, automata, and computational theory. One of the earlier texts bridging 
  theoretical computer science and practical applications.
\end{itemize}

Active in academia during the 1970s--1990s, and contributing to both theoretical computer science and applied computer architecture, no current biography or institutional profile is available other than an IEEE author entry\footnote{See \url{https://ieeexplore.ieee.org/author/37270447800}}. His present status (alive or deceased) is likewise not publicly confirmed \cite{copilot_assistant}.

\end{minipage}}
\\



\section{\textbf{COMPUTER ARCHITECTURE:} TEA Operating System Design}
\label{SECTOSSPEC}



We are going to design our own minimal operating system known as the \textbf{TOS} --- \texttt{TEA Operating System}. However, before we dive into that task, let us begin by clarifying to ourselves and everyone, about what exactly we mean by an \textbf{operating system}:

\vspace{2em}


\begin{center}
\fbox{\begin{minipage}{0.9\textwidth}
\large
\textbf{NOTE:}\\
  \textbf{Definition\footnote{Although I had with me several authoritative texts (in physical form) in my private book collection, and yet, only one seems to extend a formal definition for this concept.} of an OS}

\vspace{2em}

\begin{quotation}
\noindent \textbf{Operating System} \texttt{The set of software products that jointly controls the system resources and the processes using these resources on a computer system.}\\
\hspace*{\fill} --- \textbf{Oxford Dictionary of Computing}\cite{illingworth1996computing}
\end{quotation}

\end{minipage}}
\\
\end{center}

\vspace{2em}


The definition shared above, and which comes from Oxford\cite{illingworth1996computing}, might come off as very generic or somewhat confounding when we consider the scope of our planned TOS --- for example, we don't intend to implement a mechanism that sits between a computer's hardware (physical computing or \textit{computer engineering}) and  the software applications that utilize that hardware (software development and \textit{application programming}) in the strictest sense\footnote{Usually, an operating system should, and does underlie user-level programs or applications, and that, software operating at the OS level typically caters for things like drivers that render the hardware usable, bootstrapping and managing memory and other virtual resources such as virtual-printers, managing access to input and [media] output interfaces, computer networking primitives such as the IP protocol stack, etc. that other software/applications might need to use, catering for system security by regulating and enforcing bounds on various kinds of user and system software, etc. So that, the OS typically sits in the background and orchestrates things while the user mostly goes about their tasks using applications.}.


\vspace{2em}

Especially because much of our research and learning community is based on the \textbf{Telegram} Internet/Social-Media platform\footnote{See, for example our \textbf{Blackboard Computing Adventures (BCA)} community --- \url{https://t.me/bclectures}}, one occasionally bumps into various resources (especially e-books, but also podcasts, source-code snippets and micro-blogs) relating to both theoretical and practical computing among others, while browsing through the numerous channels, forums and ICs (\textit{Internet Communities}) that have taken root on that very robust and indispensable internet service. And so, among resources I came across, is one (potentially yet to be finished) booklet by two Professors (also a \textbf{married couple}) at the University of Wisconsin-Madison; Prof. \textbf{Remzi H. Arpaci-Dusseau} and \textbf{Andrea C. Arpaci-Dusseau}, titled ``Operating Systems: Three Easy Pieces". That book, although it delves significantly into low-level intricacies of realizing an operating system, and that much of its illustrations hinged on C-programming, does offer some good highlights of what typically underlies any mature operating system, and we shall here present an excerpt from the book for completeness's sake:

\vspace{2em}

\begin{quotation}
\noindent \texttt{...the three key ideas we're going to learn about: \textbf{virtualization}, \textbf{concurrency}, and \textbf{persistence}. In learning about these ideas, we'll learn about how an operating system works, including how it decides what program to run next on a CPU, how it handles memory overload in a virtual memory system, how virtual machine monitors work, how to manage information on disks, and even a little about how to build a distributed system that works when parts have failed. That sort of stuff.}\\
\hspace*{\fill} ---\textbf{Remzi H. Arpaci-Dusseau and Andrea C. Arpaci-Dusseau}, \textit{Operating Systems: Three Easy Pieces}, 2018\cite{arpacidusseau2018ostep}
\end{quotation}


\vspace{2em}

However, we won't only restrict our background theory on those two authorities, but also bring to mind ideas that the author of this book, and also inventor of the TEA language, once surfaced in their paper on another, earlier programming language project that preceded work on TEA; \textbf{DNAP} --- the \textit{Dynamic Nuchwezi Architecture Platform} project \cite{lutalo2023dnapsoe} that at one time\footnote{Not that the ideas and work presented at the culmination of that project became obsolete or that they can't still find practical use, but that, it was mostly undertaken as a private project initially, only to later be partially and successfully commercialized by the author based at Nuchwezi Research's home-lab, long before they embarked on its formalization during the inventor's graduate studies, and that, despite the results having been well exploited both practically and theoretically across the computing ecosystem in Uganda back then, that the project somewhat went idle and got shelved as focus moved on to more ambitious, more robust language research undertakings such as later VOSA\cite{lutalo2024vosa} and then TEA. Some great lessons and ideas learned from the DNAP project though, still are timeless and continue to be exploited even in this TEA language work.} powered much of the progress on cutting-edge research in cross-platform application programming for mobile and web\cite{lutalo2023dnapsoe, lutalodnapintro2020, a2021_github} at Nuchwezi Research.

\vspace{2em}

The essential excerpt concerns the concept of a \textbf{Software Operating Environment (SOE)}, which, especially in relation to both operating systems and programming language development, concerns the matter of how to go about effectively, methodically evaluating a programming language (in unison with its operating environment). The verbatim excerpt is as such...

\vspace{2em}

\begin{quotation}
\noindent \texttt{A SOE can be thought of as an ecosystem of related software technologies that can allow one to design, build, and run software in a given programming language on a given operating system. More formally;
\begin{quote}
\textbf{Definition 11: Software Operating Environment (SOE):} \textit{A SOE, \texttt{T}, over a programming language, \texttt{P}, on a host operating system family, \texttt{O}, also written \texttt{T(P):O}, is a system satisfying all the following properties:
\begin{enumerate}
\item{\texttt{T} has a software construction environment for \texttt{P} in \texttt{O}.} 
\item{\texttt{T} accepts \texttt{P} source code and outputs a \texttt{P} executable file in \texttt{O}.}
\item{\texttt{T} has a software execution environment for \texttt{P} executables in \texttt{O}.}
\item{\texttt{O} has at least one operating system instance where all properties (1), (2) and (3) hold.}
\end{enumerate}
}
\end{quote}}
\hspace*{\fill} ---\textbf{Joseph Willrich Lutalo et al.}, \textit{DNAP: Dynamic Nuchwezi Architecture Platform-A New Software Extension and Construction Technology}, 2023\cite{lutalo2023dnapsoe}
\end{quotation}


\vspace{2em}

With that very useful idea out of the way, and even though we might not delve into its proper application such as in quantitative evaluation of TEA vs other languages using the 8 SOE metrics first introduced in \cite{lutalo2023dnapsoe}, we shall instead leverage some of the intricacies surrounding this idea of an ``operating environment" that SOE is about, and which we might leverage in the design and construction of our minimal TEA operating system. Namely, we wish to ensure that, in our TOS, we at minimum have a working SOE for TEA programs, and this means that we must ensure to have:

\vspace{2em}

\begin{enumerate}
\item A means to create TEA programs inside this OS.
\item A means to execute/run these programs within the OS's environment.
\item A means to save to file and load from file --- or rather, a means to create executable ``self-contained" and preferably ``named" payloads --- of TEA programs that one can then run or share or read from within the OS.
\item [\textbf{Bonus}] A means for our TEA programs to interact with or operate alongside other kinds of executables in the OS environment [and/or the external world/environment(s)].
\end{enumerate}

\vspace{2em}

And so, with that theory behind us, let us embark on designing a minimal operating system that we can bootstrap and run anywhere TEA programs can run\footnote{Of course, that we are to construct our operating system using TEA and so as to especially run TEA programs should not be seen as a short-cut or anomaly because, any operating system requires some programming language in order to bring it to life --- usually that might be a low-level language such as Assembly or C\cite{kain1999advanced}\cite{arpacidusseau2018ostep}, however, and as one might come to appreciate when they realize that modern operating systems can also work inside of virtual-machines and that we can have mini-operating systems running on-top of other/host operating systems, then nothing should stop us from attempting to create or at least prototype ours using just TEA.}.


\subsection{The TOS Specification}
\label{SECTOSSPEC}

Without sanction of some working-group or committee, we have thought-up and drafted the following minimal specification as the essential basis of the \textbf{TEA Operating System}:

\vspace{2em}

%draw horizontal line
\noindent\rule{\textwidth}{0.4pt}

{
\LARGE

\textbf{TOS Specification:}

The TEA Operating System is specified based on 4 core capabilities:

\begin{enumerate}
\item File Input/Output Service
\item \verb$[$TEA\verb$]$ Programming Service
\item Basic Utility Service
\item Help/Documentation Service
\end{enumerate}

}

%draw horizontal line
\noindent\rule{\textwidth}{0.4pt}

\vspace{2em}


Moreover, and perhaps very worthwhile to note, we learn from one of the reference books on Linux --- a globally popular, and dominant free operating system --- that besides the core (or rather ``kernel") of an operating system, it is quite important to be clear on what services and utilities render the operating system usable. See what Ellen Siever and Stephen Spainhour et al. have to say about this:


\noindent
\begin{minipage}{1\textwidth}
\vspace{2em}
\begin{quotation}
{\ttfamily 

The Linux kernel itself was originally designed by Linus Torvalds at the University of Helsinki in Finland and later developed through collaboration with countless volunteers worldwide. By "kernel," we mean the core of the operating system itself---not the applications (such as the compiler, shells, and so forth) that run on it. Today, the term "Linux" is often used to mean the kernel as well as the applications and complete system environment.

}
\hspace*{\fill} ---\textbf{Ellen Siever and Stephen Spainhour et al.}, \textit{Linux in a Nutshell: A Desktop Quick Reference}, 2000\cite{Siever2000LinuxNutshell}
\end{quotation}
\vspace{2em}
\end{minipage}



And with that clarification out of the way, we then embark on specifying the details of our operating system --- and somewhat, to again reflect the manner and approach of \cite{Siever2000LinuxNutshell} in clarifying what makes Linux tick as well as how it is used, we shall especially approach the specification as though we were laying down a specification of the interaction interface that users of TOS rely upon to use the operating system for arbitrary tasks. The four categories or capabilities laid down in the mini-specification above shall guide us...


\vspace{2em}


\begin{enumerate}
\item {  \textbf{File System:} create, name, rename, copy, lookup, preview, and delete file objects.\\
\textbf{---[Basic FILE I/O Commands Interface]:}
\begin{itemize}
\item \texttt{create FILENAME} $\leftarrow$ creates blank file with name FILENAME.
\item \texttt{init FILENAME} $\leftarrow$ create a file using the given name and the currently available input/last-output or blank if none.
\item \texttt{rename FILENAME NEWNAME} $\leftarrow$ change the name of an existing file from FILENAME to NEWNAME.
\item \texttt{copy FILE1 FILE2} $\leftarrow$ copies contents of FILE1 to FILE2 creating the destination if it doesn't exist.
\item \texttt{find PATTERN} $\leftarrow$ find and list all file records whose names match the given Regular Expression pattern.
\item \texttt{show FILENAME} $\leftarrow$ preview the contents of given file.
\item \texttt{export FILENAME} $\leftarrow$ exit the system, outputing the contents of given file.
\item \texttt{delete FILENAME} $\leftarrow$ remove file with name FILENAME from storage.
\end{itemize}

}

\item {  \textbf{Programming Interface:} a command-line interface to find, run, or modify user programs or find and run system programs and commands.\\
\textbf{---[Basic Programming Commands Interface]:}
\begin{itemize}
\item \texttt{runnable FILENAME} $\leftarrow$ recognize FILENAME object as an executable program or command.
\item \texttt{programs} $\leftarrow$ show list of all available TEA programs by their names.
\item \texttt{run FILENAME} $\leftarrow$ execute the given file record as a TEA program.
\item \texttt{runnable FILE1 FILE2} $\leftarrow$ replace/override original program named FILE1 with contents of FILE2 (updates a program's code preserving its existing name).
\item \texttt{commands} $\leftarrow$ executes COMMAND as a system command if it exists, otherwise shows error message command-not-found.
\item \texttt{whatis NAME} $\leftarrow$ displays help info about a given system command or user program.
\item \texttt{delete-program FILENAME} $\leftarrow$ revokes status of FILENAME as an executable user program (doesn't actually delete the underlying file object though).
\end{itemize}

}



\item {  \textbf{Basic Utilities:} access to basic useful commands such as being able to interact with the world outside TOS as well as do non-programming work from within TOS.\\
\textbf{---[Basic UTILITY Commands Interface]:}
\begin{itemize}
\item \texttt{fetch URL} $\leftarrow$ fetch and output contents of the specified [online/network] resource.
\item \texttt{fetch URL FILENAME} $\leftarrow$ fetch and save contents at URL into file record named FILENAME.
\item \texttt{tea CODE..} $\leftarrow$ take everything following the keyword ``tea" as a TEA program, run and output the result: It's a simple way to write and run basic TEA programs (TEA-one-liners) in TOS.
\item \texttt{math EXPRESSION} $\leftarrow$ execute basic mathematics on the command-line as specified in EXPRESSION.
\item \texttt{date} $\leftarrow$ return the current date.
\item \texttt{time} $\leftarrow$ return the current time.
\item \texttt{timestamp} $\leftarrow$ return the current timestamp.
\item \texttt{print} $\leftarrow$ show or preview current output buffer contents (usually, output of last command).
\item \texttt{export} $\leftarrow$ take the output of the last command or rather, current contents of the output buffer and export that outside the operating system (also terminates the operating system session).
\item \texttt{exit} $\leftarrow$ ends the TOS session returning nothing.
\end{itemize}

}



\item {  \textbf{Help System:} a means to locate and read basic documentation about programs, system commands and the operating system.\\
\textbf{---[Basic HELP Commands Interface]:}
\begin{itemize}
\item \texttt{whatis NAME} $\leftarrow$ displays help info about a given system command or user program.
\item \texttt{help NAME} $\leftarrow$ same as \texttt{whatis NAME}.
\item \texttt{help} $\leftarrow$ present menu for user to access help about all available [essential] commands based on category.
\item \texttt{help-file} $\leftarrow$ display all available commands in the FILE I/O category.
\item \texttt{help-prog} $\leftarrow$ display all available commands in the PROGRAMMING category.
\item \texttt{help-util} $\leftarrow$ display all available commands in the UTILITIES category.
\item \texttt{help-help} $\leftarrow$ display help on how to use the HELP facility.
\end{itemize}

}


\end{enumerate}



And thus, the plan is now officially underway... to manifest an new operating system based on TEA!

\begin{figure}[H]
  \begin{center}
  %\includegraphics[trim=2cm 8cm 2cm 8cm, clip, width=0.9\textwidth,]{resources/pdfs/ProteinSynthesisStateMachine.pdf}\\
   \includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth,]{resources/images/proposing_teaos.jpg}\\
   \caption{\textbf{A Hacker's Dream Come True?} The proposal to bring to life a \textbf{TEA Operating System}!}
  \label{FIGTEAOSPROPOSAL}
  \end{center}
\end{figure}




The link depicted in \textbf{\hyperref[FIGTEAOSPROPOSAL]{Figure \ref{FIGTEAOSPROPOSAL}}} being the first to have been officially shared with members of the TEA community concerning this proposal, we shall have it point, not to the running instance of the TOS, but rather, to the potential staging source-file for the reference implementation of this operating system. And thus, those zealously interested in following what the latest working version of the single-file TEA OS code looks like --- a program that one should be able to readily load into either the TEA WEB IDE or verify on any system with \texttt{tttt} package installed and run, shall be found at the link: \url{https://bit.ly/run_teaos}




\subsection{The TOS Architecture}


Unlike what happened when the Linux kernel was developed --- particularly, that ``Linux has neither a documented conceptual nor a documented concrete architecture"\cite{Bowman:1999:Linux} --- that was said of Linux (started in 1991) in 2008! We shall especially tread carefully, and thoughtfully, so that, from the onset, the TEA operating system does have [at least initially] a conceptual architecture\footnote{As for the concrete architecture, especially because it essentially nails down the intricacies of how each actual implementation aspect in the system relates to the others, might not be accurate to formalize at the onset, and might instead be a piece of the TOS documentation that evolves later, perhaps after the initial reference implementation is complete --- in a way, to reflect the sensibilities of many implementers of complex software --- that often prefer to not over-engineer by starting out with minimal architectural blueprints (or none), and as for how the case was with Linux, that perhaps the [final] architecture is [only] reverse-engineered/extracted from the implementation\cite{Bowman:1999:Linux} after the fact!}.

\vspace{2em}

However, given that we don't wish to start out from a position of total green --- with no reference to, nor relation to what has happened in other operating system projects --- we shall keep in mind some of the architectural concepts and ideas that authorities such as the quantum physicist \textbf{Wolfgang Mauerer} has demonstrated in \cite{Mauerer2008Linux}, in relation to their detailed, authoritative documentation of the Linux kernel's architecture, but also the insightful treatment of the matter laid down in \textbf{Ivan T. et al}\cite{Bowman:1999:Linux}.



\noindent
\begin{minipage}{1\textwidth}
\vspace{2em}
\begin{quotation}
{\ttfamily 

The design process involves developing several models of the system at different levels of abstraction. As a design is decomposed, errors and omissions in earlier stages are discovered. These feed back to allow earlier design models to be improved...

A specification of some kind is the output of each design activity. This specification may be an abstract, formal specification that is produced to clarify the requirements or it may be a specification of how part of the system is to be realized. As the design process continues, detail is added to the specification. The final results of the process are precise specifications of the algorithms and data structures to be implemented.

}
\hspace*{\fill} ---\textbf{Ian Sommerville}, \textit{Software Engineering}, 1996\cite{Sommerville1996Software}
\end{quotation}
\vspace{2em}
\end{minipage}




\vspace{2em}

In \textbf{\hyperref[SECTOSSPEC]{Section \ref{SECTOSSPEC}}} we have laid down the basic aspects of the requirements engineering necessary for this project, and this is captured in an initial specification of the main capabilities that TOS ought have. However, as mentioned in the above quote attributed to the classic book on software engineering\cite{Sommerville1996Software} by \textbf{Ian Sommerville}, the process of refining a system's specification could span multiple levels of abstraction, and that earlier specifications might either feed forward into deeper levels, but also backwards into refining or revising earlier ones! However, and because we might not want to over-engineer the TOS, we shall sometimes skip or assume certain [usual] formalities that are part of [recommended] professional software engineering.


\vspace{2em}


Leveraging that, and preferring to start by specifying what the main users of the TOS shall/ought do at a high-level based on the TOS specification\footnote{This preference for a \textbf{user-oriented} design approach inspired both by the lessons we learn from \cite{Sommerville1996Software} concerning the relevance of \textbf{user-centered design}, but also from what a respectable ACM SLE paper from 2017 --- \textbf{Ankica Barišić and Dominique Blouin et al.}\cite{Barisic2017UsabilityDSL} ---  had to say concerning not just how new programming languages, but also any complex system ought be approached so as to enhance its usability and acceptability --- by modeling or designing from early-on, with target users and the environment in mind.}, we have the following Use-Case Diagram\cite{lutalo_2026_19592521_tos_usecase} specifying the main activities that each of the core users of the TOS are expected to do within the system. 

\vspace{2em}



\begin{figure}[H]
  \begin{center}
  %\includegraphics[trim=2cm 8cm 2cm 8cm, clip, width=0.9\textwidth,]{resources/pdfs/ProteinSynthesisStateMachine.pdf}\\
   \includegraphics[width=0.9\textwidth,]{resources/pdfs/tos/TEA Operating System - Use-Case-Diagram.pdf}\\
   \caption{TEA Operating System : Use-Case Design Diagram}
  \label{FIGTOSUSECASE}
  \end{center}
\end{figure}


\vspace{2em}

With a clear understanding of not just what the target users of our system are, but also what activities and roles they have in utilizing the system's components, as depicted in  \textbf{\hyperref[FIGTOSUSECASE]{Figure \ref{FIGTOSUSECASE}}}, we can then present the core system architecture for the TEA Operating System, and this is what the following diagram is for...

\includepdf[pages=1]{resources/pdfs/tos/TEA Operating System - ARCHITECTURE.pdf}


And thus, we are now ready to embark on the implementation of a proof-of-concept (PoC) of the TOS as we have specified it.



\subsection{The TOS Proof-of-Concept}


For historical purposes, it shall be worth noting that the very first attempt at writing the source code for the TEA Operating System (also which we keep a reference copy of at \url{https://bit.ly/run_teaos}), came in the form of a basic TEA program whose main purpose was just to present the banner or login screen for the system. Typically, this is like the very first interface to any operating system that users might see or encounter as they boot or load it. Simple as that sounds, it can take on arbitrary forms and could also become as complex as one wishes, however, for TOS, the initial implementation of such a basic Operating System interface came in the following form...


\vspace{2em}

{
 \Large
  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=TEA Program: TOS Boot-Screen Interface, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
#!/usr/bin/tttt -fc
I!:{oooooooo_________________
___oo_____ooooo___ooooo__
___oo____oo____o_oo___oo_
___oo____ooooooo_oo___oo_
___oo____oo______oo___oo_
___oo_____ooooo___oooo_o_
_________________________}                                     
x!:{
Welcome to TEA OS v1.0.0}
v:vBanner | i:
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
  \caption{TEA PROGRAM: The Initial TEA Operating System Boot-Screen Interface}
  \label{FIGTOS1}
}


\vspace{2em}

When we run that minimal TEA operating system program, we only get the following output (an ASCII-art banner that reads ``TEA" in a large beatiful custom font), accompanied by an input prompt with the message ``Welcome to TEA OS v1.0.0", and whose input value becomes the output that the TOS program returns, and then the system quits. So, that's basically it for starters!





\begin{figure}[H]
  \begin{center}
   \includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth,]{resources/images/teaos/teaos_bootscreen_on_teamobile.png}\\
   \caption{\textbf{TEA Operating System}: Bootscreen on TEA Mobile}
  \label{FIGTOS2}
  \end{center}
\end{figure}








%%%%%%%%%%%---------[ CONCLUSION ]--------%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\chapter{Conclusion}
\label{SECCONC}

%---refs fixed up to this point...


We have covered sufficient material to help anyone coming to \textbf{TEA}, to be able to understand where the language came from, what it is based on, how it is designed or specified, what the language grammar is, how the language is processed, what sample programs in the language look like, and finally, what the core aspects of the language's Instruction Set are, A: to Z:, as well as sufficient test cases and sample codes for almost each specified aspect of those 26 core TEA primitives. This book is additionally meant to serve as an operating manual\footnote{Almost like what \textbf{a grimoire} would be, in case computer programming was likened to practicing magick!} and working reference for those using TEA programming in practice, across domains, as well as those reading and trying to understand, debug or extend [existing] TEA programs. We have also included material on where, how and which TEA standard to install where, how to run or update it, as well as where to find the developers for feedback, how to join the TEA user, developer and research community and also how one might learn from and follow the inventor of the language himself.



%---[ END BOOK CONTENT/CHAPTERS ]

%---[ BEGIN BOOK END/APPENDICES]
\begin{appendices}

%\chapter{Other Functions of DNA Beyond Protein Synthesis}
%\label{APPOTHERFUNC}


\chapter{PROGRAMMING FUNDAMENTALS in TEA}
\label{APPEFUNDAMENTALS}


In this bonus chapter, we are going to cover some material that one might typically expect to find in a standard textbook or reference on basic programming for either students of Computer Science or anyone interested in learning programming from scratch. Typically, such material is meant to help instill in the student the idea or mental model of \textbf{how computers ``think"}. However, instead of wasting space and time repeating what is already well-catered for in many other standard books, we shall instead focus on highlighting several important foundation concepts with regards to general computer programming, that anyone interested in TEA programming needs be aware of with regards to how they can be accomplished using this somewhat new and unusual but powerful language.

\vspace{2em}

\section{Introduction and SCOPE}

We shall cover the following material or topics in order..

\begin{enumerate}

\item{\textbf{Basic Syntax and Program Structure}
\begin{itemize}
\item How to write and run a simple program such as ``Hello World".
\item Understanding statements, expressions, and program flow.
\item How TEA syntax differs from traditional/common languages such as C, Python and Java.
\end{itemize}
}

\item{\textbf{Data Types and Variables}
\begin{itemize}
\item Primitive Types: numbers, characters, strings, booleans, regular expressions.
\item Declaring and Storing values in variables.
\item How TEA types differ from types in traditional/common languages such as C, Python and Java.
\item Are there Dynamic or Static Types in TEA?
\end{itemize}
}


\item{\textbf{Operators and Expressions}
\begin{itemize}
\item Arithmetic (+, -, *, /).
\item Comparison (==, \verb!<, >!).
\item Logical (\verb:&&, ||, !:)
\item How TEA handles operator precedence and unusual operators.
\item Are TEA command qualifiers a kind of Operator or Operator Modifier?
\end{itemize}
}


\item{\textbf{Control Flow}
\begin{itemize}
\item Conditional Statements (if, else, switch).
\item Loops (for, while, do-while).
\item How Pattern Matching Works in TEA
\item Breakpoints, Conditional and Unconditional Program Termination
\end{itemize}
}


\item{\textbf{Functions and Modularization}
\begin{itemize}
\item Defining Reusable Blocks of Code: Encapsulation and Closures in TEA - code in labeled blocks, code in strings.
\item Parameters and Return Values
\item Scope of Variables
\item How TEA Functions Work - leveraging Context-Aware and Context-Unaware Code Evaluation
\end{itemize}
}


\item{\textbf{Input and Output}
\begin{itemize}
\item Reading User Input
\item Printing or Displaying Results
\item File Handling Basics
\item Highlights of How basic Input/Output varies in TEA vs C, Python and Java
\end{itemize}
}


\item{\textbf{Data Structures}
\begin{itemize}
\item Arrays, Strings, Lists
\item How to store and manipulate collections
\item How to Parse and Produce JSON in TEA
\end{itemize}
}


\item{\textbf{Error Handling}
\begin{itemize}
\item Common runtime errors (division by zero, invalid input, undefined commands, invalid syntax)
\item Debugging basics in TEA
\item Exception Handling in TEA - equivalents of try-catch constructs in TEA
\end{itemize}
}


\item{\textbf{Memory and Variable Lifetime}
\begin{itemize}
\item Stack vs Heap (in C) and correspondences in TEA
\item Garbage Collection vs Manual Memory Management in TEA
\item TEA Database/Persistent Memory
\item Manual Memory Management in TEA - especially via the CLEAR, VAULT and YANK command spaces
\end{itemize}
}

\item{\textbf{Problem-Solving Mindset}
\begin{itemize}
\item Breaking Large Problems into Smaller Steps (algorithmic thinking)
\item Writing Pseudocode before coding.
\item How to translate logic into TEA code.
\item Various ways of approach Abstraction using TEA
\end{itemize}
}

\end{enumerate}


\vspace{2em}

Of course, that is quite an ambitious undertaking, and its a work that usually might span several chapters of even fill an entire text-book on its own. However, as this book (the TEA TAZ) is a \textbf{living document}, and as its original core purpose was mostly to introduce and explain the TEA language and not necessarily all of general programming or computing skills, we shall mostly only treat of those topics at sparingly, and might especially fill-in the gaps as time and the material evolves. Thus, where necessary, sometimes refer to the earlier chapters in the book because lots of material and discussion relevant to these foundational topics is already well catered for in the many illustrations, notes and example TEA programs we have used to demonstrate how to approach solving many kinds of problems via the language.

\vspace{2em}

That said, for starters, we shall simply have a shell of the topics to be covered, and shall steadily go on filling-in the gaps as already indicated...

\vspace{2em}
  \begin{tcolorbox}[teaterminalstyle, title=Basic TEA Functions, breakable]
  \begin{lstlisting}[language=TEA,breaklines=true,numbers=left]
##--------------------------
The following Illustration
Helps Those Coming to TEA
From other languages to
Understand that there's
A Way to Create Reusable
Components in TEA much as
Everything in TEA is just
of Type Text!
DETAILS: Appendix A of TEA TAZ
##-------------------------

# Define a function that computes the transformation:
# (a,b) =f(a,b)=> (a+b)*2

# the actual function is...
V:f:{y:a|x:(|x!:+|x*!:b|x!:)*2|r.:}

#---[How to use it..]

# 1. initialize/change the parameters it processes..
V:a:1|V:b:3

# 2. Then invoke it using TEA code injection
E*!:f

# evaluates (1+3)*2
# should return 8
# uncomment breakpoint to test/confirm this
#q!:

# re-use that function with
# a=5, b= value from user
v:a:5
i!: Enter a value for b
i:|v:b

#call function... 
E*!:f
   \end{lstlisting}
  \end{tcolorbox}
